SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        Swazi agriculture displays an unusually sharp duality between an
intensive, modern, largely foreign owned and managed farming sector, and the
traditional subsistence sector. Most subsistence farmers are concentrated on
about 57% of Swaziland's land area known as Swazi Nation land; here, tradi-
tional authority aid land tenure systems prevail. Despite recent economic
growth, largely fueled by agriculture development, Swazi Nation land farmers
have not shared in the nation's prosperity, and for many years Government
agricultural programs neglected them. Since before independence in 1968, the
Swazi Government has engaged in rural development schemes aimed to improve
agriculture and upgrade social services on Swazi Nation land. Current rural
development areas are supported by the U.K. Overseas Development Ministry
(ODM), and cover about 7% of Swazi Nation land. Use of inputs and production
of crops have increased significantly in these areas, and the Government's
approach is supported both by farmers and traditional authorities.
ii.       The proposed Project would form part of the on-going rural develop-
ment program, which would be expanded from four Rural Development Areas
(RDA's) to 14 RDA's covering about 60% of traditionally farmed Swazi Nation
land, over five years. Development would be undertaken in three phases:
a land use and investment planning phase, a preliminary development phase
entailing the development of centers, services, and provision of inputs, and
an intensive development phase involving soil conservation and land develop-
ment, land consolidation and resettlement, livestock development, and pro-
vision of full agricultural and social services. IBRD has been asked to fi-
nance development in ten of these rural development areas; ODM has agreed
to continue and expand its program in four areas. The Project would specif-
ically include development of extension services and infrastructure, improve-
ment of traditional livestock in RDA's and encouragement of destocking of
communal pastures, and soil conservation through land development. Further,
agricultural inputs would be provided to farmers, and agricultural credit
services strengthened to expand credit usage. Roads and rural social
services would be developed in RDA's. Finally, central management services
for national rural development programs would be strengthened, and technical
assistance, training, studies, and monitoring and evaluation would be provided.
iii.      The Ministry of Agriculture would have principal responsibility
for Project implementation and for ensuring effective coordination with
other ministries and agencies concerned, notably the Swaziland Development
and Savings Bank (SDSB), the Central Cooperative Union(CCU), the Central
Rural Development Board (CRDB) and the Ministries of Health and Finance.
Most services of the Ministry of Agriculture would be directly involved
in Project implementation, and their activities would be coordinated by
an RDA management unit. An interministerial coordinating committee would
be established including representatives of all ministries and agencies
concerned.



- ii -
iv.       Total ProJect costs are estimated at E 14.9 million (US$17.1 mil-
lion), of which US$10.4 million or 61% would represent foreign expenditures.
The proposed IBRD loan of US$4.0 million would be to the Government of Swazi-
land; it would carry interest at 8.5% a year, and would be repayable over
20 years including 4.5 years of grace. The loan would finance about 26% of
total Project costs (excluding taxes). The African Development Bank (Nigerian
Trust Fund) has agreed to provide a loan of US$5.4 million equivalent to
finance part of the Project; the loan would carry interest at 4% repayable
over 25 years, including five years grace, and would finance most Project civil
works and operating costs for livestock developments and agricultural credit
services. The European Development Fund would provide a grant equivalent to
US$2.9 million, which would finance all costs of extension services (except
buildings) in the eight less intensive RDA's, incremental agricultural inputs,
Project training costs, and technical assistance. ODM has agreed to provide
technical assistance for the Project valued at about US$300,000. Financing
of Project components would be on a parallel basis; the co-financing part-
ners have agreed that the Project will be implemented as a fully integrated
operation, and that they would cooperate closely during Project implementa-
tion. Local sources in Swaziland would finance 18% of net Project costs. Of
this amount, the Government's contribution would amount to about 16% of total
Project costs (net of taxes) and farmers would contribute about 2% of Project
costs. Contracts for the supply of vehicles and equipment (US$1.4 million)
and fencing materials (US$0.4 million) exceeding US$50,000 would be awarded
after international competitive bidding in accordance with Bank guidelines.
Contracts fcr goods and services costing less than US$50,000 would be awarded
following Government procurement procedures, which are satisfactory. Land
development work, including terracing (US$1.9 million) would be carried out
by the Land Development section of the Ministry of Agriculture by force
account. In view of their small size, remoteness and dispersed location, all
of these civil works would be unlikely to attract international bids.
v.        Increased output in Project RDA's would be derived primarily from
increased production of a range of crops and increased productivity of cattle.
Maize production would increase by about 17,000 tons, cotton by 3,000 tons,
and vegetables by about 7,000 tons. Increased crop production would result
principally from raising yields on existing farms through more intensive
cultivation, improved farming methods, and use of more inputs; increases in
cultivated areas would also augment production. Increasing emphasis would
be placed on cash crops (cotton, tobacco, rice, vegetables), but active exten-
sion of hybrid maize should enable families to satisfy consumption require-
ments with less land, thus freeing larger parts of holdings for cash crop
production. Land consolidation and terracing of arable areas should con-
tribute to raising yields by making farming more efficient. Livestock de-
velopment programs should increase the productivity of traditional cattle
herds in the two intensive RDA's; productivity increases would result prin-
cipally from efforts to destock pastures, but also from better herd manage-
ment and pasture development (rotational grazing, upgrading through use of
improved bulls, bush clearing, artificial pastures). Marketed offtake



- iii -
should increase, and weight and grade should improve with better management.
Incremental sales of cattle are estimated at 3,000 head a year in year 6 and
5,000 head a year by year 20. Some additional milk production should result
from Project extension efforts.
vi.       The economic rate of return from the Project is estimated at
between 20 and 25% over 20 years. The rate of return is most sensitive to
delays in field level implementation and to shifts in price relationships
between production inputs and crops. The proposed Project does not entail
substantial risks, largely because it is based on successful on-going pro-
grams. The degree of success of the Project will, however, depend on the
effectiveness of extension efforts and receptivity of farmers, and on the
rate at which physical developments can be implemented. A further risk is
that services and infrastructure will not be maintained following the invest-
ment period. The Project includes measures to minimize these risks through
provisions for central management, training, technical assistance, and em-
phasis on cooperation with Swazi Nation authorities. Further, annual dis-
cussion of work programs should permit adaptation of the Project as experi-
ence warrants. The Project offers good prospects for improving services and
raising incomes in the poorest areas of Swaziland very substantially, and
developments should be readily replicable in areas of Swaziland not covered
under current development schemes.
vii.      An estimated 125,000 people would benefit from the Project, both
from increased cash income and subsistence production, and from improved
social services. The population affected would represent the lowest income
group of Swaziland, and current family cash incomes are exceedingly low
(US$115-280); while they compare with current GNP per family of at least
US$3,000 (most of it cash incomes), these figures are not very meaningful
given the sharp duality of the economy and dominant role of large expatriate
firms and farmers. Project investments in agriculture should increase labor
input in Project RDA's by the equivalent of 2,500 man-years a year. Most
civil works would be labor intensive and would employ an estimated 500 addi-
tional persons during the construction period.
viii.     This would be the first Bank Group supported project for agriculture
and rural development in Swaziland. An irrigation project for the Mapobeni
area was turned down by the Bank/IDA following appraisal in 1973-74, because
of high costs and a low rate of return, the high level of technical expertise
required, and sociological difficulties.
ix.       The proposed Project constitutes a suitable basis for an IBRD loan
of US$4.0 million at 8.5% interest repayable over 20 years including 4.5
years grace.






SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
APPRAISAL REPORT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      Agriculture dominates the economy of Swaziland, and over 90% of the
population lives in rural areas. Swazi agriculture displays an unusually
sharp duality between an intensive, modern, largely foreign owned and managed
farming sector and the traditional subsistence sector. Most subsistence far-
mers are concentrated on about 57% of Swaziland's land area known as Swazi
Nation land; here, traditional authority and land tenure systems prevail.
Despite recent economic growth, largely fueled by agricultural development,
Swazi Nation land farmers have not shared in the nation's prosperity. Since
before independence in 1968, the Swazi Government has engaged in rural devel-
opment schemes supported by the UK, aimed to improve agriculture and upgrade
social services. Development programs have been concentrated to date in
rural development areas (RDA's), 1/ where the Government has begun detailed
land use planning, intensified agricultural and social services, undertaken
soil conservation works, and provided agricultural inputs and credit. Cur-
rent rural development areas cover about 7% of Swazi Nation land; use of
inputs and production of crops have increased significantly in these areas,
and the Government's approach is supported both by farmers and traditional
authorities.
1.02      The proposed Project would form part of the on-going rural devel-
opment program (para. 3.03 and Annex 2), which would be expanded from four
RDA's (where the UK Overseas Development Ministry (ODM) has financed devel-
opment) to a total of 14 RDA's covering about 60% of traditionally farmed
Swazi Nation land, over five years. Development would be undertaken in
three phases: a land use and investment planning phase, a preliminary de-
velopment phase entailing the development of centers, services, and provi-
sion of inputs, and an intensive development phase involving soil conserva-
tion and land development, land consolidation and resettlement, livestock
development, and provision of full agricultural and social services. IBRD
has been asked to finance development in ten new RDA's; ODM has agreed to
continue its program in four areas which will be expanded.
1.03      This would be the first Bank Group supported project for agricul-
ture and rural development in Swaziland. An irrigation project was turned
down by the Bank/IDA following appraisal in 1973-74, because of high costs
and a low rate of return, the high level of technical expertise required,
and sociological difficulties.
1.04      The proposed Project was prepared by the Ministry of Agriculture,
with assistance from the IBRD Resident Mission in Eastern Africa (RMEA).
Preparation was based on the on-going Rural Development Program financed
1/   RDA's are areas of Swazi Nation Land (para. 2.06) designated for
intensive development efforts.



- 2 -
by ODM, with related activities financed by UNDP, USAID, and ILO/SIDA.
Principal modifications agreed upon during appraisal related to phasing
of investments, modification of land development proposals, and Project man-
agement. This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission that
visited Swaziland in June, 1976 composed of K. Marshall and S. Capoluongo
(IBRD) and K. Landskroner, and A. Vilakazi (consultants).
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      ihe Kingdom of Swaziland, the second smallest country in Africa
(17,368 km ), is surrounded by the Republic of South Africa and Mozambique;
a landlocked country, it nevertheless has good access to seaports, via Maputo
and Durban, and to major market centers in South Africa. Swaziland has a
long-standing relationship with South Africa, and is a member of the Rand
Monetary Area and the South African Customs Union. The total population of
Swaziland, estimated at about 525,000 in 1976, is growing at an annual rate
of about 3.2%. Of the 90% of the population living in rural areas, about
70% live in scattered homesteads on Swazi Nation Land, still closely bound
by Swazi traditional ways of life. The remaining rural population lives on
freehold title land, most of it owned and farmed by expatriate Sompanies or
farmers. Average population density is about 28 persons per km .
2.02      Swaziland has prospered since achieving independence in 1968.
Average per capita GNP was estimated at US$470 in 1975, and GDP growth
averaged about 7% per annum since the mid-1960's. The economy is relatively
diversified: agriculture and manufacturing account for about 35% and 15%
of GDP, respectively, while mining contributes just under 10%. The potential
for further economic growth and expansion is good given underexploited
resources, notably in agriculture, and the proximity to markets in South
Africa.
2.03      Recent economic development in Swaziland has accentuated the
severe dualism which is a dominant feature of the economy: the modern
sector accounts for over 80% of GDP and wage employment, and is expanding
rapidly; the traditional sector, in contrast, has remained virtually stag-
nant. While subsistence agriculture contributed about 14% of GDP in 1974,
50% of the population were entirely dependent on subsistence farming and
cattle rearing. Data on income distribution is poor, but it is clear that
it is highly skewed between the modern sector, including many non-Swazis,
and the traditional subsistence sector, which has benefitted very little
from recent economic growth. Rural-urban income gaps, however, are not
very significant, largely because of the existence of a prosperous modern
agricultural sector; nor are disparities in income among regions very marked.
The sharpest disparities lie between the traditional Swazi farmers, virtually
all on Swazi Nation land, and both expatriates and Swazis employed in the
modern sector, whether in towns or rural areas.



- 3 -
2.04      Swaziland's diversified economy is in part the product of geography,
for there are four markedly distinct topographic regions, each extendiug
from north to south (see map and Annex 1): (i) the Highveld (5,200 km )
in the west, a mountainous region of pins forests and steep valleys and high
rainfall; (ii) the Middleveld (4,700 km ), an area of fertile soils and
rolling gEasslands with rainfall of about 1,200 mm. a year; (iii) the Lowveld
(6,200 km ), further East, a low-lying lower rainfall area (700 mm average)
of savanna-type grasslands, supporting ir5igated estates and ranches; and
(iv) the Lubombo plateau region (1,300 km ) in the extreme East, with higher
altitudes, higher rainfall and fertile soils.
2.05      Swaziland's governmental and political structure is based on an
unusual dichotomy which arises from (but also partly explains) the dualism
of the economy. A modern Government (with a Cabinet of Ministers at the
summit) governs jointly with a traditional Government (with the Swazi
National Council (SNC) the principal institution); traditional Government
is quite separate and enjoys substantial power, particularly on all matters
affecting Swazi Nation land, where chiefs maintain customary law and hold
power over land allocation. The King, who has reigned for over 50 years,
is de facto executive head of both Governments; on matters affecting Swazi
Nation Land, the King must give his approval for all programs and actions.
Given the often unwritten basis for traditional institutions, and the divi-
sion of authority between the traditional and modern Governments, decision-
making affecting Swazi Nation Land can be slow and frustrating. This could
apply to the proposed Project, since both modern and traditional Government
institutions would be involved in Project implementation. However, both have
in the past supported rural development programs similar to that now proposed,
and have explicitly approved the current Project proposal.
2.06      The distinction between land held under customary tenure (Swazi
Nation land) and under modern, European-style land law (freehold title land)
is important for the Project, which would affect only Swazi Nation land areas;
implementation must conform to traditional authority and practices which gov-
ern these areas (Annex 3). The distinction results from the pre-Independence
era, when much land was granted by Swazi rulers to expatriates, ultimately
as freehold title with full rights to use and dispose of land; as much as
40% of Swaziland's land is still owned and managed by expatriates. The re-
maining land is designated Swazi Nation land (about 984,000 hectares, or 57%
of Swaziland). The King holds it in trust for the Nation, and governs it
through local chiefs. Land tenure patterns, social structure, and local
government remain very largely and quite deliberately traditional, with
the King, SNC, and local chiefs vigilant to ensure that all development
activities are undertaken through appropriate channels, and do not run
counter to traditional authority or values.
B. The Agricultural Sector (Annex 1)
2.07      Swaziland has a diverse agricultural sector, differentiated by
geographic region (para 2.04) and by the dichotomy between modern and
traditional farming. About 65% of the total land area of 1.7 million ha



-4-
is presently used for grazing and ab--:: 10% is presently under cultivation
(including fallow), while a further 10% is considered suitable for intensive
cultivation.  Irrigated agriculture :s important, given the number of peren-
nial rivers flowing through Swaziland, and an estimated 25,000 ha are now
under irrigation, mostly concentrated in the Lowveld. Most irrigation is
in large estates but there is also a potential for small irrigation schemes
(under 50 ha) throughout the country. Forests (mostly man-made) cover about
6% of Swaziland's land area, and support a thriving forest products industry.
Swaziland is an exporter of commercial crops (sugar, cotton, forest products,
livestock), but must import to feed the population. In 1974, agricultural
products (raw and manufactured) constituted over 80% of total exports, which
were valued at E120 million (US$138 million). Agricultural commodities,
notably maize, comprised about 8% of the value of total imports in 1974.
Commercial Farming
2.08      About 600 estates and farms hpld under freehold title produce most
of Swaziland's export crops; freehold title holdings average about 940-ha
each, of which an average of 70 ha is under crops; the remainder is graz-
ing land, forest, mining concessions, and waste land. There are some very
large company owned estates; other farms belong to expatriates, many absen-
tees, and to a growing number of Swazi citizens. Production techniques on
most estates and farms are modern and intensive, including use of full in-
puts and mechanization. Sugar is the principal crop, and accounted for almost
42% of total merchandise exports in 1974. Other important commercial crops
are cotton, citrus, and pineapples, while forest products are increasing in
significance. Some freehold title farms also produce livestock. The modern
agriculture sector has grown at a rate of about 10% a year for the past
decade.
Swazi Nation Land Farming
2.09      An estimated 39,000 families farm holdings averaging less than 3
ha. on Swazi Nation Land. Production is primarily for subsistence and less
than 10% of total production is offered for sale on the commercial market.
Maize, the staple food of Swaziland, is the principal crop, occupying about
80% of total crop area. Other important crops are groundnuts, sorghum,
beans, sweet potatoes, and pumpkin. In recent years, Swazi Nation land
farmers have rapidly increased production of cash crops, notably cottor.,
tobacco, and vegetables, but this represents still a very small portion
of Swazi Nation land production, which has increased at only 3% a year over
the past decade, barely keeping pace with the growth of population. Farming
techniques are almost exclusively traditional, by hand, or using oxen. Inter-
planting as a hedge against crop failure is common, and farmers generally
grow a range of crops in addition to maize. Women are the principal farmers
by tradition, with men predominantly concerned with livestock. An estimated
27,000 families own cattle. Cultivable land is allocated to families by
chiefs, and they enjoy some degree of security of tenure, including the right
to pass land on to their children. However, land may not be sold, and the
chief can always reallocate land, although this seldom occurs in practice.
Fragmentation of farming plots is common and is becoming a serious problem in
some areas with increasing pressure of new families seeking arable land.



-5-
Livestock
2.10      Livestock provides an importait source of revenue for Swaziland,
through meat and cattle exports valued at E4.7 million (US$5.4 million)
irn 1974. Totel cattle numbers were estimated at 620,000 in 1975; of these,
about 520,000 were owned by about 27,000 Swazi farmers, and grazed on Swazi
Nation land pastures (which are communally owned and managed). The remaining
100,000, generally exotic or upgraded animals, are run on freehold title
ranches, usually in the Lowveld. Cattle play a vital role in Swazi society;
cattle ownership by tradition confers prestige, cattle are a measure of
wealth and serve a banking function for subsistence farmers, and cattle sales
are a principal source of cash income for Swazi Nation Land farmers. Work
oxen commonly till fields in Swaziland, and are used for transport; cattle
provide meat and milk for the family. Goats also are an important source of
food and cash, and an estimated 250,000 goats are owned by 13,000 families.
Cattle husbandry techniques remain traditional; herding is minimal, animals
are kraaled at night and cows are milked daily, and production coeffi-
cients are low. The local Nguni breed are well adapted to local conditions,
but productivity can be very substantially increased by upgrading with exotic
breeds. Total marketed offtake is less than 10%. Ranching on freehold title
land has demonstrated the potential for profitable livestock production when
more modern techniques are employed.
2.11      Overstocking is a critical problem on Swazi Nation land.  Excel-
lent veterinary services (including universal and compulsory dipping at
over 600 dip tanks) have sharply reduced mortality, without corresponding
increases in productivity or pasture carrying capacity; the national herd
grew from 431,345 in 1955 to 510,085 in 1965, to 620,000 in 1975, an average
rate of increase of close to 2%. In many parts of Swaziland, pastures have
degenerated, soil erosion on grazing areas is plainly visible, and animals
are suffering increasingly from malnutrition. Destocking is an urgent
priority for rural development both to avert further deterioration of pas-
tures, and as a prerequisite for introducing improved husbandry techniques,
including herd management, upgrading of the indigenous Nguni breed, and
pasture improvement. The Ministry of Agriculture operates fattening and
breeding ranches, designed largely to encourage farmers to remove cattle from
communal pastures. To date, results of efforts to destock have not been
encouraging, but under a broad national livestock program, the Government has
committed substantial resources to finding solutions to the problem.
C. Crop and Livestock Marketing and Processing (Annex 4)
2.12      A very small portion of crops grown on Swazi Nation Land are pro-
duced for cash. Maize, the principal crop, is the staple food; between 75%
and 90% of production is consumed on the farm and only surpluses are offered
for sale. The same applies for other subsistence crops (beans, groundnuts,
etc.). An increasing number of farmers are, however, growing crops to obtain
cash: about 5% of Swazi Nation land holdings produce principally for sale,
and 25% sell at least some of their produce. Cotton, tobacco, vegetables and
rice are the principal commercial crops. In the past, marketing has been



- 6 -
dominated by processing companies, most South now seeking to improve market-
ing facilities for Swazi farmers through use of the cooperative movement
(para 2.25), and USAID is assisting in this effort.
2.13      Commercial Crops:  Swazi farmers sell cotton to one ginnery in
Swaziland and three in the Republic of South Africa; these in turn sell lint
to South African spinners. Prices are established in relation to those in
South Africa. Tobacco is marketed through a Tobacco Cooperative dominated by
larger farmers, which sells directly to manufacturers in South Africa;
virtually all tobacco produced by Swazi farmers is low grade pipe or chewing
tobacco. Rice is sold in South Africa, either polished or unpolished.
Vegetables are marketed through less organized channels, principally within
Swaziland.
2.14      Maize and Subsistence Crops:  The small proportion of total maize
and other subsistence crop production marketed through commercial channels
(10-25% according to success of the harvest) is sold either to local inter-
mediaries or hammer mills (80%) or to the Swazi Milling Company (20%); SMC,
by agreement with the Government, has exclusive rights to import maize to
Swaziland and to operate a commercial milling facility, and must purchase all
maize offered by Swazi farmers at Government-fixed floor prices. In 1974,
SMC imported about 20,000 tons of maize from South Africa and purchased about
2,000 tons from Swazi producers; over the past seven years, the imports have
averaged between 5% and 25% of total Swaziland consumption requirements. The
Swaziland Government gives high priority to attaining self-sufficiency in
maize, and is actively promoting efforts to assist Swazi farmers in increas-
ing maize production.
2.15      Livestock:  About 50,000 to 60,000 cattle are marketed annually
by Swaziland producers. Virtually all Swazi Nation land cattle are sold to
local butchers or private, itinerant dealers, principally when farmers need
cash; offtake is low, with many unproductive animals remaining on communal
pastures. A central objective of Government livestock policies is to encour-
age farmers to sell more and younger animals. Swazi farmers often receive
lower than average market prices because of distance from markets and inter-
vention of intermediaries. Very small numbers of goats, sheep, and poultry
are marketed through commercial channels. About 12% of cattle marketed are
exported live. Remaining cattle are slaughtered by local butchers (60-70%)
and by the Swazi Meat Corporation, which runs a modern export abattoir
(capacity 35,000 head a year).
D. Rural Development Programs (Annex 2)
2.16      The proposed Project would form part of the Government's overall
rural development program and would thus be closely related to several
other development projects, most financed by other donor or lending agencies.
The most significant of these are the ODM-financed Rural Development Program
(para 1.02), UNDP agriculture projects, USAID loans for equipment, small
farmer credit, and cooperative marketing, livestock development schemes, a
proposed CIDA dairy development project, and technical assistance and
training financed from several sources. Most projects are at too early a



stage to assess quantitative results, but it is nevertheless apparent that
a broad-based approach to rural development activities is underway, and
that the Government has mobilized considerable external resources, technical
and financial, to assist in this effort. The most significant challenge for
the Government in coming years is to elaborate an effective overall strategy
for all rural development efforts including a more explicit definition
of problems and objectives, and to develop more effective means of coordina-
tion amorg the multitude of on-going and proposed rural development schemes.
E. Government Services and Training
Institutions Responsible for Rural Development (Annex 5)
2.17      The institutional framework for rural development is particularly
complex in Swaziland. Because of the juxtaposition of traditional and modern
authority, a dual decision-making process is involved at both the planning
and execution stage. Since the full range of services and programs aimed
at assisting the rural population is involved, rural development holds the
highest priority for the Swazi government. Decisions on rural develop-
ment matters are often made at a high level, and are frequently raised at the
Cabinet and by the Swazi National Council (SNC); some can be resolved only
by the King. Many Government agencies have responsibilities affecting rural
development, and coordination among their activities is as a significant
problem. Principal responsibility for initiation, implementation and coor-
dination of rural development programs resides for all these reasons with the
Cabinet and the SNC (para 2.05).
2.18      Central Rural Development Board (CRDB):  The CRDB, established
in 1954, oversees all activities on Swazi Nation land, on behalf of the
SNC; it reports directly to the King, and includes important traditional
leaders and chiefs. The CRDB must approve all projects and plans for rural
development schemes; it normally acts on proposals at a conceptual level and
when detailed land-use plans are prepared. An important part of the approval
process is consultation with local chiefs and population involved. It also
exerts surveillance over implementation to ensure that agreed plans are
followed. The CRDB appoints Rural Development Officers (seven at present)
to assist in preparing, presenting, and carrying out development schemes;
they often act as facilitators, working actively to enforce CRDB decisions
on development schemes, and have been effective in advancing rural develop-
ment programs in the past.
2.19      Ministry of Agriculture:  (Chart 1)  The Ministry has two major
departments, Agriculture (principally for crops) and Veterinary Services.
In addition, a Land Planning Section, responsible for preparation of land-
use plans, an Economics Section, responsible for project preparation,
evaluation, and budgeting for investments, and an Administration and Finance
Section report directly to the Permanent Secretary. The Agricultural Depart-
ment has the following sections: (i) agricultural extension and the crop
production unit; (ii) RDA section, responsible for all extension staff in



- 8 -
in RDA's and for coordination of pr --'ams affecting them; (iii) land develop-
ment, responsible for soil conservation ,.orks, feeder road construction, and
development of small dams and irriga_lion schemes; (iv) fisheries; (v) forests;
and (vi) home economics.  The Veter`nary Department has a large, well-staffed,
and well-organized veterinary services division, responsible exclusively for
animal health and abattoir inspection, and a small, recently established
animal production division, which oversees livestock extension work and
activities of breeding and fattening ranches. Agricultural extension staff
are organized by district (four in Swaziland), except in RDA's; these staff
report directly to the RDA unit through RDA "project managers". The crop
extension staff is limited, with about 100 field officers now working
with an estimated 40,000 farm families (1:400); the target ratio has not
been clearly established, but will probably be about 1:200 farm families.
Many staff have insufficient training, and there are not enough qualified
staff to provide adequate supervision at intermediate levels.  Work of crop
specialists has suffered from recent UNDP financial cutbacks, (an FAO/UNDP
project supports the unit); better coordination with general extension would
strengthen the service. Veterinary services are staffed with sufficient
numbers of well-trained staff (about 180 at middle and lower levels), and
function well; livestock extension is at an embryonic stage, with four field
workers. The Ministry plans to merge crop and livestock extension services
as part of a general reorganization recently approved (chart 2).
2.20      Other Institutions:  The Ministry of Local Administration, through
District Commissioners, assists in coordination and implementation of rural
development schemes; local chiefs, the principal agents of local administra-
tion, also have substantial responsibilities at both the planning and imple-
mentation stage. Credit institutions and cooperatives (para 2.25) are play-
ing an increasingly active role through schemes to promote small farmer
credit, supply of agricultural inputs, and marketing of produce.
Manpower and Training (Annex 6)
2.21      Swaziland has severe shortages of agricultural manpower at senior,
intermediate, and field staff levels. Many senior ministry posts are filled
by expatriates; vacant posts and insufficient staff at intermediate levels
curtail the effectiveness of extension supervision, while lack of field
extension workers is perhaps the most fundamental obstacle to improving
services. The problems are exacerbated by competition for qualified staff
from private enterprise and quasi-government institutions, including the
Swaziland Development and Savings Bank (SDSB). Agricultural training at
senior levels is offered by the University of Botswana and Swaziland (UBS);
a substantial number of Swazis are pursuing undergraduate and graduate
training in agriculture at UBS or overseas. Diploma-level training is also
offered by UBS, in a three-year course enrolling forty students a year (in-
cluding Botswana students). There is at present no formal training for
certificate level extension staff. Other small training units train vet-
erinary assistants, domestic science demonstrators, and SDSB staff; a coop-
erative training center for Cooperative Department and primary cooperatives
staff is now being built. Three farmer training centers offer short course
for farmers in special fields, but centers have not been fully utilized and
their effectiveness is being questioned by Government officials.



-9-
Research
2.22      Research on crops and livestock production is carried out by an
Agricultural Research Division at UBS, which maintains a research station,
field stations, and research plots, by the Animal Production Section, which
has concentrated on beef production and pasture development, and by private
industry. Most agricultural research is of relatively recent standing, and
is principally oriented to resolve problems arising from Swaziland condi-
tions; however, Swaziland benefits from much research undertaken in South
Africa. Research has not been directed specifically to small farmer prob-
lems in the past, and at present links with extension are indirect, largely
because they are responsible to different ministries. Work has focused on
trials of new crops and cultural methods, and on specific crop problems.
The quality of research has been good, and clear recommendations for farmers
are published on a regular basis. There is some possibility that agricul-
tural research may become the responsibility of the Ministry of Agriculture
in future, which should aid in strengthening ties with extension.
F. Agricultural Credit and Cooperatives (Annex 7)
2.23      Institutional agricultural credit is well developed in Swaziland,
but it is geared primarily towards the commercial sector. Credit is avail-
able to farmers from four principal sources: (i) the Swaziland Development
and Savings Bank (SDSB); (ii) cooperatives, including both primary societies
and the Central Cooperative Union (CCU); (iii) processing companies; and
(iv) commercial banks. Virtually no commercial bank credit is available to
Swazi Nation Land farmers; credit to them from SDSB and the cooperatives is
still very small in amount, but is increasing in importance; credit from
processing companies is also growing in significance. Availability of
unorganized credit to small farmers is almost nil.   A rough estimate of
small farmers credit usage is about E700,000 (US$805,000) a year. Lack
of availability of agricultural credit, principally because of institutional
blockages, appears to be a significant obstacle to increased uptake of
inputs on Swazi Nation Land.
2.24      SDSB is the principal institution concerned with agricultural
credit. Established in 1965, it is an experienced and well-managed insti-
tution, with a well trained staff. Based in the capital, it has five branch
offices. At present, less than 5% of SDSB's portfolio represents loans to
farmers on Swazi Nation land; however, it is well placed to assist in
promoting development of small farmer credit in future, and has received a
long-term loan of US$750,000 from USAID for this purpose. The principal
drawbacks to SDSB small-scale credit operations are the high cost of making
such loans, cumbersome procedures, and security requirements. SDSB's
authorized capital is E5,000,000 (US$5.8 million), partly subscribed by the
UK and USAID. As of MIarch 31, 1975, outstanding loans stood at E8.8 million
(US$10.1 million). About 37% of total loans were for agriculture, 30% for
the business sector, 23% for housing and 10% for other purposes. Of total
agricultural loans, (E3 million, US$3.4 million), 68% were long or medium
term loans to the commercial sector (large farms), 20% were mechanization
loans (mostly for tractors), 6% were advances paid to farmers against



- 10-
cattle consigned to fattening ranches, and 6% were seasonal loans of less
than E500 (US$575); of this, a small part of tractor loans, most of the
cattle advances, and virtually all seasonal loans under E500 were to Swazi
Nation land farmers. In 1975-76, SDSB made about 1,500 seasonal loans of
less than E500; average size of loan was E125. Interest on small seasonal
loans (below E500) is 6%; on all other agricultural loans it is 10.5%. Data
on loan repayments are poor, but SDSB management estimates that over 34% of
total loans and 24% of total amounts disbursed to Swazi Nation Land farmers
are delinquent. SDSB has recently employed bill collectors to assist in loan
recovery; preliminary results are encouraging. SDSB loan procedures are
cumbersome, and all loans must be secured by about 150% of loan amount;
cattle are accepted as security but Swazi Nation land farmers without cattle
cannot obtain credit from SDSB. Approval of applications by extension staff
and a local advisory committee including government officials is required
for all loans; paperwork is substantial. SDSB is seeking to streamline
loan procedures through establishment of a stand-by line of credit scheme,
simplification of procedural requirements, and increased use of indirect
loans through cooperatives (para. 2.25). SDSB management hopes thus to
reduce the now very high cost of small farmer credit and to increase credit
usage.
2.25      The Swazi Government is promoting the cooperative movement as
part of its rural development program. Organization of primary cooperatives
is being encouraged, and the Government is assisting in organization and
development of the Central Cooperative Union (CCU).  The objective is to
utilize CCU and primary societies to organize supply and sales of agricul-
tural inputs, to assist in marketing produce, and to increase availability
of credit to farmers. CCU has received financial and staff support from
the Government, and should shortly formalize agreement on UK technical
assistance and an E500,000 working capital loan. USAID recently approved a
project to provide assistance to cooperatives in the field of marketing. An
ILO/SIDA project comprises technical assistance and development of facilities
for cooperative training and development. As a result, CCU has expanded
its activities rapidly over the past two years, and now supplies about one
third of inputs on Swazi Nation Land; most activities have been and will
continue to be concentrated in RDA's. To date, however, CCU's role in
providing credit to farmers is very restricted; primary societies provide
seasonal loans to members and have borrowed from SDSB, but amounts involved
are very small. Proposals are now being considered whereby CCU would borrow
funds from SDSB and onlend to member primary societies, which in turn would
organize lending to member farmers (including recovery). Problems include
CCU's limited management capacity and experience and small membership of
cooperatives (4% of Swazi National land farmers). In the short term, close
cooperation between SDSB and CCU will be required. Cooperative development
is at an early stage, and still relies heavily on Government support and
technical assistance; CCU stands in considerable need of improvement in
management and controls. Nevertheless, use of cooperatives for rural de-
velopment activities offers many advantages, and the substantial financial
commitment of several donor agencies to cooperative development reflects
the importance accorded to it by these agencies and the Government.



- 11 -
2.26      Additional sources of credit to farmers are cotton ginneries
and the tobacco cooperative (para 2.13), which provide inputs on seasonal
credit, deducting cost plus about 12-14% per annum interest from sale price.
About one half of all Swazi farmers now buy inputs for cash.
G. Rural Social Services
2.27      The Ministry of Health provides rural health services.  Health care
in rural areas it is provided in 37 clinics now operating. Specialized
program staff (tuberculosis, eye care, etc.) visit clinics on a regular basis.
Principal disease problems are respiratory and gastro-intestinal diseases,
bilharzia, and malnutrition. Family planning advice and materials are pro-
vided through United Nations Fund for Population Activities (UNFPA) supported
Mother and Child Care and Family planning services. Nutrition education is
part of the home economics program of the Ministry of Agriculture, and forms
part of a new UN-supported status of women program. Major constraints to
improving health care are lack of trained staff, need for new facilities, and
the high cost of recurrent expenditures involved. Enrollments in primary and
secondary schools are low in rural areas, and failure and drop-out rates are
high. Schools are not now oriented to providing education for rural youth,
but curriculum development studies are now underway, aimed at making education
more relevant for rural youth. Rural Education Centers are being established
under an IDA-financed project to provide both adult education and specialized
training. Other rural social services are limited. Clean domestic water
supplies are rare, but are being installed in RDA's; they are clearly popular
and beneficial. Community development funds administered by district commis-
sioners assist local chiefs and communities in locally initiated projects such
as spring protection, bridges, and school construction.
III. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
3.01      The Project would form part of the Swaziland Government's contin-
uing program of rural development. It would, over five years, strengthen
agricultural services and provide rural infrastructure in ten rural devel-
opment areas (RDA's) (para 1.01), and would strengthen central management
for rural development programs. The Project would specifically include:
(a)  Strengthening and development of extension
services and infrastructure for crops and
livestock in 10 RDA's;
(b)  Livestock development in 2 of these RDA's through
provision of infrastructure for more sophisticated
cattle herd management, notably fencing, stock water-
ing dams, additional dip tanks, and cattle trucks for
marketing;



- 12 -
(c) Land development works, in 2 of the RDA's, including
consolidation of land holdings, delineation of arable
and grazing areas and related resettlement, and soil
conservation works;
(d) Provision of incremental agricultural inputs, including
fertilizers, seeds, and pesticides, in 10 RDA's;
(e) Strengthening of credit services for small farmers in
10 RDA's;
(f) Development of access and feeder roads in 10 RDA's;
(g) social infrastructure development, including domestic
water supplies and health centers in 2 RDA's;
(h) Central management services, including technical
assistance and operating costs for Project management;
(i) Studies, including a cattle marketing study, future
project preparation, training for extension staff and
farmers, and monitoring and evaluation of Project progress.
3.02      The Ministry of Agriculture would have principal responsibility for
Project implementation and for ensuring effective coordination with other min-
istries and agencies concerned, notably the Swaziland Development and Savings
Bank (SDSB), the Central Cooperative Union (CCU), the Central Rural Develop-
ment Board (CRDB) and the Ministries of Health and Finance. Most services of
the Ministry of Agriculture would be directly involved in Project implementa-
tion, and their activities would be coordinated by an RDA management unit. An
interministerial committee would be established to oversee coordination, in-
cluding representatives of all ministries and agencies concerned.
3.03      The proposed Project would follow directly from an ODM-financed
Rural Development Area Program, initiated in 1970. In most aspects of
basic project design and objectives, the Project would continue and ex-
pand upon the previous program. Further, ODM has agreed to continue and
expand its own Rural Development Program, and the two projects would be
implemented in parallel, with joint management within the Ministry of
Agriculture.
B. The Project Area (Annex 8)
3.04      Ten rural development areas (RDA's) have been defined and desig-
nated for development under the proposed Project. They are located in all
four principal geographic regions of Swaziland, and together cover about
35% of total Swazi Nation Land areas, and 20% of the total area of Swaziland.
About 25% of Swaziland's population live in Project RDA's. The RDA's cover
about 44% of land farmed or grazed by Swazi farmers. The characteristics
of the areas may be summarized as follows:



- 13 -
RDA               Area (ha)  Population         Region
(i)  Lubombo - Mpolonjeni          98,588      27,770    Lowveld and Lubombo
(ii)  Mahamba - Zombodze             19,810     16,108    Middleveld
(iii)  Bekinkosi - Mliba             27,232      14,154    Middleveld
(iv)  Mahanga - Hluti               32,453      17,779    Middleveld
(v)  Masala - Vikisijula           22,585       4,923    Lowveld
(vi)  Nkambeni - Madlangampisi      27,702       5,075    Lowveld
(vii)  Sandleni - Luqolweni          38,350      17,620    Middleveld
(viii)  Siphofaneni - Maphobeni        24,993      5,075    Lowveld
(ix)  Siphocosini - Motshane        19,070       7,655    Highveld
(x)  Sithobela - Madubeni          38,167       7,908    Lowveld
348,950    124,067
Annex 8 provides a description of each area, including location, topography,
climate, soils, and agricultural and livestock position before development.
Location of all RDA's are shown on the map. Considerable basic information
has been assembled for each RDA, which should provide a good basis for land
use planning to be undertaken under the Project. Two RDA's (i and ii above)
have been designated for intensive development; in other RDA's Project de-
velopments would be limited to development of infrastructure and extension
services and land use planning.
3.05      RDA's are areas of Swazi Nation land designated for concentrated
development efforts; definition of RDA's is the responsibility of the Min-
istry of Agriculture, which has selected areas with concentrations of popu-
lation, development potential, and relative geographic homogeneity. RDA
boundaries do not follow administrative, political or geographic boundaries;
they are administrative units specifically designed as a vehicle for develop-
ment efforts rather than as long term jurisdictions. Development programs are
now underway (since 1970) in four other RDA's, under the ODM-financed Rural
Development Program (para. 3.03), and four extensions of these RDA's have been
designated for future development under the ODM program. Total area of the
RDA's financed by ODM is about 139,000 hectares and population 72,000. Thus,
a total of 14 RDA's, covering 487,730 hectares, have been delineated; to-
gether, these areas cover about 60% of communally held Swazi Nation Land, 50%
of all Swazi Nation land, and 28% of all of Swaziland. The proposed Project
would include basic development costs in ten RDA's, while the ongoing ODM-
financed program would support development in the remaining four RDA's.
3.06      All Project RDA's are accessible by all-weather gravel roads;
one RDA is also served by the main tarmac road from Mbabane to the Transvaal
border, and the country's only railroad traverses four RDA's. Communications
between the RDA's and major cities and towns are good, maintained through
public bus services and telephone and telegraph connections. Elevation,
climate, soils and cropping patterns vary with respective locations in Low,
Middle, or Highveld. In general, water resources are adequate to provide
water at least for human and animal consumption, and for small irrigation
schemes in many areas. In all RDA's, maize is the major crop; cotton is
grown on up to 12% of Lowveld individual holdings. Groundnuts and beans are



- 14 -
next in importance. Livestock is important in all RDA's; about 8,800 stock
owners maintain an estimated 141,000 head of cattle and about 91,500 head of
goats and sheep. There is a serious overstocking problem in virtually all
areas.
3.07      All RDA's have primary and secondary schools.  Most primary schools
were established by missions, with or without Government support. School
capacities vary greatly, from 26 to over 500 pupils; facilities and staff
are inadequate in most areas, but requirements should be met through national
programs for school construction and teacher training. Health facilities are
provided through rural clinics (in general about two or more within or near
each RDA), run by Government or missions (usually with Government aid).
3.08      Agricultural extension services are currently inadequate in Project
RDA's. Only two RDA's have an Assistant Extension Officer, and only one RDA
has an Assistant Animal Health Inspector; four RDA's have Domestic Science
Demonstrators and seven of the ten RDA's have between one and three Field
Officers. None of the RDA's have Cooperative Department staff or secretary-
managers or livestock extension officers.
C. Detailed Features
(a) Extension Services and Infrastructure
3.09      Extension services for crops and livestock would be strengthened
in the 10 Project RDA's. The extension service would aim at increasing
production of food and cash crops, notably hybrid maize, cotton, vegetables
and fruits, and tobacco; most would be dryland farming, with the excep-
tion of minor irrigation schemes (dams) where appropriate. Livestock ex-
tension work would focus on promoting destocking by encouraging increased
cattle sales, and on introducing better herd management through rotational
grazing and pasture improvement. Further extension efforts would include
assistance to primary cooperatives, home economics and nutrition, and fish
pond and small stock development. Promotion of small scale dairy development
would also form part of the extension program. The principal investment
items would include: buildings for extension offices, housing for extension
staff, depots for inputs and marketing of produce, vehicles and equipment,
including tractors for Project developments and a farmer tractor hire pool,
incremental wages and salaries for extension staff, and other incremental
operating costs (Annex 11, Tables 1-7).
(b) Livestock Developments
3.10      Livestock developments under the Project would form part of a broad
national program for livestock development, supported by UNDP, ODM, and other
donors (para 2.16); the priority objectives are promotion of destocking of
Swazi Nation land pastures, principally through marketing efforts and devel-
opment of cattle fattening and breeding ranches, upgrading cattle through
a national breeding program, and raising herd productivity through improved
herd and pasture management. Under the Project, livestock developments



- 15 -
would include general extension work in all RDA's (para 3.09), in support
of national programs, and intensive livestock development in two RDA's
(Lubombo-Mpolonjeni and Mahamba-Zombodze); here, the objective would be
to introduce collective and improved management of all herds on suitable
communal pastures. Similar programs have met with some success in existing
RDA's, but the difficulty of changing traditional husbandry patterns is
recognized, and it is clear that the full support of the population will be
necessary to achieve success (Annex 1). Project developments would support
improved management practices through intensive extension work, fencing
(perimeter and paddocks), construction of new dip-tanks using improved
dipping materials, small dams for stock water, and pasture development,
through bush-clearing and establishment of improved pasture (generally as a
green belt between arable and grazing areas). Trucks would be provided to
transport cattle to fattening ranches or to market, in all ten RDA's, and
farmers would be encouraged through extension and other publicity to consign
cattle to fattening and breeding ranches. Improved bulls would be provided
in all RDA's from Government breeding stations to farmers who had agreed to
follow recommended management practices. The principal investment items
would be: trucks, civil works (dams, dip-tanks, bush-clearing, pasture
improvement), fencing, incremental livestock extension staff salaries, and
other incremental operating costs (Annex 11, Tables 8-10).
(c) Land Development and Conservation Works
3.11      Land development works would be based on land use plans (paras.
2.18 and 4.04), and would aim to avert soil erosion caused by improper land
use (cultivating steep slopes without protection), rationalize utilization of
scarce land resources through delineation of arable and grazing areas, con-
solidate fragmented land holdings, and resettle homesteads to appropriate
locations as required, and enhance land productivity through terracing,
irrigation development, and fish ponds. Terracing is principally designed
to control erosion, and involves construction, with earth-moving equipment,
of sloped contour bunds and artificial waterways (Annex 1). On more gentle
slopes, modified terraces or grass strips should provide adequate erosion
control. Existing equipment would be utilized, and should be supplemented
under a future USAID loan now under consideration. Land development works
would, at least in the initial years, be confined to the two intensive
RDA's, where the following works would be undertaken: (i) about 2,600 ha
fully terraced, (ii) about 5,000 ha terraced at a reduced level (about 60%
less intensive than full level terracing), and (iii) existing grass strips
removed and realigned as necessary to follow the slope contour. About 2,000
homesteads would be resettled and new homesites mechanically levelled, as
part of agreed plans for land consolidation and designation of arable and
grazing areas. Such resettlement and land redistribution has been successful
in existing RDA's, provided plans have the full support of the chiefs and
affected population. Dongas (gullies) would be reclaimed and the construction
of over 40 km of artificial waterways would control drainage. A limited num-
ber of fish ponds and small (10-50 ha) irrigation schemes would be developed
as conditions permit. The principal investment items would include expend-
itures for land development (equipment operating costs, maintenance, and
spares), materials, and incremental maintenance costs (Annex 11, Tables 11-13).



- 16 -
(d)  Agricultural Inputs
3.12      Incremental agricultural i.ftct would be supplied to farmers in
RDA's through CCU. The principal i..'uts provided would be fertilizer, pesti-
cides, planting materials, and suppiies for marketing and storage. Estimates
of requirements are based on past experience in RDA's and agricultural produc-
tion projections. Annex 11, Table 14 shows estimated Project requirements.
(e) Agricultural Credit Services
3.13      Availability of credit to Swazi farmers in RDA's would be increased
through measures to strengthen SDSB field operations in RDA's. SDSB staff
would be assigned to RDA's, and provided with an office cum house and a vehi-
cle. They would assist extension staff and cooperatives in arranging seasonal
credit for agricultural inputs and in recovering loans. Credit usage should
increase, which should aid SDSB and the cooperatives in designing better
credit services, reducing the cost of credit, and determining real costs of
providing credit, with a view to establishing clearer interest rate policies.
The principal investment items would be buildings, vehicles, materials, and
incremental operating costs (Annex 11, Tables 15-17).
(f) Road Development
3.14      About 900 kilometers of new access and feeder roads would be con-
structed within the ten RDA's; existing roads in Mahamba-Zombodze would
be improved and partly retraced to conform with resettlement plans. The
roads would lead from farms to extension centers and improve access to
adjacent regions and towns. They would be graded, but not graveled. About
300 culverts and a limited number of drifts and bridges would complement road
development works. The principal investment items would be materials, equip-
ment operating costs, and labor; labor costs represent approximately 50% of
total road development costs (Annex 11, Table 18).
(g) Social Infrastructure
3.15      Two rural clinics would be constructed and equipped in Lubombo-
Mpolonjeni, and an addition built to an existing rural clinic in Mahamba-
Zombodze. Health facility requirements in other RDA's have not yet been
assessed. Each clinic would be provided with an ambulance and basic medical
equipment. The principal investment items would be: construction costs,
vehicles and equipment, building maintenance, and vehicle and equipment
operating costs. A limited number of rural water supplies for human con-
sumption would be developed with the cooperation of the RDA staff and local
population. Most would be simple gravity systems conveying water to taps
near homesteads. Investments would include materials, development costs,
and maintenance (Annex 11, Tables 19-21).
(h) Project Management
3.16      The RDA management unit within the Ministry of Agriculture would
be expanded and strengthened to enable it to manage and coordinate develop-
ments in all 18 RDA's. Project investments would include technical assist-
ance, staff salaries, vehicles and equipment, and management operating costs
(Annex 11, Table 22).



- 17 -
(i) Technical Services
3.17      Technical services would include:
(a) Technical assistance for crops and livestock; 15 man-years
of technical assistance would be provided to assist the
Government in replacing key staff previously provided under
FAO/UNDP projects;
(b) Training for Project management staff and field extension
staff; a certificate-level training program would be intro-
duced in cooperation with UBS to train field officers, in-
service training would be organized, and pilot farmer
training would be undertaken;
(c) A livestock marketing study and future Project prepara-
tion; and
(d) Monitoring and evaluation of Project progress through teams
of survey workers backed up by qualified staff of the
Ministry of Agriculture and the Central Statistical Office.
Further details on investments are given in Annex 11, Table 23.

E. Financing
3.19      The financing of Project costs would be shared as follows:

3.20      The proposed IBRD loan of US$4.0 million would be to the Government
of Swaziland; it would carry interest at 8.5% a year, and would be repayable
over 20 years including 4.5 years of grace. The loan would finance about 26%
of total net Project costs. The African Development Bank (Nigerian Trust
Fund) has agreed to provide a loan of US$5.4 million equivalent to finance
part of the Project; the loan would carry interest at 4%, repayable over 25
years, including five years grace, and would finance most Project buildings,
road development, livestock infrastructure (except fencing), irrigation dams
and fish pond developments, and operating costs for livestock developments and
agricultural credit services. The European Development Fund has agreed to
provide a grant equivalent to US$2.9 million to finance costs of extension
services (except buildings) in the eight less intensive RDA's, incremental
agricultural inputs for four years, Project training costs, and technical
assistance for the senior RDA's manager. ODM has agreed to provide technical
assistance for the Project valued at about US$300,000. Financing of Project
components would be on a parallel basis; the co-financing partners have agreed
that the Project will be implemented as a fully integrated operation, dis-
tinguishing among respective donor financed components only for purposes of
procurement and disbursement. The co-financing partners would cooperate
closely during Project implementation. Local sources in Swaziland would
finance 18% of net Project costs. Of this amount, the Government's contri-
bution would amount to about 16% of total Project costs (net of taxes);
farmers would contribute about 2% of Project costs, including financing of
seasonal inputs, contributions to livestock infrastructure, land development,
and buildings, as well as on-going maintenance costs. Financing details are
shown in Annex 12, Table 2. Signature of a loan agreement between the AfDB
and the Government of Swaziland and fulfillment of AfDB conditions of effec-
tiveness, and signature of the financing agreement between the EDF and the
Government and fulfillment of EDF conditions of effectiveness would be con-
ditions of effectiveness for the IBRD loan.
3.21      Funds for all Project investments financed by IBRD, except agri-
cultural credit services, and rural health, would be channelled by the Govern-
ment to the Ministry of Agriculture. Funds for rural health would be passed
on to the Ministry of Health. Funds for the agricultural credit services
component would be passed on to SDSB as a Government grant, since it would be



- 20 -
intended as a program to assist in developing effective credit institutions
and policies. Assurances as to financing arrangements were obtained during
negotiations. Retroactive financing up to US$100,000 would be provided from
November, 1976 for employment of Project staff and preliminary development
activities.
F. Procurement
3.22      For Project components financed by IBRD, contracts for the supply of
vehicles and equipment (US$1.4 million 1/) and fencing materials (US$0.4 mil-
lion), exceeding US$50,000 would be awarded after international competitive
bidding in accordance with Bank guidelines. Contracts for goods and services
costing less than US$50,000 would be awarded following Government procurement
procedures, which are satisfactory. The Central Transport Organization (CTO)
would be responsible for tendering and for maintenance of all Project vehicles.
Land development works (US$1.9 million) would be carried out by the Land Devel-
opment Section of the Ministry of Agriculture by force account. In view of
their small size, remoteness and dispersed location, all of these civil works
would not attract international bids; interest by qualified local contractors
would also be limited. Draft tender documents for all contracts exceeding
US$50,000 would be submitted to IBRD for approval before invitations to bid
were issued. Assurances as to the above procedures were obtained at negotia-
tions. Procurement for parts of the Project financed by AfDB and EDF would be
based on the standard procedures of these institutions.
