H. Accounts and Audit
3.24      The Financial Controller, under the supervision of the Chief Proj-
ject Coordinator, would maintain separate records and accounts adequate to
monitor Project progress and to identify expenditures for respective Proj-
ect components. In addition, SDSB would maintain accounts for Project funds
channelled through it. SDSB accounts are audited by a private auditing firm
based in Swaziland; past accounts have been fully satisfactory. CCU accounts
are not satisfactory, but steps are being taken to improve accounting pro-
cedures and institute proper audit. Assurances were be obtained at negotia-
tions that: (i) separate accounts for the Project would be maintained by the
Ministry of Agriculture and SDSB, consistent with accepted accounting practice;
(ii) independent accountants, selected by the Government and acceptable to
IBRD, would audit Project accounts annually; and (iii) the Government would
make available the audited accounts and a report thereon to IBRD within six
months after the close of each fiscal year. The auditor for Government
accounts would probably be the Auditor General for Swaziland, since he is by
law responsible for auditing all Public Accounts (Annex 16); organization of
that office is satisfactory. Several good audit firms are based in or repre-
sented in Swaziland.
I. Environmental Impact
3.25      The Project would include several measures explicitly intended
to alleviate one of Swaziland's most significant environmental problems -
soil erosion. Land development works, notably terracing, grass strip
improvement, and gully rehabilitation, would protect soil from erosion.
Further, the livestock development components would form part of the
Government's overall efforts to improve traditional herd management; over-
stocking and indiscriminate grazing are now the most serious cause of soil
erosion. The Ministry of Agriculture would restrict development in and
actively protect valleys in the Lubombo Plateau where rare species of flora
and fauna are found. Project-supported health services also would have a
positive environmental impact.



- 22 -
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Project Management and Coordination
4.01      The Ministry of Agriculture would have principal responsibility
for management of most Project components and for coordination of overall
Project implementation. The Department of Agriculture within the Ministry
would carry out extension programs and land development activities in all
RDA's, would oversee staff of other ministries in these areas, and would be
responsible for ensuring that proper coordination took place on matters of
interministerial concern. For its implementation, the Project would rely
to a great extent on existing staff and institutions (including SDSB and
CCU), and the principal task of Project management would be to strengthen
and coordinate existing services and administrative units. The proposed
Project would be administered jointly with the ODM-financed Rural Develop-
ment Areas Program (para 3.03), and most arrangements discussed below would
apply equally to both projects.
4.02      The Director of the Agriculture Department would oversee manage-
ment of Project developments, through an RDA Management Unit responsible
directly to him (Chart 2). This unit would include a Chief Project Coordi-
nator, Deputy Project Coordinator, Financial Controller, Senior RDA's Manager,
and appropriate supporting staff. Annex 13, appendix 1 shows terms of re-
ference for these posts. Appointment of staff to these four positions would
be a condition of loan effectiveness. The RDA Management Unit would have a
dual role: it would plan and implement directly, through ministry divisions,
several Project components, notably agricultural extension,land development
and RDA livestock developments; further, the Unit would mobilize and coor-
dinate activities of other administrative units responsible for developments
in RDA's, both within the Ministry of Agriculture and in other ministries,
notably land use planning, supply of inputs and marketing, credit services,
and social infrastructure. The Unit would be supervise directly all Agricul-
ture Department staff working in RDA's, the Senior Agricultural Officer would
supervise staff working in all other areas. A "Project Manager" would be
posted in each intensive development RDA. Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that IBRD would approve the role and responsibilities of the
RDA Management Unit.
4.03      An interministerial committee would be established to assist
in Project coordination. It would include senior representatives of the
following Ministries and agencies: Ministry of Agriculture (the Permanent
Secretary-Chairman and Chief Project Coordinator-Secretary), Ministry of
Finance, Ministry of Commerce and Cooperatives, Ministry of Health, Ministry
of Education, Ministry of Works, Power and Communications, SDSB, CCU and
CRDB. The Committee would meet at least twice a year to act on all out-
standing Project issues, notably those involving policy matters or requiring
inter-ministerial decision, and to assess Project progress. Three existing
committees would also assist in Project coordination: the Rural Development
Committee of the Cabinet, on major issues requiring high level coordination,
the marketing and inputs committee which plans input purchases and marketing
activities, and the Projects Committee of the Ministry of Agriculture, which
coordinates programs within the Ministry and acts on all decisions involving
deployment of funds (Annex 5).



- 23 -
4.04      The Central Rural Development Board (CRDB) (para. 2.18) would be
directly involved in Project implementation at two stages:  approval of land
use plans, and overseeing implementation of those plans in all RDA's. The
CRDB's responsibility is principally to ensure that development programs are
consistent with Swazi tradition and reflect the wishes of the population.
CRDB action on all land use plans is thus vital to Project success, both to
ensure that Swazi Nation institutions, which have primary responsibility for
matters affecting Swazi Nation Land, support all Project developments, and
because the CRDB represents the population affected by the Project. Rural
Development Officers, who represent the CRDB, would assist in preparing plans,
securing their approval, and in overseeing and enforcing their implementation.
Management of Project Components
4.05      Land Use Planning:  The first stage of development in all RDA's
would be preparation of land use plans, broken down by individual chiefdoms.
The Land Planning Section of the Ministry of Agriculture would prepare plans
(as it has done in the past for all RDA's), beginning with surveys of the
area, followed by consultation with Rural Development Officers, Chiefs,
and the local population. The plans specify delineation of arable and grazing
areas, location of homesites, and of other buildings and centers. Poten-
tial for small-scale irrigation schemes is explored. The plan is then sub-
mitted to the CRDB, which again consults with chiefs concerned to ascertain
their views. The CRDB then approves the plan, often with modifications,
and it becomes binding; chiefs are required to indicate their approval and
agreement to implement the plan through a signed document. Only then can the
plan be implemented. Land use plans would be prepared initially for the two
intensive RDA's, then for the other Project RDA's. Assurances were obtained
at negotiations that land use plans would be prepared, and appropriate appro-
vals secured.
4.06      Extension.  The RDA Management Unit would develop effective field
extension services in each RDA, building on existing services, but expanding
and diversifying them to permit more frequent and focussed contact with
farmers. In the two intensive RDA's (para. 3.04), a Project manager would
directly oversee all extension staff; in other RDA's, an assistant extension
officer would have responsibility for extension work. Agriculture and
livestock extension work would be handled by the same field officers, who
would be multipurpose agents; for livestock work, the Veterinary Services
Department would provide additional technical support. The target ratio of
extension workers to farmers is 1:200. Some specialized extension staff would
work on crops with special requirements. The extension staff would rely
on technical support from the Crop Production Unit; assurances were obtained
at negotiations that the Government would ensure that the unit was adequately
staffed with qualified specialists in the fields of tobacco, cotton, grains
and legumes, and horticulture.
4.07      Livestock Development.  Project managers would direct livestock
extension in intensive RDA's. Developments would commence with delineation of
arable and grazing land (planning stage). Promotion of destocking would have
the highest priority, and would involve popularization of fattening and



- 24 -
breeding ranches, better organization of marketing, and provision of better
price information to farmers. Extension efforts would seek to convince local
population of adverse effects of overstocking on pasture quality and animal
condition. Extension staff would then plan and, in cooperation with the local
population, carry out the fencing program, initially perimeter fencing sep-
arating arable areas from pasture, then establishing paddocks within grazing
areas. Promotion of improved herd management and pasture improvement would
follow. Priority would be given to herd rotation and upgrading of the herd
through provision of improved bulls (Brahman and Simmentalher), as part of the
National Breeding Program. Farmers would be encouraged to focus on production
of beef on communal pastures, and to obtain milk for family consumption or for
sale from small dairy units within the arable areas. IBRD would expect to
review the overall livestock development program for Swaziland, given its
crucial importance for other aspects of rural development. Assurances were
obtained during negotiations that the Government would consult with the Bank
at least once a year on its overall strategy for livestock development and on
progress made in implementing it. Further, assurances were obtained that
adequate staff would be available to provide technical support for the live-
stock program as it affects RDA's, and specifically that qualified specialists
would be available for beef production, range management, and livestock
extension.
4.08      Land Development and Road Improvement.  The Land Development Section
of the Ministry of Agriculture would carry out all land development activi-
ties in RDA's; development would be focused in intensive RDA's, with the
exception of road improvement, which would be carried out in all RDA's. The
Land Development Section would organize and mobilize units near each RDA where
development work was planned; actual works would be supervised by the unit
manager, in cooperation with the RDA Project Manager. All land development
works would be in accordance with approved land-use plans. USAID has provided
equipment and technical assistance to the Land Development Section in the
past, and is now considering an expanded program of assistance, which would
include considerable additional equipment and technical assistance. Land
Development works have in the past included terracing of virtually all arable
land in RDA's; in Project RDA's, terracing works would be limited to those
areas where slope justified terracing as a soil conservation technique.
Criteria for selection of areas for terracing are discussed in Annex 1,
Section F. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Gov-
ernment would consult with IBRD on the program of terracing in all Project
RDA's,indicating the rationale for terracing where it is recommended, prior
to undertaking such works. For all land development works, the full involve-
ment of the population would be essential, particularly when plans called for
land consolidation and resettlement of homestead. The RDA Management Unit
would work closely with the chiefs, CRDB and the population.
4.09      Agricultural Inputs.  CCU would be responsible for organizing
the supply and distribution of agricultural inputs. Orders would be placed
following the decision of the Marketing and Inputs Committee, and would be
based principally on RDA staff projections of input requirements in RDA's.
CCU would order from suppliers and arrange for delivery of inputs supplies



- 25 -
to CCU-operated depots. CCU would sell inputs to farmers for cash, or on
seasonal credit provided either by SDSB or through primary cooperative socie-
ties (paras. 2.24 and 2.25).
4.10      Agricultural Credit Services.  SDSB would be responsible for imple-
mentation of the agricultural credit component, which would be intended to
supplement the on-going USAID supported farmer credit scheme. SDSB would
recruit and second credit advisers to RDA's, where they would work closely
with extension staff and with Cooperative Department personnel. Assurances
were obtained that: (i) the Government would consult with IBRD at least
once a year on credit policies for small farmers, and specifically on credit
programs involving RDA's and the cost of credit, and (ii) that SDSB would
report on small farmer lending and specifically on its cost and implications
for interest rates at least once a year, and discuss these reports with IBRD.
4.11      Social Infrastructure.  The Ministry of Health would be responsi-
ble for planning and supervising construction, expansion, equipping and
operation of clinics. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
that the Government would provide adequate and qualified staff for all
clinics developed under the Project. RDA staff and PWD would develop
domestic water supplies. Simple gravity systems would be built in coopera-
tion with the benefitting population, who would have full responsibility for
maintenance of the systems. The water supply program would be integrated
with a national rural water supply plan now under preparation.
4.12      Technical Services.  The Ministry of Agriculture would have prin-
cipal responsibility for technical services, including recruitment and super-
vision of technical assistance personnel, preparation of terms of reference
for studies, direction of training activities, and monitoring and evaluation
of Project progress. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the
Government would: (i) employ qualified technical assistants for key Project
management posts (Chief Coordinator, Deputy, Senior RDA's manager, Financial
Controller) if Swazi staff are not available; (ii) obtain IBRD approval of the
terms of reference, experience and qualifications required for these posi-
tions; and (iii) consult with IBRD on appointments. IBRD would also approve
terms of reference for other technical assistance posts and be consulted on
proposed appointments. Assurances were obtained that the Government would
submit for prior IBRD approval terms of reference and proposals for studies,
and proposals for monitoring and evaluation would be approved by IBRD.
Monitoring of Project progress would be the responsibility of the Economics
Section of the Ministry of Agriculture, which would be supplemented by an
economist. Teams of enumerators would survey farmers in RDA's to determine
impact of extension and operating problems.
Annual Work Programs and Project Budget
4.13      The RDA Management Unit, in cooperation with the Economics Section
of the Ministry of Agriculture and the Ministry of Finance, would prepare an
annual work program, as part of the Government's budget process. This program
should include IBRD, ODM, USAID and other directly related activities.



- 26 -
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Government would submit
to IBRD for its review and concurrence a draft program by August 31 of each
year. This would be discussed and agreed upon during Project supervision
missions. The program should include full cost information, distinguishing
various donor-financed activities; modifications to Project investment pro-
posals should be introduced where implementation experience so warrants.
Technical Assistance and Training
4.14      Expatriate technical assistants would be required to fill several
Project management positions throughout the period of Project implementation,
because of severe shortages of trained Swazi staff (para 2.21). Expatriate
staff could be recruited directly by the Government or obtained through bi-
lateral agencies. Swazi staff should be recruited to fill positions wherever
possible, and assurances were obtained during negotiations that the
Government would discuss with IBRD on an annual basis its proposals for
training Swazi nationals to fill all positions. At a minimum, a qualified
Swazi agriculturalist should fill the post of deputy RDA coordinator, and
Swazis should be designated and trained to fill other established management
posts in future; no full-time counterparts would be assigned to expatriate
staff, as the Government prefers to post Swazis in established positions as
soon as formal training is completed. Swazis might fill one or more of the
RDA "project manager" posts, and should certainly hold those posts before
1980. Organization of a proposed new certificate level training program for
field extension staff would be essential for implementation of the proposed
Project, as there are now insufficient field officers to permit an expan-
sion of current extension programs. Development of the course would be a
condition of effectiveness.
Central Transport Organisation (Annex 5)
4.15      Central Transport Organisation is a centralized vehicle and equip-
ment pool serving all government agencies; it is responsible for vehicles
procurement, replacement and maintenance. CTO holds title to the vehicles
and equipment pool and charges a rental to the agencies which lease indivi-
dual items. CTO's good service performance would be of great importance
to ensure satisfactory Project implementation. CTO would maintain all vehi-
cles required for the Project and would earmark all vehicles purchased with
Project funds for Project purposes. In the event that CTO was unable to
provide adequate maintenance services to Project implementing agencies, the
Government would ensure that these agencies could service essential Project
vehicles through the private sector. Assurances were obtained that the
Government would consult with the Bank on vehicle and equipment renewal
policies and charges levied for use of Project vehicles.
Project Implementation Plan and Periodic Reporting (Annex 14)
4.16      Project implementation procedures were discussed during nego-
tiations, and a Project implementation plan was agreed upon. Assurances were
obtained that the Ministry of Agriculture would be responsible for submit-
ting quarterly reports on Project progress to IBRD, which would include
full financial information, discussion of principal achievements, and prog-
ress of Project monitoring and evaluation.



- 27 -
V.  PRODUCTION, MARKETING, FARM INCOME, AND GOVERNMENT BENEFITS
Incremental Production
5.01      Incremental output in Project RDA's would be derived primarily from
increased production of a range of crops and increased productivity of cattle.
Increased crop production would result principally from raising yields on
existing farms through more intensive cultivation, improved farming methods,
and use of more and correct packages of inputs; increases in cultivated areas
would also augment production. Increasing emphasis would be placed on cash
crops (cotton, tobacco, rice, vegetables), but active extension of hybrid
maize should enable families to satisfy consumption requirements with less
land, thus freeing larger parts of holdings for cash crop production. Land
consolidation and terracing of arable areas should contribute to raising
yields by making farming more efficient. Incremental crop production has
been estimated as follows, based on projections of crop production in each
RDA (Annex 10):
Crop             Year 6                 Year 20
 -------(tons)------------
Maize             13,000                17,000
Beans                350                   700
Groundnuts           750                 1,300
Potatoes           2,000                 6,500
Cotton             1,200                 3,100
Tobacco              500                 1,000
Other                500                 1,000
It should be emphasized that these projections are tentative, given poor
data available, and represent an approximate order of magnitude of expected
output. Livestock development programs (in conjunction with overall national
schemes) should increase the productivity of traditional cattle herds in the
two intensive RDA's; productivity increases would result principally from
efforts to destock pastures, as this would reduce herd size, permitting
improved stock quality, but also from better herd management and pasture
development (rotational grazing, upgrading through use of improved bulls,
bush clearing, artificial pastures). Marketed offtake should increase, and
weight and grade should improve with better management. Incremental sales
of cattle are estimated at 3,000 head a year in year 6 and 5,000 head a year
by year 20 (Annex 10). Some additional milk production should result from
Project extension efforts.
Marketing
5.02      Virtually all incremental Project production would be marketed
through commercial channels, and Project investments should assist farmers
in selling crops and livestock by improving facilities, transport and price
information, in conjunction with a recently approved USAID Cooperative Mar-
keting Project. Small amounts of crop production (particularly beans and
groundnuts) and of cattle and milk would be consumed by farmers. Increased



- 28 -
maize production should enable Swaziland to reduce imports from South Africa,
although not entirely eliminating future import requirements. Maize and
other staple food crops would be marketed through local buyers or the
Swaziland Milling Corporation (para. 2.14). Cotton and tobacco would be
exported to South Africa through existing channels (para. 2.13), although
a new tobacco buying center and a new ginnery, with Swazi ownership, may
be established during the life of the Project. No difficulty should arise
in marketing output since quantities are small relative to South African
requirements. Cattle would be sold live to South Africa or slaughtered at
the Swaziland Meat Corporation for export (para 2.15). The UK has been the
traditional market for Swazi beef, and EEC import quotas and levies on imports
have posed problems for Swaziland; however, recently negotiated agreements
with the EEC have established quotas and rebates of import levies which are
satisfactory, at least in the short term (Annex 4). A marketing study should
further assist the Government in identifying new markets for beef.
5.03      Prices of all commodities produced except maize are not controlled
by the Swazi Government, and in the past market prices have been determined
by world market forces, which largely set South African prices for the com-
modities concerned.  Actual farm-gate prices should be somewhat increased,
notably for cattle, by marketing improvements under the Project. However,
prices received by Swazi producers do not differ substantially from market
prices (which are used in this report) because of Swaziland's small size and
the limited role of intermediaries. Maize prices are controlled by the
Government and include subsidies to producers and consumers; however, these
subsidies are lower than those applied in South Africa. The Meat Corporation
applies a floor price for cattle, but it is substantially lower than market
prices (Annex 4).
Producer and Government Benefits
5.04      Farm Income.  Farmers in Project RDA's should benefit from sub-
stantial increases in cash income. All aspects of the Rural Development
Program (extension, inputs, credit, marketing, roads) should assist farm-
ers in raising yields and diversifying to cash crops; the cash investment
required by farmers would be small in relation to expected return. Pro-
jections of increases in farm income are unreliable, given limited past
experience and poor base data. However, estimates of production costs and
yields (Annex 10) suggest that cash income could be at least doubled. Pre-
development farm income in the Highveld averages E100 (US$115) a year;
Middleveld farm income is estimated at about E245 (US$282), and Lowveld
income about E200 (US$130). It is expected that, by Project Year 6, farm-
ers in all RDA's would on average increase cash income from crops by 100-175%;
increases in income would be highest in the Highveld and Lowveld, and lowest
in the Middleveld. In intensive RDA's, the estimated increase in income from
cattle sales is estimated at about E60 (US$69), or an increase of about 80%
above pre-development levels. Estimates of gross margins at successive de-
velopment levels (Annex 10, Tables 1-8) suggest that increases in income
and in cost:benefit ratios would be adequate to ensure high levels of farmer
participation. The increase in production resulting from additional labor
inputs on several crops, notably tobacco, cotton, and vegetables, should be



- 29 -
relatively high. Increases in labor requirements are estimated at 65-80%
above pre-development levels. Swazi officials believe that labor should be
readily available, given population pressure and the present relatively low
labor input in cropping in most regions.
5.05      Impact on Government Budget.  The Project would entail, to a large
extent, expenditures which cannot be recovered directly from beneficiaries.
Further, it would involve development of services and installations which
would require on-going budgetary commitments by the Government; notably, de-
velopment of extension services would require long-term commitments of staff
and operating costs. Efforts would be made to maximize farmer participation
in development works, including fencing, pasture improvement, and water supply
installation, and to ensure that local communities assume most, if not
all, recurring maintenance costs. This would lessen the financial burden
on the Government, but should also ensure that local population was fully
involved in Project Implementation. Government support of initial invest-
ments and basic services would be appropriate, however, given the very low
level of development of Project RDA's and sharp disparities in income and
available services between population in these areas and elsewhere in Swazi-
land. Farmers would pay the full cost of agricultural inputs, and should
increasingly assume a greater share of the cost of credit (Annex 7). The
Government intends to elaborate more explicit policies towards the issue
of recovery of expenditures on rural development; in order to encourage a
continuing and constructive dialogue on the subject, assurances were obtained
during negotiations that the Government would consult with IBRD at least once
a year on cost recovery policies. The estimated recurring costs likely to
result from Project investments after Year 6 would be between US$700,000 -
US$750,000 (including physical contingencies); of this, perhaps 10% would be
maintenance costs borne by farmers. While these costs are high, the Govern-
ment's fiscal position should enable it to bear these additional expendi-
tures. Benefits to the Government would be limited to increased tax revenue
from local processing companies.
VI. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
Benefits
6.01      The benefits of the Project would consist of:  (i) increased produc-
tion and marketing'of crops (valued at US$7.2 million at full development)'and
livestock (valued at US$0.9 million at full development); (ii) provision of
rural infrastructure and services (roads, water supply, health centers, nutri-
tion, information, and family planning); (iii) strengthening of overall capa-
city to manage rural development projects; and (iv) protection of arable and
grazing areas against soil erosion. Most of incremental Project production
would be exported, resulting in foreign exchange earnings, and import require-
ments for maize and other staple foods should be reduced.
6.02      The economic rate of return from the directly productive elements
of the Project is estimated at between 20 and 25% over 20 years (Annex 18).
All Project investment costs which relate directly to expected increases in



- 30 -
agricultural production were included in the analysis; further, estimates of
costs of other incremental development schemes which should directly affect
Project RDA's were included in the analysis. Costs of social infrastructure
and a share of central management costs and technical services attributable
to ODM-financed RDA's and future project preparation were excluded from the
analysis (estimated cost about US$1 million total). An estimate of the value
of incremental farm labor required for the Project was included in the analy-
sis. Unskilled labor employed for construction of Project civil works was
valued at the prevailing rate for agricultural labor, which was assumed to
reflect the economic cost of under-employed labor. Foreign exchange costs and
benefits were valued at prevailing exchange rates. Prices used in calcula-
tions are 1975-76 market prices for crops and cattle, which were assumed to be
equivalent to farmgate prices, increased by 15% to reflect expected price
increases to February 1977; expected shifts in world market prices of princi-
pal cash crops were taken into account. The rate of return analysis indicates
that the Project should yield significant benefits to the economy of Swazi-
land; it should be emphasized, however, that the analysis entails many uncer-
tainties, including poor initial data and lack of quantifiable field experi-
ence, the existence of a large number of other projects and programs affecting
Project RDA's, rendering attribution of benefits to any one scheme problema-
tic, and uncertainties involved in predicting a "with" and "without" Project
scenario. The rates of return were tested for sensitivity, which indicated
that they are most sensitive to delays in field level implementation and to
major shifts in relationships between production costs and prices.
Risk
6.03      The proposed Project does not entail substantial risks, in large
part because it is based on an on-going and relatively successful development
program. The degree of success of the Project will, however, depend on the
effectiveness of extension efforts and receptivity of farmers, and on the rate
at which physical developments can be implemented. This, in turn, will hinge
principally on the caliber of central management and of staff, and on support
for Project developments proferred by local chiefs and the CRDB. One risk is
that services and infrastructure will not be maintained following the invest-
ment period. Finally, the participation of several cofinancing partners
in the Project will complicate the task of Project management. The Project
includes measures to minimize these risks through provisions for central
management, training, technical assistance, and emphasis on cooperation with
traditional authorities. The management risks are offset to some degree by
Swaziland's previous experience with implementing rural development programs.
Further, annual discussion of work programs should permit adaptation of the
Project as experience warrants. Project success would be curtailed if on-
going and proposed programs financed by other donors ceased, but the Project
would not depend directly on their continuation. The Project offers good
prospects for improving services and raising incomes in the poorest areas of
Swaziland, and developments should be readily replicable in areas not covered
under current development schemes.



- 31 -
Employment and Income Distribution
6.04      An estimated 125,000 people would benefit from the Project, both
from increased cash income and subsistence production, and from improved
social services. Of these, about 44,000 would benefit from more concentrated
social services and extension in the two intensive RDA's. The population
affected would represent the lowest income group of Swaziland; current esti-
mated family cash incomes are exceedingly low (US$115-280) and they compare
with current GNP per family of at least US$3,000 (of which most represents
cash income); however, these figures and comparisons are not very meaningful
given the sharp duality of the economy and the dominant role played by large
expatriate firms and farmers.   Project investments in agriculture should
increase labor input in Project RDA's by the equivalent of 2,500 man-years a
year. Most civil works would be labor intensive and would employ an estimated
500 additional persons during much of the construction period.
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
