SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture and Livestock - Sector Review
1.        A description of the Swaziland agricultural sector (including
livestock) is presented, as follows:
(a) General, including geographic regions, climate, soils.
(b) Crop Production.
(c) Livestock.
(d) Fisheries.
(e) Agricultural Research.
(f) Soil Conservation.
A. General
2.        Swaziland, one of the smallest 'ountries in Africa, measures
about 190 by 145 km, and covers 17,360 km . Population density is about 29
per sq km, but it is now projected that it will rise to over 60 per sq'km
within 25 years. Agriculture dominates the economy and society; it has fueled
Swaziland's recent economic growth, provided more than 60% of the value of
exports in 1975, and employs about 53% of the active population.l/ Swazi-
land's agriculture is surprisingly complex and diverse. The complexity arises
both from geography, since there are a wide range of ecological conditions,
and from the historical background which has resulted in a sharp dichotomy
between a modern, largely foreign-owned farming sector, and traditional,
subsistence agriculture.
Geographic Regions
3.        Four geographic regions are commonly distinguished, each extending
north-south (See map 2). They range from temperate highlands to low-lying
semi-tropical savannah, and are summarized in Table 1. Regional characteris-
tics can be briefly described as follows:
1/   Modern sector only



ANNEX 1
Page 2
(a) The Highveld 1/: is a mountainous area in western Swaziland,
forming the eastern edge of the South Africa Highveld. The
topography is sharply dissected with deep gorges; several
perennial rivers cross the area. Large parts of the highveld
are covered with pine and eucalyptus forests, most of which are
man-made. Much land is under natural pasture.
(b) The Middleveld: lies east of the Highveld. The topography is
rolling terrain, ranging from mountainous in the west to gently
undulating savanna in the east.
(c) The Lowveld: is generally flat savannah-like terrain. The
climate is semi-tropical, dry savanna with characteristic
vegetation.
(d) The Lubombo Plateau: in the extreme west of the country,
rises from the Lowveld in a sharp escarpment. Topography is
similar to the Middleveld. The terrain is rolling, hilly
country, sharply dissected by valleys and gorges.
Climate
4.        In general, Swaziland's climate may be described as subtropical
with summer rains; 75-83% of the precipitation occurs in the summer. Mean
rainfall varies from 508 mm at Big Bend (Lowveld) to 2286 mm in Nottingham
(Highveld). Rains are seriously deficient, even in the Highveld, during an
average of one year in ten. Climate histograms, including rainfall and
temperatures, are shown in Table 2; Table 3 shows recent monthly rainfall
figures for two locations. Swaziland has a good network of long-term weather
recording stations, some dating from the nineteenth century, so that rainfall
data is plentiful and relatively reliable.
Soils
5.        The distribution of major soil types is closely related to the
relief and geological units. In the Highveld, Upper Middleveld and Lubombo
ecological zones the main soil types are deep, acid and freely drained red and
yellow ferrisolic and ferralitic soils. Many soils are underlain by quartz
stonelines which can impede mechanical cultivation. Although their natural
fertility is low, (except in Lubombo), with adequate fertilizer and lime
applications these soils yield well. Drainage, infiltration rates and avail-
able moisture holding capacity is excellent for dryland or irrigated cropping.
Throughout the Lower Middleveld, soils are generally grey or red light textured
soils derived from granite or gneiss. The rooting depth is between 40 and 70
cms., although on the flatter slopes it increases to 100 cms. or more.
1/   Veld has several connotations in Southern Africa, but normally means
terrain or topography.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
Shallowness and light texture restrict available moisture holding capacities
for rainfed cropping. Fertility is generally low and erodibility high. The
Western Lowveld is underlain by sandstones and shales which give rise to heavy
textured clay-pan soils, often covered with light textured material. Poor
sub-soil drainage and high exchangeable sodium restrict their use for irrigated
cropping, while low available moisture holding capacities in the light textured
topsoils tend to reduce rainfed crop yields. The Eastern Lowveld is underlain
by basalt which gives rise to an association of red, brown, and black clays.
Shallow variants (20 to 40 cms.) of these clays cover about 45% of the area.
The fertility of these soils is excellent although phosphorus is deficient.
Poor drainage is a problem with the black clays, and sodicity and salinity
occurs in some low lying soils. However, for rainfed cropping (of cotton)
these soils yield well, provided rainfall is sufficient. Annex 8 gives
further information on soils in Project RDA's and Appendix 1 summarizes the
principal soil classifications used in Swaziland.
Slopes
6.        Slopes vary considerably; they range from a median gradient of
3% in the Lowveld to 12% in the Middleveld, although there
are some lower continguous areas with less than 7% slopes along the Lowveld
margin. In the broken Highveld the slopes are from 18-50% or steeper.
Table 4 shows land potential according to slope.
Land Use
7.        Land use patterns and the organization of agricultural production
are sharply distinguished on freehold title land and on Swazi Nation land
(Annex 3). At present, about 43% of the total land area is held under freehold
title, while 57% is Swazi Nation Land. Table 5 gives a summary breakdown
of land use. In general, about 70% of the total land area is classified
as grazing land, with unimproved pastures; a further 6% is under improved
pastures. About 8% of the land area is under crops with a further 1% of
cropland lying fallow; about 6% of the land area is under forest. About
9% is classified as "other land", including urban areas, houses, roads,
water, and waste land. Marginal land and unusable land amount to more than
58% of the total. Considering natural characteristics alone, and taking no
account of social or economic conditions or prevailing levels of agricultural
technology, physical land potential may be summarized as follows:
Area '000 ha         %
Arable land 0-12% slope                385             22
Poor arable - good grazing             344             20
Wet land and rivers                     49              3
Grazing land                           452             26
Steep and Rocky                        506             29
Total land                  1,736            100



ANNEX 1
Page 4
B. Crop Production
8.        Agricultural production patterns differ materially among the four
geographic regions described above. In addition, there is a sharp distinc-
tion between a modern and traditional farming sector. Agricultural production
on freehold title land is generally operated on commercial lines by large
companies or expatriate farmers, with estates growing almost exclusively
export oriented crops. Most farming is well managed and makes use of modern
equipment and the inputs necessary for commercial production. Over 60% of
freehold title cropland is irrigated. On Swazi Nation Land, farming is mainly
for subsistence with an emerging tendency to grow more cash crops such as
cotton, tobacco, rice and vegetables. The major crops grown and production
are shown in Table 6. Table 7 shows production on Swazi Nation Land, and
Table 8 summarizes average yields. In summary, maize is grown on about 100,000
most Swazi Nation Land; sugarcane is grown on 17,000 ha, virtually all on
freehold title land; cotton occupies 13,000 ha, of which 9,000 is freehold
title land. Maize production was estimated at 88,000 tons in 1972/73, sugar
cane at 1,607,000 tons, and cotton at 12,000 tons. In terms of value, sugar
production was valued in 1972/73 at E13.4 million, citrus production at E2.8
million, and cotton on freehold title land at E2.7 million.
9.        The principal crops grown on Swazi Nation Land are described briefly
below: sugar cane and citrus, the principal economic crops in terms of
export value, are not included since their importance for small Swazi farmers
is minimal at present.
(i) Maize
10.       Mfaize is the staple food of the Swazi people and is the most widely
grown crop in the traditional areas of Swaziland. Table 9 shows maize produc-
tion data. About 40% is grown in the Middleveld, 34% in the Lowveld, 18% in
the Highveld and 8% in Lubombo. Yields fluctuate widely from season to season
and are in the range of 0.8 tons and 1.5 tons per hectare, with the lower
levels in the Lowveld region. Current yields are comparable to neighboring
countries. Experimental yields with new varieties and optimum inputs are
high, especially in the Middle and Highveld regions: normal expectations
under rain grown regime are 6.5 tons per hectare but substantially less in the
low rainfall regions. Recent seasons have produced 10 tons per hectare grain
yield from both rain and irrigated crops. Thus, the potential for very
substantial increases in yields has been proven but can only be achieved if
all inputs are used and if cultural practices are correct and properly timed.
Information as to variety and fertilizer usage, etc. is available; recommen-
dations have been derived following 16 years of agricultural research. The
optimum yields without irrigation are shown below:



ANNEX 1
Page 5
Average                    Expectation of
Attainable Yield    attaining yields out of
Region                                  Ton/ha                 10 Seasons
Highveld                              6.0 - 7.1                    9
Middleveld (moist)                    5.5 - 6.5                    6-7
Lumbobo                               4.5 - 5.5                    6
Middleveld (dry)                      2.5 - 2.5                    4
Lowveld                               2.0 - 2.5                    2-3
Source: Advisory Bulletin No. 7. U.B.L.S.
11.       The most significant single problem of maize growing in Swaziland
is witch weed (Striga sp.); this plant parasite attaches itself to the roots
of the maize plant and competes for moisture and soil nutrients with corres-
ponding loss in yield. Control is by either pulling out the plants before
flowering or by several sprayings of 2.4D for three seasons. A further
problem causing fluctuations in yields is variations in weather conditions
from year to year; dry periods of two to three weeks at the time of tasseling
can be a significant problem.
12.       Storage of maize also poses problems; whether used for feed or sale,
inadequate storage results in losses, now estimated at about 20%. The main
crop is harvested in March and is stored in cribs until dry enough for shelling.
Cobs are usually shelled in June or July and the grain is put into storage.
Emphasis must be placed on drying the grain to under 12.5% moisture; threshed
corn should be treated and stored in aluminum silos which have some shade to
prevent sweating. Traditional storage methods are underground pits, woven
straw baskets, mud tanks, and clay pots. Oil drums and jute sacks are also
used. A grain storage officer in Swaziland is currently carrying on and
expanding as existing Grain Storage Project.
13.       The importance of improving maize yields within the Swaziland
farming pattern is twofold: first, substantial quantities of maize are
imported from South Africa every year; if present acreage were to yield
2 tons per ha (a modest target compared with the potential) the present
shortfall could be met. Second, if a smaller acreage of maize satisfied
family food requirements through higher yields, this would free land for
production of cash crops, thus raising farm incomes.
(ii) Cotton
14.       Cotton is grown both as a dry land crop and with irrigation in the
Middle and Lowveld regions of Swaziland. Production by Swazi farmers has
increased rapidly since 1970, and in 1975 the Swazi Nation Land share of the
crop was estimated at 42%. Table 10 shows cotton production from 1963-64 to
1973-74.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
15.       The Swaziland Cotton Board was established in 1968.  Previously,
cotton matters were dealt with by a Cotton Advisory Committee. The principal
functions of the Board are: cotton improvement, chiefly by control of plant-
ing seed through the Seed Improvement Scheme; cotton breeding work and ento-
mology work financed by the Board at the Lowveld Experiment Station, Big Bend;
advice to the Minister of Agriculture on such matters as seed importation,
dates for cotton planting, eradication; and cotton marketing. The Board
administers the Cotton Fund, derived from the cotton levy paid by growers
annually from seed cotton deliveries to ginners; from this levy subventions
(E29,309 in 1975) are paid to the University of Botswana and Swaziland (UBS)
for cotton research and improvement of planting seed supplies.
16.       The varieties of cotton seed recommended by UBS are:
Dryland (Middleveld):   Albacola 70
Dryland (Lowveld):       Albacola 70
Irrigated (Lowveld):    Deltapine 5826
Albacola 70
Cotton seed is supplied by South African companies following competitive bidding.
17.       Recommendations on all aspects of cotton growing are available
to the farmer; there is a closed season by law and the eradication date
as well as the planting date are gazetted as legal notices. Research yields
are shown below (seed cotton):
With Irrigation:    Lowveld              ceiling of 4,000 kg (20 bales) per ha.
Dryland              Lower rainfall                  1,000 kg ( 5 bales) per ha.
Higher rainfall                 2,000 kg (19 bales) per ha.
The countrywide yield for 1973 was 1,000 kg per ha.
18.       The Cotona Cotton Ginnery at Matsapa (Annex 4) is involved in
an intensive effort to promote cotton growing in Swaziland. Credit is offered
on a crop lien basis to Swazi producers through the company at rates normally
1-1/2-2% above the going bank rate. The ginnery is responsible for collection
of the crop and the price is paid, according to grade, in two separate pay-
ments. The initial payment is made at the delivery date and the final payment
at the end of the season when the crop has been sold to the South African
spinners. Costs of inputs are deducted for these payments. The dramatic
increase in cotton production by small scale Swazi farmers reflects both the
favorable price situation and the promotional efforts of the Cotton Board, the
Ministry of Agriculture, and the ginnery. Consideration is being given to
localizing the cotton ginnery or to establishing a new ginnery with Swazi



ANNEX 1
Page 7
ownership. The Cotton Board's objective is to increase production to
33,000 tons of seed cotton by 1985. In this event, another large
ginnery would be required, probably in the south of the country.
(iii) Tobacco
19.       Production of tobacco is increasing rapidly on Swazi Nation Land.
Production in the 1960's was around 40% by Swazi Nation land farmers and this
rose to 80% in 1975, when about 1,760 Swazi farms produced tobacco. Total
national production in 1975 was 258 tons; total value, at 92 c per kilogram,
was estimated at E237,352. The potential for increasing tobacco production
is excellent, given soils and climate; average yields of 1,000 kg per hectare
are easily attainable. Best yields are 2,500 kg per hectare. The profit
margin is estimated at between E1,000 and E1,400 per hectare. Table 11 shows
Tobacco Production from 1960-1974.
20.       The tobacco section of the Ministry of Agriculture at present
consists of 1 senior extension officer, 2 extension officers, 2 assistant
extension officers, and 5 field officers. This staff is assisted by an
expatriate tobacco expert and an associate expert. The objectives are
to increase production in Swaziland to 500 tons per year. Three problems or
bottlenecks in production have been identified: (i) poor quality seedlings;
(ii) shortage of drying barns; (iii) marketing problems. The problems of
seedlings will be overcome by establishing large central seed beds (274 acres
already operating) and selling good quality seedlings. Encouragement in
building suitable barns will be responsibility of the extension staff.
Initially, the tobacco section will assist with transport of tobacco to the
Nhlangano buying center but in addition plans are proceeding to establish a
further buying center at Manzini.
(iv) Sorghum
21.       Approximately 3,000 hectares are under Sorghum.  The crop is mainly
grown in the Lowveld where it is useful because of its drought resistance.
The bulk of the production is used for home brewed beer. About 100 tons a
year are sold to the Swaziland Milling Company, which imports another 3,500 -
4,000 tons per year for distribution to local retailers. The sorghum price
for 1975 was E72 per ton. Traditional yields average 800-1,000 kg per hectare;
UBS has indicated a ceiling yield of 2,700 kg per hectare.
(v) Groundnuts
22.       An estimated 5,000 hectares of groundnuts were grown in 1974;
this has remained fairly constant over the past years. Groundnuts grow best
in the moist Middleveld to the Lowveld. Traditional farming yields are low,
in the range of 600-700 kg per hectare; research yields at Malkerns and
Nhlangano have been as high as 2,000-3,000 kg, and in the Middleveld at
Luve yields from 1,500-2,000 kg per hectare. The crop probably has limited
potential for expansion in Swaziland given low gross margin returns.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
(vi) Beans
23.       Dried bean production in Swaziland is estimated currently at 1,000
m. tons per year. Only small quantities are marketed commercially. Contender,
speckled sugar, red canadian wonder and brown harricot are the most important
varieties, with speckled sugar being the most popular. Under good management
900 kg-122 kg per hectare can be expected under rain grown conditions, and up
to 1,400 kg under irrigation. Prices vary according to variety; August, 1975
prices were as follows:
Sugar Beans                      E 24 per 90 kg bag
Contender Beans                  E 20 per 90 kg bag
Jugo Beans                       E 18 per 90 kg bag
The return per hectare can be high with recommended inputs.
(vii) Rice
24.       Almost all rice grown is on freehold title land in the Middle
and Lowveld regions. A large proportion is grown in C.D.C. holdings at
Tshaneni, where the rice is milled and sold to South Africa. Small areas of
rice are grown by Swazi farmers under the supervision of Taiwanese Agricul-
tural missions at Matsapa, Emvembili and Nkungwini. About 80 farmers are
involved and in 1974 production was 316 tons of rice. In the Northern R.D.A.
(Emvembili) 47 farmers farm 2 acres each of rice, followed by vegetables
(mainly tomatoes for canning and consumption). The average income per farmer
is E 1,300 but the best farmers reach E 2,000. IR22 long grain rice is grown.
The problems that have emerged are water control, bird damage and sun crack-
ing. At present the Chinese mission provides machinery for land preparation
and harvesting free of charge. For hulling and polishing there is a fuel
charge only. Mean yields are 6 tons of paddy (unpolished rice) per hectare; 8
tons is considered feasible. On the basis of this successful experience,
irrigated rice production should be considered as an alternative in the
expansion of crop production where water is available.
(viii) Vegetables
25.       Vegetable production has increased rapidly in recent years and
at least 750 hectares were under production in 1975. The bulk of production
is on Swazi Nation Land and is grown under irrigation. Significant potential
exists and some extension staff are available to give advice on irrigation
potential and vegetable production. Most schemes have been small irrigated
plots (20-50 hectares), both within and outside RDA's. Table 12 gives data on
vegetable production. Potatoes are the principal crop and occupy 75% of the



ANNEX 1
Page 9
area under vegetables. Tomatoes, asparagus, strawberries, watermelons, and
green corn are also rapidly expanding. A level of production should shortly
be attained which would permit the substitution of all imports from South
Africa. Problems are poor marketing facilities and the lack of transport.
However, the large urban markets of Johannesburg, Pretoria, and Durban are
accessible to Swaziland and winter vegetables could be sold at these outlets.
Swazi Traditional Farming Methods
26.       Swazi traditional agriculture has two aspects:  cropping, which
is predominantly the women's occupation, and cattle raising which is normally
the men's domain. All agricultural activity, whether it be cropping or
cattle raising, is conducted according to traditional methods and techniques.
Traditional technology is very rudimentary, consisting of the hoe, used
to break the ground and cover the seed (which is often sown broadcast).
This technology is still used in many rural areas. Men have participated
increasingly in crop production with the advent of the ox-drawn plow. The
work of weeding and reaping, however, remains largely in the hands of women.
Swazi farmers often employ magic in the form of medicines mixed with the seed
before planting to give it germinating power; they are also believed to cause
the crop to multiply and thus help to achieve maximum productivity. Magic is
also used to protect crops against insects, thieves and other predators like
birds. Similarly with cattle breeding, medicines are burned in the cattle
kraal, to strengthen the cattle and to protect them against hurtful influences
and machinations of sorcerers. Women are forbidden to enter the cattle kraal
or even to drink milk or eat milk products when they are menstruating because
this might harm cattle. Certain medicines smeared on the rear of cows (com-
pounded with hippotamus fat) are supposed to be aphrodisiacs for bulls.
27.       Irrigation and rotation of crops are techniques which were not
part of the traditional store of agricultural knowledge. Knowledge about
cattle raising is generally greater than about cropping, but in both cases,
traditional methods were followed and little, if any, innovation has taken
place. Modern procedures including use of fertilizers and insecticides,
were often interpreted at first within the traditional framework of magic,
as sorcery. This has tended to discourage the use of the new techniques
because the social sanctions for witchcraft are high. These attitudes are
changing, but cognizance must be taken of such attitudes in extension work.
Demonstration fields may be a useful way to overcome prejudice. Further,
greater attention should be given to training and assisting women, to enlarge
their understanding of agriculture and to provide them with modern techniques
in raising crops, because they are the main agricultural producers.
C. Livestock
28.       Livestock plays a vital role in the Swaziland economy and is
closely integrated within traditional agriculture. Total cattle numbers



ANNEX 1
Page 10
were estimated at 620,000 in 1975; of these, about 520,000 are owned
by about 28,000 Swazi farmers, and grazed on Swazi Nation Land pastures.
The remaining 100,000, generally exotic or upgraded animals, were run
on freehold title ranches, principally in the Lowveld. The present
cattle2stocking density for all Swaziland is estimated at 36 beasts
per km , the highest in Africa. Table 13 shows livestock numbers from 1971-
1975; Table 14 shows the evolution of cattle numbers from 1948-1975. An
estimated 263,000 goats are owned by 14,000 families. Sheep are widely owned,
and virtually every farm keep some poultry, with an average of 10 per farm.
About 20,000 merino sheep trek annually from South Africa to the Highveld for
winter grazing.
29.       Livestock on Swazi Nation Land.  Cattle play a vital role in Swazi
society; by tradition, cattle ownership confers prestige and the number of
cattle owned is seen as being more important than their quality. Cattle are a
source and measure of wealth, and serve a banking function for subsistence
farmers. Work oxen commonly till fields in Swaziland, and are used for
transport; cattle provide meat and milk for the family. Cattle sales are the
principal source of cash income for Swazi Nation Land farmers. As a result of
this keen interest in livestock and their importance for traditional agricul-
ture, combined with the excellent quality of veterinary services in Swaziland,
cattle numbers have increased steadily over the past 20 years. The result is
an ever increasing number of cattle grazing a gradually decreasing area of
land, since more and more arable land is required for food crop production.
30.       The continuing increase in cattle numbers poses a serious and
urgent problem. Pastures are now seriously overstocked, with an estimated 1.8
ha per animal unit (A.U.) compared with a carrying capacity of between 2.5 and
3.0 ha per A.U. Overgrazing is common, soil erosion is occurring in certain
areas, and stock condition is affected at critical times of the year. The
overstocking problem must be viewed as a serious constraint for future live-
stock development, as no development efforts can succeed in face of the
current level of stocking and with present growth trends. Overstocking
has been recognized as a serious threat to development in Swaziland for many
years; it is troubling to observe that despite this recognition and efforts to
reduce herd size, cattle numbers continue to increase without abatement.
31.       Traditional husbandry techniques aggravate the problems of over-
stocking. Pasture land is owned communally and there is no systematic manage-
ment in herding and grazing. Animals graze crop residues within the chiefdom
of their owners in winter, and communal pastures in summer. They are kraaled
at night, cows are milked daily, and animals are seldom if ever fed supple-
ments. Weekly or biweekly visits to the Government dipping tank have been
compulsory for some decades and appear to be virtually universally accepted;
indeed, dip-tanks have become a traditional gathering place and cattle market.
In 1970, 60% of all farms had cattle, and average herd size was 19; only a
small number of farmers (less than 5%) owned more than 50 head of cattle.
Technical coefficients were poor with low calving rates (probably less than



ANNEX 1
Page 11
50%) and slow growth to maturity. Mortality ranges between 4 and 10%, and has
averaged about 6% over the past decade. Malnutrition is probably the principal
cause of high mortality and low fertility. These low coefficients are not
surprising given rudimentary husbandry techniques and the limited importance
attached to commercial-style production of animals for sale as beef.
32.       An important traditional practice in Swaziland is "sisa", whereby
cattle are effectively loaned by one individual to another. The lender
retains his ownership rights and can claim offspring; the borrower has
the full right to utilize the animal for milk or draft.
Commercial Cattle Ranching
33.       On free%old title land, over 100,000 cattle are run on modern-
style ranches, on a strictly commercial basis. CDC holds a substantial
herd in the vicinity of Tshaneni. About 50% of commercial ranch cattle are in
the Lowveld. Availability of pasture land is good, and stocking rates do not
exceed levels recommended as optimal for the country; significant amounts of
pasture land have been improved by bush-clearing and by pasture improvement.
Marketed offtake from freehold title ranches is estimated at 14% a year,
and the quality of animals produced is generally excellent; virtually all
cattle are exotic breeds or improved local stock. Successful ranching has
demonstrated the potential for profitable livestock production in Swaziland on
a technical level.
Animal Health
34.       The animal health situation is good, both on Swazi Nation Land
and on freehold title land. The major African cattle scourges are not
present in Swaziland (East Coast Fever, eliminated since the 1950's, rinder-
pest, bovine pleuroneumonia), while other diseases are systematically and
effectively controlled by a well-organized and well-staffed veterinary ser-
vice. Swaziland's position within Southern Africa explains to a large degree
the Government's success in instituting and carrying out effective disease
control programs. There are no restrictions on exports of beef from Swaziland
on health grounds. The principal disease control technique is dipping; the
country is divided into dip tank areas, and dipping is compulsory every week
in summer, and every second week in winter. There are about 345 Government-
owned dip tanks and sprays, where dipping is free for Swazi Nation Land
cattle, and 340 privately-owned and operated dip tanks and spray races, which
are inspected by the Government. Livestock are inspected at dips and smears
are taken as a routine measure from all dead and slaughtered cattle. Failure
to bring all cattle for dipping results in prosecution, including a stiff
fine: there were 1,200 prosecutions in 1975. Records kept at dip-tanks are
complete and apparently remarkably accurate, providing a good basis for
control of cattle movement and an excellent statistical base for analysis of
the herd. One problem is that resistant strains of ticks have developed, and
the veterinary service is seeking to replace current dipping materials with a
more effective substance that is also biodegradable.



ANNEX 1
Page 12
35.       Foot-and-Mouth disease is a continuing threat to Swaziland because
it is endemic in neighboring Mozambique. Strict precautions have been
established in Eastern Swaziland to control movement of cattle from Mozambique
and adjacent areas. A buffer zone has been created between a cordon fence
along the border and a second cordon fence, 24 km. west of the border. Two
quarantine stations have been established along the second cordon fence, and
all animals entering any part of Swaziland west of the cordon from the buffer
zone must be vaccinated against FMD and are quarantined. Only two outbreaks
of the disease have occurred during this century, one in 1965 and one in
1969.
36.       Other continuing disease problems are brucellosis, which is being
controlled by a new compulsory calf vaccination campaign started in 1974
in Hhohho district, and beef measles (cysticercus bovis): this disease
results in substantial condemnation at slaughter (12% of carcasses were
infected in 1975), and hence economic loss, but disease control must begin
with sanitary conditions of the human population. Blackquarter takes a heavy
toll of young cattle, despite the availability of an effective vaccine.
Parasites are a problem, particularly for young cattle, but control measures
appear to be effective. Tuberculosis is not a serious problem, and only 3
carcasses at the abattoir were found infected in 1975. Anthrax is not a
common disease in Swaziland; two cases were recorded in 1975. Health control
for animals other than cattle is also effective, and most common diseases are
fully controlled, although the orientation of the veterinary service is towards
cattle. Sheep and goats are dipped routinely every year, and vaccination is
effectively carried out. Parasitism, heartwater, and sheep scab and goat
manage are the principal diseases recorded in small stock. For poultry,
Newcastle disease is the most serious problem, and 379,000 birds were vac-
cinated in 1975. Fowl pox is common, but is declining.
37.       The Animal Disease Act of 1933 provides an effective tool for
control of animal disease; the Director of Veterinary Services has wide
powers if disease problems threaten. He can quarantine areas, restrict stock
movement, order vaccination programs, and enforce such regulations. The
result is that Swaziland; regime of animal health services is excellent.
Cattle Breeds and Herd Structure
38.       Cattle are predominantly of the local Nguni breed; they are well
adapted to survival under Swaziland conditions, but are slow to develop
and productivity for dairy or beef is low. Exotic breeds have been success-
fully introduced on freehold title ranches, but little upgrading has been done
on Swazi Nation land to date. The principal breeds introduced to date have
been beef type animals of the Africander, Brahman, Hereford, and Simmentalher
breeds (and crosses); exotic or upgraded animals account for about 7% of the
total cattle population. The herd structure on Swazi Nation Land is charac-
terized by large numbers of old oxen and a high bull-cow ratio (average 1:10
compared with a recommended rate of 1:25). Male animals are seldom castrated
at a young age, and herd structure is characterized also by the high proportion
of old and unproductive females. Table 15 gives a breakdown of herd structure
in 1975.



ANNEX I
Page 13
Pasture
39.       About 1,324,000 ha are classified as grazing land, or 76% of the
total land area of Swaziland; much is of poor quality, and can also be classi-
fied as waste land (para. 11). About 801,580 ha is on Swazi Nation Land, and
522,472 ha on freehold title land; 124,436 ha is reported as improved.
Quality of pasture varies widely, from region to region, and with the level of
stocking. In general, Highveld and Middleveld pastures are of poorer quality
than the Lowveld; grass species are poor, and many pastures are referred to as
'"sour veld", characteristic of poorly drained areas with acid soils; grasses
are generally unpalatable to cattle during winter. Middleveld pastures are
significantly affected by overgrazing, and the consequences are sparse inter-
ceptive canopy, or even bare ground, and much soil erosion. Grasses are
moderately sour with scattered low trees. In the Lowveld, more fertile, less
acid soils support numerous grass species which are highly nutritious, excel-
lent grazing grasses. Panicum, Themeda, Brachiaria and Setaria species are
abundant after the rains. Grasses are generally "sweet" - palatable and
nutritious all year. However, any weakening or destruction of the grasses
upsets the natural balance between them and the trees; overgrazing in the
Lowveld thus results in encroachment by thorny scrub which is difficult to get
rid of. Lubombo pastures have close affinities with the Middleveld, although
there are rare plants here more closely linked with the Zululand coastal
belt.
40.       In general, pastures on freehold title land are well-managed,
stocking rates are close to optimal levels for all regions, and over-grazing
is rare. However, on many freehold title farms, particularly those with
absentee owners, Swazi Nation Land cattle graze freely and pasture deterio-
ration is a problem. On Swazi Nation land pastures, overgrazing is a
serious problem, the result of overstocking and the lack of grazing manage-
ment (para. 38). Optimum stocking rates are estimated at 2.65 ha per A.U.
for the Middleveld and Highveld and 4.0 ha for the Lowveld; the latter figure
is high because of the high level of bush encroachment and the small propor-
tion of arable crops which could provide fodder during the winter. Current
stocking rates are 2.1 ha per A.U. for the Lowveld, 1.33 ha per A.U. for the
Middleveld, and 1.94 ha per A.U. for the Highveld. Overgrazing has resulted in
denudation of pasture in the Highveld and Middleveld and has exacerbated bush
encroachment in the Lowveld.
41.       Efforts to improve pastures, increasing carrying capacities and
raising the nutritive quality of grasses, fall into three categories: (a) bush
clearing; (b) pasture control and management; and (c) pasture improvement.
Bush encroachment has significantly reduced available pasture in the Lowveld
since thick bush prevents cattle from grazing on increasingly wide areas;
efforts have been undertaken to clear bush and control further encroachment.
Experiments recently undertaken under the aegis of the FAO/UNDP Livestock
Project include mechanical, chemical, biological and bush burning methods,
with techniques varying for different types of bush.   The objective is to
determine which methods are most effective and most economic for long-term
control of various types and densities of bush. The most successful method
tried to date on thick bush in the Lowveld zone involves chaining (with large



ANNEX 1
Page 14
tractors) followed by burning. Simple hand-cutting with axe and chain saws
in the higher bush characteristic of the Middleveld (also followed by burning)
has proved satisfactory. Particularly in rural development areas, efforts are
underway to improve pasture management through control of stock numbers and
establishment of rotational grazing. Finally, pasture improvement is being
undertaken, although still largely at an experimental level. New pasture
species are being evaluated at the UBS research station, and field testing is
being carried out. Different pasture establishment methods are being em-
ployed, including sod-seeding, sowing after burning and full cultivation.
Possible establishment of "green belts" of Napier grass, setaria or stylo-
santhes are being considered together with replacement of current grass strips
on arable land (all native species) with improved varieties. Research results
demonstrate that improvement of pasture combined with improved management
could dramatically increase pasture carrying capacity and contribute to
resolution of overstocking problems.
Government Livestock Installations
42.       The Government is currently involved directly in livestock produc-
tion through its veterinary and livestock services, operation of fattening
ranches, breeding stations and livestock extension centers, and research
and training. These activities are described briefly below. Annex 6 describes
the organization of livestock services.
43.       Fattening Ranches.  The Ministry of Agriculture operates two
fattening ranches, and has plans to open two additional ranches in the near
future. These ranches form part of the Government's policy of destocking and
export promotion. Swazi producers are encouraged to send cattle to ranches,
thus reducing pressure on communal pasture; on the ranches, quality of cattle
is substantially improved in a relatively short period through good management
on good pastures. One ranch at Lavumisa was established in 1964 as a holding
ground; the other at Impala has operated since the 1950s. Table 16 gives
details of current and proposed fattening ranches.
44.       The fattening ranches accept all cattle, but priority is now
given to cattle from Swazi Nation Land. All owners pay a fee of EO.65
per month, deducted from the final sale price. In theory, only steers
are accepted at the ranches, with bulls castrated on entry. In practice,
both females and bulls have been kept on the ranches in the past (witness
figures on calves in Table 17).  Owners retain some right to indicate
when they wish to sell animals, but the ranch can sell animals following
a specified number of refusals.  On sending an animal to a fattening
ranch, the owner can now obtain a cash loan for 80% of the value of the
animal from SDSB, receiving the remaining portion plus profit from
weight gain and improvement in grade on final sale. The fattening
ranches have not in the past contributed to resolving overstocking pro-
blems; this is partly because they are not sufficiently well-known among
Swazi farmers, but also because substantial numbers of animals from
freehold title farm have been accepted. Currently, a major effort is



ANNEX 1
Page 15
being made to improve management on the ranches and to promote the idea
of sending animals to a fattening ranch for profit among Swazi farmers.
Table 17 shows the origin and disposition of cattle passing through the
Lavumisa and Impala ranches during 6 months of 1975.
45.       The government also proposes to establish "SISA" ranches to aid in
destock and upgrading the national herd (Table 16). ODM is expected to
finance development, and ranches are to be located on areas purchased under
the ODM land purchase scheme (Annex 2). "SISA" ranches are derived in concept
from the traditional practice of SISA (para 32); farmers would "lend" breeding
females to ranches, which would progressively improve and upgrade stock. The
Livestock Department proposes to use SISA ranches as holding grounds for excess
RDA cattle until the ultimate concept is fully understood and established.
46.       Breeding Stations.  Three breeding stations are now operating,
at Big Bend in the Lowveld, at Mpisi in the Middleveld, and near
Mahlangatsha in the Highveld. The latter station has received assistance
from USAID; the two former stations have benefitted from ODM and FAO/
UNDP assistance. The principal objectives have been experimental,
maintenance of stud herds, and production of improved bulls for sale or
distribution to Swazi farmers. The breeding program at present focuses
on improving the Nguni breed through upgrading with Brahman and Simmentalher
in a three-way cross scheme. Emphasis is placed on keeping detailed perfor-
mance records and evaluating the breeding program as it progresses. Although
breeding programs have been in existence for some time, it is only recently
that a major and systematic breeding program has been instituted, it is too
early to assess results at this stage.
47.       Livestock Extension Centers.  Two livestock extension centers
operate at Mpisi and at Big Bend. The objective was to encourage Swazi
farmers to send cows to the center to be bred to improved bulls.
48.       Livestock Research.  Animal production research is concentrated
the three breeding stations, where research work includes collection and
processing of data. Additional research work is concerned with feeding
trials with sorghum silage, mineral and protein additives suitable for feedlot
fattening, growth promoting supplements, and stocking rate trials to establish
carrying capacity. These trials have been relatively limited to date and have
been centered at fattening and breeding ranches. Bush control experiments
have been conducted, on both breeding and fattening ranches, and UBLS is
conducting pasture research at Malkerns. The Veterinary Department engages in
veterinary research related to disease problems. Up to the present, veterinary
and animal health research has dominated the livestock field, to the detriment
of animal production; on the other hand, Swaziland benefits considerably from
work under similar ecological conditions in the Republic of South Africa.
Milk Production and the Dairy Industry
49.       Milk is an important product of traditional animal husbandry in
Swaziland; cows in milk are normally milked once or twice daily during



ANNEX 1
Page 16
the summer months. Milk is a basic staple of the diet, and is generally
consumed in a soured, curdled form. Only a small proportion of milk pro-
auced is sold for cash, but within rural communities sales of fresh milk
fetch high prices. Annual production is estimated at an average of 350
liters per cow, of which about 150 liters is consumed by the calf. Table 18
shows holdings reporting cows and milking cows.
50.       The commercial dairy industry in Swaziland is small, and supplies
of milk for the urban population are largely provided by imports of liquid
milk from the Republic of South Africa. Fresh milk produced in Swaziland
comes principally from a few freehold title farms, which produced an estimated
660,000 liters of fresh milk in 1973-74, and from traditional producers in the
Lowveld who provide milk to the dairy plant. In 1974-75, freehold title deed
farmers sold 120,000 liters to the Swaziland Dairy Board, and Swazi Lowveld
producers sold 212,500 liters. About 1.1 million liters of fresh milk were
imported. Local production is concentrated in the summer months, November-
May, leaving a large import requirement during the period May to November to
meet local demand for fresh milk. Data on overall milk production indicates
that only about 1% of milk produced in Swaziland and retained for human
consumption is sold through commercial channels. Sales were higher in the
past, and in 1960 Swazi farmers sold the equivalent of 5 million liters of
milk in the form of cream. This was sold to the Republic of South Africa as
butterfat, while skim milk was retained by farmers. Because of low producer
prices, deterioration of collecting facilities, and poor management of the
company concerned, production sales declined drastically, to less than 1
million liters equivalent in 1972. The Swaziland Dairy Board, created in 1971
is responsible for processing, purchasing, and marketing all dairy products in
Swaziland. An ISCDD (International Scheme for the Coordination of Dairy
Development) mission visited Swaziland in 1973 and produced projections
on supply and demand, and on management of the dairy industry. Dairy Board
operations are described in Annex 4.
Livestock in Swaziland: Problems and Potential
51.       The scope for livestock development in Swaziland is considerable;
potential for both expanded beef production (largely for export) and dairy
production (for domestic consumption) is good. Although production oriented
research in Swaziland has been limited, nevertheless commercial farming
has demonstrated successful and economic farming techniques and patterns,
while experience in neighboring areas in South Africa is also relevant for
some parts of Swaziland. The principal problems for livestock development are
overstocking and the need to encourage fundamental changes in traditional
production techniques. These problems are complex and difficult, and arise
from basic social patterns and farmer motivation; they have been recognized
for many years and efforts to overcome them have met with limited success.
Nevertheless, the growing urgency of the problem of overgrazing and increasing
pressure on grazing land as arable area expands offer some grounds for opti-
mism that farmers will respond to development programs. Solutions to the
over-stocking problem are not readily available. A number of measures have



ANNEX 1
Page 17
been attempted since the problem was recognized as such, ranging from pub-
licity campaigns, establishment of holding grounds to remove cattle from
communal pastures, and a compulsory levy of 10% of all herds imposed by the
King in the 1940's for the Lifa fund. Current efforts focus on offering
incentives for farmers to send cattle to fattening ranching by demonstrating
their profitability, and increasing carrying capacity on Swazi Nation Land
pastures by improving herd management (fencing, rotational grazing) and
pasture improvement. Nevertheless, this promises to be a long, slow effort,
with no guarantee of success. Other proposal include imposition of restric-
tions on total herd numbers in a given area, commensurate with its carrying
capacity, and removal of breeding animals and progeny to sisa ranches, making
Swazi Nation land pastures effectively fattening areas for beef. It may be
necessary in the not distant future to schedule areas, fix maximum cattle
therein, and impose sanctions on those who violate the regulations. The
Government is understandably reluctant to impose such restrictive and nega-
tive controls, preferring to rely on chiefs to work out local solutions to the
problem, but recognizes that more generalized control mechanisms may neverthe-
less become inescapable.
D. Fisheries
52.       The potential for fisheries development in Swaziland was largely
ignored until recently. Beginning in 1972, the Ministry of Agriculture
showed interest in developing a fisheries program and within the last two
years, a U.N.D.P. expert assisted by a Peace Corps worker has initiated
a development project which has aroused considerable interest and enthusiasm.
The fisheries program aims at exploring the potential for fisheries in
Swaziland, and is concerned with the promotion of fish ponds, the exploita-
tion of fish in existing dams, and fishing in rivers. The Swazi population,
despite the absence of traditions of fishing or consuming fish, have res-
ponded to initial development efforts, notably fish ponds, far more readily
than anticipated, and fish appears to be giving acceptance as part of the
diet quite rapidly. There is excellent potential for fish pond development.
Many ponds (about 200) are located in Rural Development Areas; they are
stocked with carp and tilapia. The possibility of trout farming is being
explored in the Highveld. Two fish hatcheries are now operating, one situated
in Mbabane and the other in the Highveld. CDC is involved in commercial
fishing at the Sand River Reservoir, and are selling fish at 90c per kilo.
There is some prospect that the Dairy Board may in future become involved in
fish marketing.
E. Agricultural Research
53.       Agricultural research was the direct responsibility of the Ministry
of Agriculture from 1958-1971, and during this period Swaziland which received
substantial aid for research from the Commonwealth Development and Welfare funds.



ANNEX 1
Page 18
In 1971, however, the research division was transferred to the University of
Botswana and Swaziland (UBS), and now forms part of the University. There is
some possibility that the research division may revert to the Ministry
of Agriculture in the near future, but no formal decision has yet been made.
The research division functions under policy guidance from the National
Research Council; an agriculture committee establishes priorities for research
within the Council's broad policy structure. Priorities are translated into
work programs by the research division during planning meetings. The research
budget is voted entirely separately, however, as part of the University's
overall budget process.
54.       The basic objective of the Research division is to produce
practical, economic recommendations on crop and animal production,
which will assist in the economic improvement of the agricultural industry
in Swaziland. In summary, most aspects of crop research have been well
covered by past programs, and future work programs are planned to fill
gaps in existing knowledge. Further work on irrigation and mechanization
is proposed, in addition to specialized subjects such as tobacco and
grain storage. The Agricultural Research Division publishes high quality
advisory bulletins summarizing the division's work; these should serve
as guides for extension workers and farmers.
55.       The research division is based at the University's campus in
the Malkerns valley, near Manzini.  Research stations are operated at
the following locations: the Lowveld Experiment Station, Big Bend,
the Nlangano Experimental Farm, the Hebron Research Plot, the Mangcongco
Research Plot, and the Luve Research Plot. A research plot was operated
at Vuvulane, but was discontinued in August, 1975. A proposal is now
under consideration to establish a further research plot at Mdimba.
56.       Headed by a Chief Research Officer, the research division has
12 sections, which reflect its principal research activities:
Crop Agronomy
Soil Fertility and Crop Nutrition
Horticulture
Pasture and Veld Management
Plant Pathology
General Entomology
Soil Chemistry
Soil Physics
Dryland Crop Agronomy
Biometrics
Cotton Breeding
Cotton Entomology
The chief research officer is also responsible for maintaining close liaison
with two wholly-industry financed units; for some years Government policy has



ANNEX 1
Page 19
encouraged the modern sector of agriculture to assume responsibility for
running its own affairs and meeting its own research requirements. Since
1966, with financial aid from the U.K. and elsewhere, non-government research
units have been set up for pineapple, citrus, forestry and to a limited extent
cotton breeding and entomology.
57.       A brief description of the various research sections follows:
(a) Crop Agronomy: Trials concentrating mainly on variety evaluation
have taken place for many crops. These have been followed by
population, times of planting, fertilizer application, and
herbicide testing trials. From results of this work, general
husbandry recommendations for most regions of the country have
been formulated, including varieties, plant population and
spacing, times of planting, fertilizer, weed control, and
cultivation techniques. Future work will include maintaining
a watching brief on the major crops and developing major exotic
crops in a systematic fashion from introduction through compila-
tion of complete recommendations.
(b) Soil Fertility and Crop Nutrition: The primary objective of the
section remains the definition of crop response related to soil
type and regions, and to major and minor nutrients within
Swaziland. During 1966 a comprehensive district fertilizer
program was initiated, and continued with increasingly extensive
countrywide coverage until 1972/1973. The results of this sub-
stantial exercise remain to be finalized, and should help to
improve fertilizer recommendations. As the district fertilizer
program replaced the need for traditional N.P.K. trials, more
emphasis was placed on time and trace element response. The
work of the section has been restricted in recent years due to
lack of staff.
(c) Horticulture: The section was reestablished in 1971 after a
break of six years. Most previous work was on pineapple and
citrus, which are now run and financed by the industries
concerned. The main objectives concentrate on fresh
market vegetables within the following outline:
(i) The introduction of new varieties of vegetables and
fruits to improve the quality and eventually bring
year round production of fresh vegetables in Swaziland.
(ii) To investigate all aspects of vegetable growing in order
to produce a complete list of recommendations for the various
climatic zones of Swaziland. This information will then be
available for use by farmers and could form the basis for
future work if a particular market or industry were created
for specific crops.



ANNEX 1
Page 20
(iii) To assess the feasibility of growing crops for exports to
Europe.
Recommendations for growing most vegetables in the Middle and
Lowveld are available; the following are fully documented:
Asparagus               Green beans             beetroot
cabbage                 carrot                  cauliflower
eggplant                green peas              lettuce
onion                   peppers                 Swiss chard
pumpkin                 squash                  tomato
melon
Research work on crops for canning continues and includes investigation on
tomatoes, asparagus, youngberry and granadillas. Further work includes a
complete appraisal of subtropical fruit production potential with the
production of advisory bulletins on: (i) Strawberry, youngberry,
granadilla; (ii) Paw-paw, pineapple, banana; (iii) Avocado, guava, litchi;
(iv) Macadamia, pecan; (v) Mango, citrus and (vi) Temperate fruits (apple,
plum, peach, pear, apricot).
(d) Pasture and Veld Management: Pasture research resumed in 1974
with the appointment of a research officer. The new program
is centered on three basic activities:
(i) Plant introduction and testing
(ii) Nutritional studies
(iii) Evolution of management techniques using field
scale trials with livestock.
Much information is available on the first two items and great importance is
now paid to field scale trials. Cooperation with the range management
specialist on the Ministry of Agriculture fattening ranches and also with the
project managers of R.D.A.'s is being maintained so that techniques can be
experimented with on a field scale. The importance of bush encroachment is
recognized and experiments with vigorous legumes as a control are being
considered. Efforts are being made to find a simple system for seeding
undisturbed but possible denuded and eroded pasture without the use of
machinery.
(e) Plant Pathology: The Plant Pathology Research unit has both a
service and a research function. With the imminent appointment
of a replacement research officer the section has several
immediate tasks to perform:
(i) To streamline the routine control and servicing of
phytosanitary regulations including revising the
regulations;
(ii) To provide a checklist of major crop diseases within
Swaziland as a basis for future investigational
and advisory work;



ANNEX 1
Page 21
(iii) To institute trial programmes, where applicable, on crops
already known to require urgent attention, including cotton
(verticillium wilt), field beans (rust), rice (blast),
and a range of vegetable crops.
(f) Entomology: This section was established in early 1976 with
the following objectives:
(i) Compilation of a checklist of all insect pests for the
major and minor crops within Swaziland;
(ii)  The development of a research programme to provide
practical and economic recommendations for the control
of the various insect complexes;
(iii) To maintain and enlarge the existing insect collection.
(g)  Soil Chemistry:  The soil chemistry section has been responsible
for providing backup soil and plant analysis for the Ministry
of Agriculture. The section fulfills the following functions:
(i) continuation of the present cooperative soil and plant
analysis service to other research sections;
(ii)  development of soil and plant analysis techniques to
provide information for more detailed and accurate
fertilizer recommendations;
(iii) determining more closely the interaction between lime
application, soil pH, and availability of major
nutrients on plant growth and productivity;
(iv)  in cooperation with the Soil Fertility Section to assess
the micro-nutrient status of soils in Swaziland;
(v) in conjunction with the Soil Physics Section, to provide
recommendations on reclamation and management of saline
and sodic soils with particular reference to irrigation
schemes.
(h) Soil Physics: The unit was formed in May 1976 for the following
reasons:
(i) The noted structural deterioration of what were previously
regarded as prime agricultural soils;
(ii)  The almost complete lack of data on soil physical properties
(infiltration rates, hydraulic conductivity, water holding
capacity, aggregate size distribution, stability and
porosity) and their relation to dryland and irrigated
crop production practices.



ANNEX I
Page 22
(i)  Dryland Crop Agronomy:  The main objective of this section
will be to develop and describe economic methods of crop
production suitable for the management of raingrown crops.
The emphasis is on the development of farm management
practices and farming systems.
(j) Biometrics: This section is being established and con-
firmation of funding is still awaited.
(k) Cotton Breeding: The unit is assisted by a cotton breeder
under a technical assistance agreement with the U.K.
government. Its objectives are:
(i) To find cotton varieties particularly suited to the
Swaziland environment which will give high yields,
high ginning percentages and lint suitable for the
textile industries of Swaziland and South Africa.
(ii) To test imported varieties and to consider the need to
incorporate into any new Swaziland variety the following
traits:
Tolerance to Verticillium wilt
Resistance to other diseases, including
Bacterial Blight
Resistance to Jassid
Resistance to other pests
Storm resistance
Compact growth habit
Earliness
Reduced seed coat fuzz
High oil and protein content of seed.
(iii) To maintain stocks from many parts of the world which may
have been grown commercially in Swaziland or in trials,
these stocks to include Del Cerro and other long staple
types, which could be grown under irrigation in the
Lowveld if conditions were suitable.
(iv) To maintain rogued stocks of the current commercial
varieties and promote a certified seed scheme for the
commercial production of seed for sale to farmers.
Only the first stage (RR) to be grown on the
Experiment Station, the second and third stages to
be grown on commercial farms.
(1) Cotton Entomology: The work of this section has in the past con-
centrated on providing small scale farmers with the methods and
knowledge to undertake efficient pest control. In summary, the
results of five seasons work are as follows:



ANNEX 1
Page 23
(i) Spraying equipment design was re-assessed and emphasis
placed on the use of the tailboom;
(ii) Insecticides and acaricides were re-appraised and with
carbaryl and dimethoate the continued recommendation
of D.D.T. to the exclusion of a more toxic alternative
was made;
(iii) The problems of small scale farmers in measuring insecticides
in the field were considered. The production of heat-sealed
polythene packs containing sufficient amounts for knapsack-
full and bearing properly printed instructions in English
and in Swazi was organized; these have been on sale for the
last three seasons;
(iv) The timing of spraying in relation to pest infestation and
the economics of pest control on raingrown crops were
examined. Detailed scouting recommendations were issued
including specially printed scouting booklets as aids;
(v) For use by farmers in drier areas the development of methods
of use and of insecticides for a battery powered U.L.V.
atomiser was carried out. A functioning research project
with vehicle and a senior staff member was handed over to
the Ministry of Agriculture before the 1973-74 season to
facilitate the controlled expansion of the technique.
Although all of this work is relevant for the small farmer,
much of it also has significance for the large scale grower.
The correct insecticides to use, the timing of spraying and
scouting and the development of the handheld U.L.V. technique
are all of direct interest to the large grower. Future tasks
include working briefs on pesticides, on insect resistance and
the production of cotton production hand briefs for Swaziland.
In general, there is a need to direct agricultural research efforts
more directly to the needs of small farmers and to establish more clearly
links between extension and research. The transfer of research to the Ministry
of Agriculture should assist in attaining this objective.
F. Soil Conservation
58.       The major erosion problems in Swaziland arise through overgrazing
on the higher slopes. Very little erosion is evident on the arable areas,
although there are some signs of erosion along badly aligned waterways.
Virtually all arable land in Swaziland has grass strips on the contour.
This is the result of an extensive campaign during the 1940s; grass strips
were made compulsory, and were established following an order from the King
(then Paramount Chief). The strips have been maintained in a remarkable
fashion, and appear to have been effective in controlling soil erosion except
where poorly aligned.



ANNEX 1
Page 24
59.       To date there has been a very intensive program of terracing
in the existing rural development areas. These are machine made terraces
involving a sloped contour bund replacing the grass slopes and taking
runoff water into fenced waterways. The advantages of these terraces
are that they help to prevent soil erosion (although there is no real
evidence that they are more efficient than well aligned grass strips).
Secondly, together with the removal of grass strips terracing makes the
task of farm consolidation somewhat easier. The main disadvantage
of the terraces is that the cost has risen to at least E65 per hectare
which means that it would be virtually impossible to carry it out on a
wide scale. Further, machinery and staff are insufficient to meet even
modest targets. Shortages and breakdowns of heavy equipment have
delayed the terracing progress and it has become a major bottleneck in
rural development programs.
60.       The Government and the appraisal mission agreed that under the
proposed Project, terracing works should be substantially modified and
reduced. Full terracing should be undertaken only where steeper slopes
would justify the investment as a soil conservation technique. Elsewhere,
reduced terracing (i.e. every 3 to 4 grass strips) and realignment of
grass strips should be sufficient, and should facilitate maintenance in
future. A full study of the problem of soil conservation and the utility
of various forms of terracing would be very useful, and USAID is likely
to undertake such a study as part of their design of a proposed project
to strengthen the Land Development section. Experience to date seems
sufficient to justify a modified program, given the high costs involved
of continuing with existing policies, particularly since these policies
are not themselves based on reliable basic studies.
61.       Since the principal soil erosion problems arise from over-
grazing on the uplands, emphasis should be placed on livestock develop-
ment programs in future as the most effective way to prevent further
soil erosion and to conserve land resources.
January 13, 1977
