ANNEX 2
Page 1
SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Rural Development Programs
1.        This Annex describes the principal projects and programs aimed at
promoting development of rural areas in Swaziland, as follows:
A.   Historical background of programs.
B.   The ODM Rural Development Areas Program.
C.   UNDP Agricultural Development Projects.
D.   Livestock Development Schemes.
E.   Projects for Development of Credit and Cooperatives.
F.   Land Development.
G.   Other Projects.
Appendix 1 lists the principal on-going and proposed projects related to
rural development.
A. Historical Background of Rural Development Programs
2.        The first significant attempt at promoting development in rural
areas in an integrated fashion in Swaziland was undertaken beginning in 1944,
by an autonomous body, the Native Land Settlement Board. Its principal aim
was to provide land for settlement of 27,000 Swazi people living on European-
owned farms. Two areas were set aside for settlement and development, one
in the northern part of the country, in the vicinity of Herefords, and
the other in the south, in the Hlatikulu District; 55,000 hectares of Crown
Land were released and U.K. grant funds were used to purchase an additional
93,000 hectares of expatriate-owned farm land. 1/ Legislation was then passed
to control grazing, stock numbers, crop rotations, soil conservation, and
rights of occupancy. However, in 1954, the Native Land Settlement Board
(NLSB) was merged with the Department of Land Utilization and this resulted
in a relaxation of controls and a general collapse of the scheme.
1/   For background on land tenure, See Annex 3.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
3.        This program is now regarded as a complete failure.  The principal
problem was that development activities and plans were imposed from above,
by a colonial administration. The Swazi Land Settlement Act (1 February,
1946), gave broad powers to the Permanent Secretary of the Ministry of
Agriculture, and relied on enforced compliance with strict development rules,
with the ultimate sanction for the farmer expulsion from the settlement
scheme. Traditional leaders were not involved. Some settlement areas where
substantial investments were made are now included in Rural Development Areas;
the current level of development reportedly differs little from areas not
affected by the scheme.
4.        A second development program was initiated in 1965-66, shortly
before Independence. Known as a Rural Development Area Program, it was
designed from the start to involve the population, including farmers and
their chiefs, in planning and implementation of schemes. Eight areas,
averaging 4,800 hectares each, were initially chosen, namely Nkwene, Maduleni,
Sipocosini, Mpolonjeni, Mahlangatsha (Mambathweni), Bulandzeni, Bekinkosi and
Sitataweni. Greatest attention was given to improvement of roads, dams,
fences, and drainage systems. In addition, domestic water supplies were
improved, some grazing areas were fenced, and village centers were developed.
A grazing management system was established at Mpolenjeni which included
fencing and control of stock numbers.
5.        Unlike the NLSB schemes, the overriding emphasis in the 1965/6
rural development program was on infrastructure. Little attention was given
to increasing farmer incomes through improvement of dryland crop farming
techniques, and livestock management. Further, the UNICEF/OXFAM vegetable
and egg production program, which occupied most to the efforts of the exten-
sion service at that time, was not directly relevant to the needs of the
majority of farmers. Only at Mpolonjeni was an attempt made to establish a
grazing management system, including fencing and stock control; the principal
reason for the lack of attention to livestock management was the absence of an
animal production or livestock extension sevices. Also the 1965/6 Rural
Development Program lacked integration, and the individual areas were too
small to justify stationing professional management staff at the area centers.
Thus, the impact of the program was superficial and of a temporary nature, in
that developments lapsed when support was withdrawn, while even for infra-
stucture, no proper system for maintenance was left in place. Here too,
differences between areas included in and excluded from the development
program are now barely perceptible.
6.        In 1968, shortly before Independence, the U.K., in consultation with
the provisional Swaziland Government, appointed a commission to study the
land question as it related to economic and agricultural development of Swazi
farmers (Annex 3). The Hobbs Commission published its confidential report on
land distribution and utilization in 1969, and its recommendations formed the
basis for a U.K. land purchase program. Major considerations behind the
program were the alarming level of overgrazing on Swazi Nation Land and the



ANNEX 2
Page 3
need to rationalize land use, particularly in heavily populated areas.
Thus the aim of the program was to buy freehold title land adjacent to Swazi
Nation Land, and use it for resettlement and destocking. About t sterling 1.5
million in grant funds were made available for this purpose. To date, about
E 500-600,000 have been disbursed, and other purchases are the subject of
negotiations. Many problems have been encountered in utilizing purchase
areas in an appropriate and productive manner, and in identifying suitable
farms whose owners were willing to sell, but those appear close to resolution.
No purchase area has yet been settled, although some areas are being used
as breeding stations or as fattening ranches. A list of purchase areas and
their utilization is given in Table 1.
B. The Rural Development Areas Program (ODM Scheme)
7.        In 1970, a new Rural Development Area Program was initiated, sup-
ported by the U.K. Overseas Development Ministry (ODM). The scheme is now
underway and is being expanded. It provides the basis for the proposed
Project. The land purchase program provided part of the stimulus for the new
program, which utilized some elements of the 1966 program described above
(para 5). The scheme is perceived as a long-range program to promote agri-
cultural development in rural areas and to bring about improvements in the
living standards of the Swazi farmer. Development is organized in Rural
Development Areas (RDA's), which are coherent areas of Swazi Nation Land
selected, principally by the Ministry of Agriculture, for their agricultural
potential, the interest of the local population in development, ecological
homogeneity, and size of population (Annex 8, Part A).
8.        The ODM-financed project is concentrated in four RDA's. The principal
investments financed by ODM are:
(a)  Project management staff, including an RDA coordinator
in the Ministry of Agriculture and a Project Manager
responsible for development activities in each RDA.
Other staff at present are drawn from other sectors
of the Ministry of Agriculture and other concerned
industries as appropriate;
(b) Civil works, notably construction of project centers
with offices, depots for inputs, and housing for
key staff in each RDA, and
(c) Equipment and vehicles.
Other development progams have been closely affiliated with RDA activities,
with activities coordinated principally by RDA staff. Most significant are
land development and soil conservation activities, undertaken with USAID-
financed equipment, provision of inputs in conjunction with the Central



ANNEX 2
Page 4
Cooperative Union (CCU) with SDSB as banker, and livestock development in the
context of the FAO/UNDP livestock project (see below). In other words, the
project has provided key staff and a framework for planning and organizing
a range of key services in an intensive manner, in a few selected areas.
9.        Development in all four ODM-financed RDA's is well underway.
The crop extension effort in all four areas is concentrated specifically
on increased production of maize, cotton, tobacco, beans, vegetables and
rice. This is to be achieved through a more intensive and better supervised
extension service and by making inputs available to farmers. Livestock
extension is primarily aimed at increasing herd offtake and in setting up
management systems for the communal grazing areas. Other components, such as
construction of dipping tanks, road improvements, input supply and tractor
hire services, are implemented and co-ordinated in the field by professional
project management staff and associated extension officers. Important program
objectives are consolidation of land holdings, principally to facilitate
orderly development of crops and livestock, and soil conservation through
terracing. Social components, such as clinics and schools were not specifi-
cally included in the project proposals; however, the different Ministries
responsible have been involved in such developments where and when it has been
possible.
10.       The position of each area may be summarized as follows:
(a)  Northern RDA:  Located on the northwest frontier
of Swaziland, development was initiated here first
in 1971. The RDA covers 21,214 hectares (including
adjacent purchase areas), and has a population of
14,700. About 750 cattle owners hold 12,000
livestock units. Progress was rapid at first but
encountered problems in 1973 when the Central Rural
Development Board (CRDB) held up development in two
areas for some time. The response of farmers to
provision of inputs through cooperatives has been
particularly encouraging. Livestock development
has begun very slowly, but there has been some response
to efforts to encourage removal of cattle, at least
on a temporary basis, and perimeter fencing and paddock
fencing has begun. The principal obstacles have been
resistance by chiefs to irrigation schemes, fencing
and farm consolidation in part of the area, and delays
in financing due principally to lack of equipment and
high rates of breakdown. There are seven registered
cooperative societies with a total membership of 272 (end
1975). Works are generally behind target, notably for
financing and fencing, but nevertheless, progress is
steady despite delays and difficulties.
(b) Central RDA: Located just north of the Mbanane-Manzini
Road, the area is within the Mtilane Valley, and comprises



ANNEX 2
Page 5
five chiefdoms. Total area is 8,900 hectares plus
10,100 hectares now being purchased under the ODM Scheme.
Population is 8,200, and the area has 8,053 cattle plus
small stock. Development began in 1974. Principal
activities to date have been construction of the project
center, including a CCU input shed, road development,
fencing, and development of irrigation schemes. Livestock
development has not yet begun.
(c) Southern RDA: Located north of Nhlangano in Shiselweni
district, the area covers 8,906 hectares, with a popula-
tion of 4,080, and 4,170 livestock units. Development began
in 1974 and has focused on development of the Project center,
roads, and land consolidation, terracing, and irrigation develop-
ment. Delays have occurred particularly in the terracing
program, which has delayed land consolidation, and in
construction, principally because of transportation
problems. Livestock development activities have not yet
begun.
(d) Mahlangatsha RDA: Located in south western Swaziland in
the Highveld, the area covers 22,500 hectares, including
purchase areas, and has a population of about 5,000; there
are an estimated 6,000 livestock units. Development began
in 1974. The project center has been established, and
offices, a cooperative center, tractor pool, and workshops
are all functioning. Terracing and other land development
works have been delayed by equipment shortages.
Physical development targets and achievements for all RDA's are shown in
Table 2. Table 3 shows sales of inputs in RDA's in recent years.
11.       The experience in RDA's to date is encouraging.  In particular,
the response of the population and of most traditional leaders to changes
instituted under the program augur well for the future. Misunderstandings
with the CRDB appear to have been resolved, and the King and Chiefs reportedly
view the program with favor; during the appraisal mission's stay in Swaziland,
the Prime Minister, the Chairman of the CRDB, and the Secretary to the
Cabinet accompanied an ODM-mission on a visit to Mahlangatsha RDA and
reportedly reaffirmed the Government's full commitment to the concept and
design of the scheme. Neighboring chiefs are said to be anxious that
their areas be accorded RDA status in order to benefit from improved
infrastructure and services. Quantitative evaluation of progress is,
however, problematic. Data on changes in RDA farming patterns and gross
production since inception of the program is-not available. Increased
sales of inputs give some indication of progress, and extension workers
report marked increases in area under cash crops. A survey is now underway
to assemble information on the southern RDA, but more systematic statistical
surveys are also required.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
12.       The principal problems encountered by the RDA program to date
have been identified as follows:
(a) lack of continuing liaison on policy and implementation
between the Ministry of Agriculture and the Central Rural
Development Board;
(b) slow progress in developing a crop marketing system
designed to serve the small farmer;
(c) lack of a clear and effective program to address the
livestock production/overstocking problem;
(d) slow development of rural institutions capable of
maintaining infrastructure, vehicles and tractors;
(e) Logistic problems, including poor maintenance of vehicles
and equipment by the Central Transport Organization (CTO)
and problems with equipment shortages and breakdowns by the
land development units;
(f) Staff shortages, at field extension worker and project
manager level; during appraisal only two out of four project
manager posts were filled.
13.       ODM and the Swazi Government intend to continue and expand the
ongoing program in four RDA's. These activities would be a separate project
from the project proposed for IBRD financing, but the two would be administered
jointly by a single unit within the Ministry of Agriculture. ODM has committed
h 3 million to the RDA program over the three year period 1977-79.
C.  UNDP Agricultural Development Projects
14.       FAO/UNDP have supported two ambitious and wide-reaching projects:
a crop development project, which provided technical assistance for a number
of specialists in different fields, and a livestock development project
aimed at developing an animal production service, through technical assistance
and some direct investment in a breeding program.  Both projects have been
seriously hindered by recent cutbacks in UNDP funding, and a number of key
staff have departed.
15.       Crop Development Project:  The project, initially approved for
1973-75, followed by an extension 1976-78, was to provide a project manager
and specialists in various crops, notably cotton, tobacco, horticulture and
grains and legumes.  The objective was to develop an effective nucleus of



ANNEX 2
Page 7
qualified specialists within the Ministry of Agriculture, to strengthen
and direct the overall extension effort. The project is closely affiliated
with technical assistance provided by other donors, notably ODM (soil testing,
grain storage) and the US Peace Corps. The major focus is on the small
farmer. Objectives were defined as follows:
(a) Development of extension services through training
and technical assistance.
(b) Farmer training, including demonstrations.
(c) Assistance to research, and in transmitting research
results to the farming community; and
(d) Assisting farmers in obtaining credit and in developing
market outlets.
The project has encountered serious problems. Most significant, recent UNDP
financial cutbacks have obliged UNDP to terminate all but two experts.
FurtherItwo Project Managers were obliged to depart, primarily for other
reasons. ODM has agreed to provide some of the key technical assistance posts
originally included in the project.
16.       Livestock Development Project:  The FAO-UNDP Livestock Development
Project was initiated in 1975; UNDP initially committed US$602,858, which
was subsequently increased by US$460,934. The project's objective is to
increase productivity of the national herd through development of extension
services, development of breeding stations designed to upgrade the national
herd, and improvement and expansion of fattening ranches to assist in de-
stocking Swazi Nation Land pastures. Assistance in marketing of cattle was
also included. Initially, five experts were provided: a project manager,
and specialists in beef production, dairy, range management, and livestock
extension. The project also included associate experts and volunteers. The
project was severely affected by recent UNDP cutbacks, and all four specialist
posts were dropped, although most will probably be filled through bilateral
assistance. The project manager works directly with the Department of Vete-
rinary Services of the Ministry of Agriculture and the head of the animal
production division is co-manager of the project. Thus, despite the effective
dismantling of the project per se, the basic program objectives continue
as the core of the Ministry's Livestock Development Program.
D. Other Livestock Projects
17.       ODM has provided assistance for development of fattening ranches,
breeding stations, and SISA ranches. Most are located on former freehold
title farms purchased under the ODM land purchase scheme. These livestock
installations are described in Annex 1; ODM Land purchases are summarized in
Annex 3.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
18.       Dairy Development:  The Canadian International Development Agency
(CIDA) has agreed to financing a dairy development project, including con-
struction of a new dairy plant, development of milk collection and cooling
facilities, and technical assistance. That cost is estimated at Canadian
$5 million.
19.       USAID has provided assistance for development of a breeding ranch
in the Highveld.
E. Credit and Cooperative Development
20.       The principal schemes for development of credit involve assistance
to the Swaziland Development and Savings Bank (SDSB). ODM and USAID assisted
in establishing the Bank; USAID has made a stand-by long term loan of US$750,000
for small farmer credit. USAID has also provided technical assistance to
SDSB.
21.       Several donor agencies are providing assistance for cooperative
development. ILO/SIDA have a US$1 million cooperative development scheme
including development of a cooperative training and development center
near Mbanane and technical assistance, with six experts. USAID has recently
approved a US$2.7 million loan for cooperative marketing development,
involving provision of 10 experts to CCU: ODM has agreed to provide a general
manager for CCU, in addition to a loan of E 500,000 for working capital.
F. Land Development
22.       USAID has assisted in developing the Land Development Section
(formely soil conservation unit of the Ministry of Agriclture) through pro-
vision of equipment, development of workshops, and technical assistance.
A new loan of about US$4 million and grant of about US$3 million are under
consideration to further expand and develop the section.
G. Other Projects and Schemes
23.       Other rural-oriented projects and schemes are briefly summarized
below and in Appendix 1; the intention is to indicate the range and type of
donor-financed activities underway and neither list purports to be complete.
(a) Fisheries Development: UNDP has provided a fisheries
expert to explore prospects for fish-pond development.
(b) Vuvulane scheme. Concurrently with the establishment
of the Mhlume sugar mill and the production of sugar



ANNEX 2
Page 9
cane for the mill, the Commonwealth Development Corporation
(CDC) in 1959 began a program of trials with Swazi
farmers growing irrigated crops of sugar cane, cotton,
maize, and vegetables in the northern Lowveld. As a result
of these trials three types of holdings were established
under the Vuvulane irrigated Farms Company (VIF):
10 farms of 41 hectares each for people of all races.
5 farms of 20 hectares each for Swazi farmers.
60 small holdings of 3-1/4 hectares each for Swazi farmers.
Since then, the 10 large farms have been reduced to three and are no longer
part of V.I.F., the five 20 hectare farms have flourished and with one excep-
tion the freehold farms have been sold to the Swazi farmers. There are now
over 200 small holdings of 3-1/4-6-1/2 hectares in size farmed by Swazi
farmers on 20 year leases; a total of 1,200 hectares of irrigated land are
involved. By 1978 at full development of this phase 1,625 hectares will be
under irrigation. The project has met with considerable agricultural success,
but has encountered politial and social problems.
(c) The Republic of China has supported two rice schemes in central
and northern Swaziland, which have met considerable agricul-
tural success;
(d) ODM is financing a grain storage project to improve farm storage
of maize and other grains by Swazi farmers; Danish assistance is
financing a seed multiplication scheme;
(e) Ingwavuma Basin Study: The African Development Bank is financing
a feasibility study for rural development, including irrigated
farming, in the Ingwavuma Basin. The feasibility study was in
progress during appraisal, and the consultants expect to prepare
indicative development plans before the end of 1976;
(f) Agricultural Training: FAO/SIDA are financing 14 experts to
staff the agricultural department at UBLS. ODM has financed farmer
training center development. SIDA is financing animal health
training.
(g) A training scheme to upgrade domestic science demonstrators
is underway; UNDP provided an expert (since departed) and
an associate expert; SIDA is assisting also.
(h) ODM is financing a full orthophoto-mapping program covering all
Rural Development Areas in Swaziland; these will provide the basis
for future land use planning.
(i) Status of Women: The UN has initiated a project aimed at improving
the status of women.



ANNEX 2
Page 10
(j) A third major sugar scheme is under consideration, which
would be Tibi Yo Fund financed and might include IFC participation.
(k) Forestry development schemes in the Highveld are under
consideration.
(1) A rural water supply scheme is being considered.
(m) ODM has financed equipment for a district road development
project.
(n) UNDP and USAID are supporting a rural literacy scheme
and primary school curriculum development. The UK is
assisting with farm surveys.
(o) Rural health schemes include ODM assistance for construction
of clinics, UNFPA for mother and child care and family planning,
and USAID assistance for a pilot bilharzia control scheme.
(p) Technical assistance is provided by many donors, including
the U.K., USAID, Peace Corps, UNDP and SIDA.
September 14, 1976

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Land Tenure and Sociological Background
A. Land Tenure
Classification of Land Tenure Systems.
1.        Two quite separate categories of land tenure are recognized in
Swaziland under Swazi Law:
(a) Swazi Nation Land; and
(b) Freehold Title Land (also designated Title Deed Land and
Individual Tenure Land).
This distinction has evolved since the colonial era in Swaziland. It results
from a series of events which designated part of the land area as subject
to modern land law, with full, European-style freehold title rights, and
with ownership predominantly non-Swazi (Freehold Title Land), while the
remaining land area remained within the traditional structure of customary
law, with land rights governed according to traditional patterns and authority
(Swazi Nation land). The significance of this distinction for current develop-
ment programs is substantial, both because of the economic gap that has
emerged between the modern, non-Swazi production on freehold title land,
contrasted with the persisting traditional way of life and production on Swazi
Nation Land, and because Government and law differ substantially for the two
types of land. It will be noted from the map that land of the two main
categories is scattered alternately throughout the country in all four eco-
logical zones.
2.        Classification of land tenure should also include land covered by
various different regimes, including urban land within gazetted townships,
which is effectively freehold title land, Tibiyo Fund land, which belongs to
the Swazi Nation but is often managed on a commercial basis, under the autho-
rity of the King and Swazi National Council, and Crown land and Lifa land
(purchased with proceeds of a levy of 10% of Swazi cattle by the King),
categories which still cover some land but which effectively are hangovers
from the colonial era. The King owns substantial land areas directly.
Further, the British Government has financed a scheme to purchase freehold
title land for the Swazi Nation; the legal status of this land is not yet
entirely clear, as some parcels may become indistinguishable from other com-
munally held Swazi Nation Land, while others may be designated as government
land.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
3.        The land area of Swaziland is divided among the different land
tenure categories roughly as follows:
ha               %
Swazi Nation Land                       984,500           57
Of which:
Tibiyo Fund, King's
land, Government
stations, etc.             184,500           11
Land held or grazed by
Swazi farmers.             800,000           46
Freehold Title Land                     751,900           43
Of which:
Urban Land                   9,300             1
Freehold Title Farms
and Estates                742,600           42
Total Land Area                   1,736,400          100
4.        Areas defined now (before the Project) as Rural Development areas
(RDAs) cover-about 63,700 ha. or about 8% of land held or grazed by Swazi
farmers, or 6% of total Swazi Nation Land. Included in the totals for Swazi
Nation Land are an estimated 68,500 ha. purchased with ODM grant funds under
the ODM land purchase scheme; some purchases have not been yet formalized
(Annex 2, Table 1). These areas are to be used as Government stations,
fattening or SISA ranches, or extensions to RDA's, to be used for farming or
grazing by Swazi farmers.
5.        It should be noted that different statistics appear on division
of Swazi Nation Land and Freehold Title Land; the principal source of dis-
crepancy appears to be inclusion or exclusion of recently purchased areas
and the classification applied to government land.
Swazi Nation Land - General Description
6.        Swazi Nation Land is owned communally by the Swazi people, but is
vested in the King who holds it in trust for the nation; in fact, the King
holds the official title to all Swazi Nation Land. Holdings are allocated
to individuals by the traditional chiefs acting on behalf of the King and
under guidance of the Swazi National Council. Holdings are allocated for
farming plots and homesites; all other land is held communally rather than
individually, and may be used equally by all members of the community. It



ANNEX 3
Page 3
should be noted that the wide powers of the King and his chiefs are based
largely on control of land. This control over land gives the Swazi National
Council, (which advises the King particularly on land matters, and which is
the citadel of tradionalism in Swaziland) so much social and political power
that it could, before the King's dissolution of Parliament, even veto the
legislation passed by Parliament if that legislation had to do with land use
on Swazi Nation Land.
7.        The right to use land and other resources on Swazi Nation Land
is confined to members of the Swazi Nation. Other conditions are that the
individual must offer allegiance to the area Chief and that the Chief concerned
accepts that person as his subject. The majority of people settled in Swazi
Nation Land areas have in fact inherited their rights to the use of land and
other resources. These rights can be broadly classified as: rights to a
homestead site and arable lands, and rights to the use of communal resources
such as pasture land, water, woodland, etc. Thus, any Swazi settled on Swazi
Nation Land is subject to traditional authority; this is not the case for
Swazis who live on freehold title land or in urban areas.
8.        Once a residential site has been occupied and arable lands culti-
vated, the ownership rights of the homestead group are strengthened. The
area allocted, however, remains the property of the Nation and the user has
no right to sell the land. Ownership rights may be diminished through failure
to use the land and are dependent on continued membership of the community.
Any individual, whether a newcomer to a Chiefdom or an established inhabitant,
may acquire rights over arable land in any one of four different ways:
- By direct grant from the Chief.
- By direct grant from an individual within the Chiefdom (who
may grant the right to use land but not the right to settle).
- By loan from an individual within the Chiefdom (in which
case the land remains vested in the lender).
-  By inheritance (although in theory the family must make a
new request for the land).
Although no one can buy or sell land, while land is under crops the farmer's
exclusive rights to its use cannot be disturbed. In practice, individuals
are very seldom deprived of their land, but the lack of real security of
tenure is thought to have acted as a constraint to investment in land develop-
ment (fencing, planting trees) by the traditional community.
9.        Rights to the use of communal resources, such as pasture land,
woodland and water, are held communally and not exclusively. An individual is
not confined to any particular area - for example, all Swazis are theoreti-
cally permitted to use grazing land anywhere in the country; in practice, each
individual restricts his activities to areas accessible to the homestead.
After harvest, plowed land with standing crop residues reverts back to com-
munal use and the community grazes stock without hindrance. This has in the
past impeded fencing of arable land except around the homestead. Communal
ownership of grazing lands has contributed significantly to the present



ANNEX 3
Page 4
problem of overstocking in the rural areas - for the individual, grazing area
appears to be unlimited but for the group as a whole, the stocking rate is one
of the highest in the world. Further, as in other traditional systems where
pastures are communally owned, the individual's incentive to improve pasture
is limited since all would share equally in the benefits of his investment.
10.       Within the homestead group, the family head has the authority to
allocate his allotted area of arable land to various recognized sub-groups,
termed "houses". A house may consist of a wife and children in a polygamous
homestead, a married son or brother or various other kin groupings. Land
allocated to the various houses is retained and after death of the homestead
head each house will tend to establish a homestead of its own. This process
leads to arable land fragmentation - a problem which is likely to become more
serious in the future since the population growth rate is at present over 3%
a year, and since there is limited available arable land for expansion.
11.       In 1970, there were an estimated 38,800 individual holdings (arable
land and homesites), covering about 117,000 ha. This number has certainly
increased since then with population growth. The majority of individual
holdings (61%) range in sizes from 1 to 5 ha, while only about 12% are of
5 ha or greater. The country average for a family holding is about 3.0 ha;
holdings tend to be larger in the Lowveld (average 4.5 ha), where rainfall
is low, and smaller in the Highveld (average 2.1 ha). Tables 2 and 3 shows
the pattern of holdings of Swazi Nation Land.
12.       Administration of Swazi Nation Land.  Swaziland's dual system of
Government affects Government and particularly development projects located
on Swazi Nation Land. While the Ministry of Finance has primary responsibility
for designing and selecting development projects, the Swazi National Council
(SNC), through the Central Rural Development Board (CRDB), must finally decide
which projects are to be approved. Thus all projects and proposals must go
through two different types of approval prior to implementation. This problem
is compounded since the decision makers of the SNC require more time and
eaploy different criteria than the modern Government in making decisions.
Thus, there is generally a long time lag between the initiation and the
implementation of development projects. This duality of approvals also means
that replication of successful projects is not necessarily automatic since
each project is judged on by the SNC on its own merits. It is significant
that the system is democratic in the sense that, in theory, each interested
Swazi voice is heard prior to decision; in practice, however, the most important
voices are those of the aristocracy.
13.       Although the decision criteria of the SNC are not always known
to outsiders, the goal of the SNC has been stated many times by the King as
being the economic and human development of the Swazi Nation. The advantage
of the system is that it assures that each project approved has received the
full support of the Swazi people as well as the Swazi Government, and that, in
the view of traditional lenders, the proposed changes are within the bounds of
the absorptive capacity of the traditional Swazi culture. In practice, the
system makes it possibile for a local chief to halt or seriously hinder
a project if he feels it causes too much disruption in his area; it is there-
fore esssential that local chiefs be involved at an early stage in the plans
affecting their areas.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
14.       The King alone has the right to alienate Swazi land and allocate
it for development purposes, and he has done so in the past. He can decree
that certain practices are permissible and the old ones disallowed, and he can
(as he has done) give leadership to modernization. So far, he has tended to
use these powers cautiously and sparingly, but he has nevertheless expressed
his full and unequivocal support for the basic concept of rural development
programs now underway.
Freehold Title Land
15.       The origins of the current dual system of land tenure can be traced
the nineteenth century; Swazi rulers granted vast land concessions to foreigners,
often in exchange for very small sums of money or gifts. Some concessions
were purchased outright by concessionaires while others were held subject to
payment of an annual rental to the Swazi authorities. A commission was
appointed by the British Government in 1907 to investigate problems relating
to the concessions granted, since objections had been raised by the Swazis
The commission concluded that the vast majority had been granted in perpetuity
and recommended that in future they should be given the same status as a 99
1/3 year lease under the Roman-Dutch law of the Territory.   They also deter-
mined that about one half of the total land of Swaziland belonged to the Swazi
people and delineated those areas. The same Commission recommended that
holders of concessions which had been validated by the commission authorities
be permitted to convert these to freehold title on application. Subsequent
attempts by Swaziland to invalidate these actions were overruled by Great
Britain; most recently by the House of Lords in 1920.
16.       Most land concessions were converted to freehold title and con-
cessionaires retained the right to do this until 1973. An order in Council
in that year declared all land still held under original concession title
or lease was to remain so held "at the will and pleasure of the King on
such terms J he may determine". This has been interpreted to imply that
once the 99 1/3 year lease has expired, land will revert to ownership by
the Swazi Nation.
17.       The rights of a freeholder in Swaziland are similar to those in
European countries (in which modern Swazi Land Law is modelled). Until 1972
any individual (citizen or non-citizen) could buy and sell land free from
restriction. In 1972, a Land Control Act was instituted which stipulated that
any non-citizen wishing to purchase land must obtain permission from the Land
Control Board. If the applicant's proposals for using the land are deemed to
be in the interests of the country, permission may be granted provided that
these proposals are carried out. A non-citizen owning land in Swaziland
remains free to will land to his (or her) decendants, and citizens are still
-permitted to buy and sell freehold title land without restriction. One effect
of this action has been to reduce very substantially the market value of land
in Swaziland, which is said to be worth as little as one-tenth the value of
comparable land in South Africa.
18.       Freehold Title Land occupies about 752,000 ha or 43X of the total
land area of Swaziland. The land is scattered throughout the country in
about 790 separate holdings. This includes about 54,000 ha of crop land,



ANNEX 3
Page 6
99,000 of commercial forests, and about 554,000 of pasture land. The bal-
ance is mining concessions, unusable land, or urban land. An estimated
40% of all Freehold Title land is used for winter grazing for sheep from
tne Republic of South Africa. Crop land is divided over about 390 farms,
averaging 140 ha each. Table I illustrates the character of Freehold
Title Land holdings.
19.       An estimated 30% of freehold land is considered underutilized
and could be used readily by Swazi farmers. Some of this land is being
repurchased under a grant from the United Kingdom (Annex 2) for use as grazing
land and other purposes in selected development areas. The SNC has recently
stated that all freehold title land sales are now subject to the SNC approval.
This establishes an institutional framework which allows the repurchase of
the freehold title land but at a pace slow enough not to adversely affect the
commercial agricultural sector.
20.       In recent years, a problem has arisen affecting Freehold Title Land.
An estimated 15,000 Swazi households farm on Freehold Title Land, some as
tenant farmers or employees ("farm dwellers") others simply as "squatters"
when they have settled without the permission of the owner. A Board has been
established to deal with "farm dweller" disputes; it appears that the farm
dwellers do have recognized rights to continue farming unutilized land or at
least to some compensation.
Hobbs Commission Report
21.       Land tenure issues have been highly controversial for Swaziland,
given foreign ownership of almost half the land in the country. Rights to
land have been disputed throughout this century, and the Swazi Nation has
made efforts to obtain the return of the land to the Swazi Nation, often by
repurchasing it. An active concern has been to reclaim land without diminishing
its productivity, a problem since Swaziland depends very heavily on agricultural
production on freehold title land. In 1969, shortly after independence, a
commission including representatives selected by the British and Swazi Govern-
ments examined the issue of land tenure in Swaziland in an effort to resolve
the problem without raising the historical disputes. The Hobbs Commission
Report which resulted from this Commission's review, made recommendations on
land policy, notably in designing a project for UK-funding which would repur-
chase underutilized freehold title. The mission's terms of reference specified
that it was the Swazi Government policy that, in the main, only underdeveloped
land should be acquired, and that land questions should be examined in economic
(rather than political) terms. The committee recommended that about 3,900,000
hectares of freehold land, assumed to be underutilized, be purchased for
specific development purposes.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
B. Sociological Background
Population of Swaziland
22.       In 1966 (the most recent census), the population of Swaziland stood at
395,139, comprising 381,586 Africans, 9,157 "whites," and 4,395 other groups,
mostly "colored" and foreign Africans. The projected 1975 population was
471,500 people. The rural population clusters heavily on Swazi nation land,
with 260,554 people on 9,096.9 sq. km (1966). This was divided among the main
districts in the following rank order: Manzini, with 2,037.3 sq. km. had
72,064 people; Shiselweni with 73,388 people on 1,966.8 sq. km.; Hhohho with
61,825 people on 1,836.1 sq. km.; and Lubombo with 51,277 people on 3,256.2
sq. km. The European population on Swazi nation land is very small.
23.       On freehold title farms, Lubombo district had the largest popula-
tion in 1966 with 28,676 people on 2,683 sq. km. followed by Manzini with
19,957 people on 2,009.1 sq. km.; Shiselweni and Hhohho had 18,753 people on
1,794.1 sq. km. and 18,357 people on 1,688.1 sq. km. respectively.
24.       The most significant concentration of the urban population is in the
two towns of Mbabane and Manzini with 15,220 and 6,668 respectively (in 1966).
The total urban population in 1966 was 40,817 or 10.3% of the total population
of the country.
Social System
25.       Social Structure.  The social order of Swaziland is predominantly
traditional. Undergirding individual actions is the a traditionalism ensuring
that social actions are determined by what seems to be required by duty,
honor, personal loyalty and proper standards of right and wrong; this value-
orientation can act as a kind of social imperative. This social order derives
from the sacredness of tradition whose validity is reinforced socially and
psychologically by fears of the anger of the ancestral spirits which bring
with it magical evils if tradition has been transgressed.
26.       The living symbol of all Swazi traditions and of the unity of the
nation is the King (Ngwenyama) who has divine credentials of authority
which give unquestioned legitamacy to whatever he does or says. King Sobhuza
II has reigned as Paramount Chief and as king for over 50 years. The social
hierarchies which derive from this structure are first the King and the Queen
Mother, and the Royal household. Second in the social hierarchy are members
of the Aristocracy; after the royal household and the aristocracy come the
commoners.
27.       The basic units of social organization are families which are,
ideally, polygamous. For a marriage to take place, the groom's family must give
"lobolo" (bride wealth) to the bride's family. This is in the form of cattle
- normally eleven, unless the woman is of royal or aristocratic birth in which



ANNEX 3
Page 8
case the "lobolo" is much higher, depending on the social status of her
family. The social institution of "lobolo" gives cattle a very high social
value, for they decide the wife-getting abilities of a man and therefore the
founding of families. A number of related families form kinship groups.
These are descendents of one known founding ancestor, and are often four
generations in depth. Kin groups form extended families and are corporate in
character, that is, they hold property such as cattle and land in common;
groups share religious rituals because they have common ancestral spirits.
Kin groups also have a common titular leader who is the most senior man in the
extended family. They are exogamous, i.e., men from one kin group can only
marry women from another kin group. Several kin groups form clans, which are
families who believe themselves to be descended from a common mythical ances-
tor. Clans are dispersed and do not have a corporate character. The dif-
ferent clans form the Swazi nation, and all owe allegiance to the King.
28.       Traditional Political Authority.  The traditional political system
rests on the social structure described above. The King is the supreme ruler
and all the people owe fealty to him. He is the symbol of the unity of the
nation and is its religious leader. The King rules the land through chiefs,
who derive all authority from the King. The chiefs serve the function of
district administrative officers; no chief in Swaziland is an independent
potentate, as all are subject to the King.
29.       Social Change.  Despite the strength of traditional institutions and
authority in Swaziland, the pace of social change has quickened in recent
years. The growth of the modern farms and industries have had some effect on
neighboring Swazi farmers, as have the spread of Christianity and education.
Most Swazis remain outside the factory system or even outside the rapidly
growing towns of Manzini, Mbabane and other smaller towns, but new socio-
economic groups have been created by the mining industry in neighboring South
Africa, by the large sugar estates in Swaziland, and by growing industrial
development in the country. These groups depend mainly on earned incomes for
their livelihood although generally they still practice some agriculture,
particularly cattle keeping and subsistence farming. There is a growing
system of market agriculture which, in some rural areas, has replaced the
traditional system of local subsistence production. In the south, for example,
tobacco and cotton have become important cash crops. This growing trend
toward market agriculture, especially with respect to tobacco and cotton, is
due largely to new market opportunities. Similarly, a growing interest in
cash crop production of maize, vegetables, potatoes, and beans for the growing
number of urban consumers has stimulated new social and cultural developments
in the country.
30.       Market agriculture raises new problems for the social structure and
much more acutely for the traditional land tenure system. For example, the
concentration on cash crops which are not for consumption, e.g., tobacco and
cotton, enraged the rulers so that at one time there was an angry outcry from



ANNEX 3
Page 9
the highest political authorities in the land against non-food crops in the
south. This indignation was short-lived and the farmers were allowed to con-
tinue raising cotton and tobacco. The need for higher production of
high value crops like cotton, tobacco, and sugar raised questions of require-
ments for more land, and ultimately for improved methods of production which
are alien to Swazi agricultural technology.
31.       Another social problem is the challenge of market agriculture
to the traditional social hierarchy. Enterprising farmers are almost exclusi-
vely commoners and they become conspicuously wealthier than either their
immediate chiefs or neighboring aristocrats. This creates in the community a
new source of power, the rich farmer who is often more prestigeous than the
traditional leaders, and on whom the chiefs become dependent for resources,
thus distorting the traditional power structure. Traditions of village and
family unity and solidarity have been very much shaken by new developments in
Swaziland. Migration of the young to the Towns and to the South African mines
and cities gives rise to social alienation, for they tend to develop contemp-
tuous attitudes toward the traditional culture and traditional attitudes.
This is seen in the desertion of rural areas and a growing preference for
urban residence which frees them from the impositions of traditional authori-
ties and from the seemingly empty and largely unmonetized existence of the
rural areas. Material acquisition remains a less important goal for rural
farmers and this is seen particularly in their reluctance to sell their cattle
in spite of the good prices cattle can fetch in the market.
32.       The changes required by introduction of more modern agricultural
techniques are very substantial. New crops require constant vigilance and
new patterns of land use. For cattle, there is need for improved stock,
for improvement of pasture, proper range management and for destocking. All
these challenge traditional cultural practices and attitudes. Despite the
promise of increasing wealth, improvements on the whole have been slow on
Swazi Nation Land and traditionalism and lack of knowledge accounted for much
of lag and failure to take advantage of the new opportunities. Despite these
factors, the biggest handicap faced by farmers on Swazi Nation Land is that
major improvements in agriculture require some capital, and the small, typical
Swazi farmer has very limited monetary resources.
33.       The Role of Women.  The importance of women in agriculture and their
pivotal role in production have not received the attention they merit in
development programs in the past. This is due to the socio-cultural preju-
dices of Swazi society, which is predominantly male-oriented and male-dominated.
Yet women are the social dynamo of Swazi society; Swazi family life and
economy are difficult to imagine without the women in the leading roles, and
women do most work in farming. The neglect of the crucial role of women
ignores the fact that they are the most active workers in social programs like
rural health, nursing, adult literacy, in "save the children" organization,
and in training women in rural skills. Their importance in the rural areas
should be enchanced by the fact that they often have more formal education
than the men due to the use of young boys as herders while girls go to school.



ANNEX 3
Page 10
Women also tend to have a higher social motivation as workers because for them
any improvement is an improvement of their children's lot. Further, few Swazi
women experience difficulties with alcoholism. To date, however, most agricul-
tural training and extension efforts focus on men, and domestic science
programs, primarily oriented to women, were not directed towards directly
productive and profitable activities. There has been some improvement in
recent years, but the potential contribution of Swazi women to rural develop-
ment has scarcely been touched up to now.
January 13, 1977
