SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Marketing, Processing, and Prices of Crops and Livestock
A. Principal Crops
Maize
1.        The principal staple crop of Swaziland is maize.  Other staples
include beans, sorghum, and pumpkins. Maize is produced principally on Swazi
Nation Land, but about 11% of total production is produced on freehold title
farms. Maize is grown on about 75% of total cultivated Swazi Nation Land.
2.        Maize is grown by Swazi farmers principally for subsistence and
only a small proportion of maize harvested is sold outside the farm. The
quantity of maize marketed through commercial channels varies considerably:
when yields are low, only about 10% of the crop is marketed; in a good year,
as much as 25% may be marketed. Thus, 75-90% of total production is consumed
on the farm. Virtually all production on freehold title land is sold through
commercial channels. The rural areas are generally self-sufficient in maize;
however, not enough surplus is produced to feed the urban population.
3.        Swaziland farmers have two principal market outlets for maize:
small shops and hammer mills located throughout the rural areas, and a large
commercial mill (the Swaziland Milling Company) located at Manzini. Locally
grown maize can be sold without restriction or control by the farmer to any
buyer, at whatever price he can obtain. However, the Government fixes a
floor price for producer price levels (para. 10).
4.        The Swaziland Milling Company (SMC), established in 1958, is owned
50% by private interests (Hirsch Holding Co.) and 50% by the Swaziland govern-
ment (NIDC). Up to the present, the company has operated under an agreement
with the Government specifying that SMC has the exclusive right to import
maize into Swaziland, and to operate a commercial milling facility, and that
the company must purchase all maize offered to it at a Government fixed floor
price. This agreement is still in effect, on a year to year basis, but the
Government has made clear that it seeks to terminate SMC's monopoly over
grain imports.
5.        SMC is obligated to purchase all maize offered to it within Swaziland,
provided that it meets certain quality standards. These are: (a) maize must
be fit for human consumption; (b) it must be in a sound container; and (c)
moisture content must not exceed 12-1/2%. The fixed price is on the basis of



ANNEX 4
Page 2
a minimum eight bags 1/ collected on the farm by SMC; if a producer delivers
maize, he is reimbursed transport costs. SMC has two marketing depots,
in addition to retail outlets. Recently, the Central Cooperative Union (CCU)
has served as a collection agency, purchasing maize from farmers, and deliver-
ing it to SMC.
6.        Over the past seven years, SMC has purchased an average of about
3% of estimated total maize production. The amount purchased varies sub-
stantially with fluctuation in production; the maximum purchased was 10% in
one year, while in three out of seven years no locally produced maize was
purchased. Local hammer mill operators purchased significantly greater
quantities than SMC; in 1973, for example, local mills purchased about 8,000
tons, while SMC received 2,000 tons. Hammer mills reportedly pay substantially
higher prices than SMC; however, it is likely that much of the throughput
of the local mills is returned to farmers for consumption.
7.        Maize Imports.  Swaziland is a net importer of maize.  The quantity
imported to cover the deficit varies significantly with each harvest, but
ranges from 5 to 25% of total consumption requirements. Maize imports amount
to 2 to 3% of the value of total imports. Table 1 shows maize imports,
maize prices, and value of production and imports. SMC posseses the only
import license issued by the Government. SMC arranges for an allocation of
maize through the South African Maize Board, then orders direct from suppliers.
Small quantities of maize grain is imported and milled in Swaziland, but the
bulk of imports consist of maize meal. The Government is actively seeking to
attain self-sufficiency in maize production through a campaign to promote
maize production. A difficulty in the face of the import substitution policy
is the fact of lower maize prices in South Africa, the result of subsidies.
8.        Consumption:  Per capita maize consumption for the period 1967-74
averaged about 27 kg, which is relatively high compared with neighboring
countries. SMC distributes maize throughout Swaziland to retailers or to its
own retail outlets; the proportion of total consumption handled by SMC varied
from 11-25% for the period 1967-73, with average of 18%.
9.        Maize Prices:  Maize priced to the producer are fixed on an annual
basis. The official price is determined by the Ministry of Agriculture, which
relies for its decision on the recommendation of the Maize Advisory Board. The
Board includes the following members:
1/   1 bag = 90 kg.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
Permanent Secretary, Ministry of Agriculture (Chairman)
Representative of the Ministry of Commerce and Cooperatives
Representative of the Ministry of Finance
Representative of the Ministry of Agriculture
Representative of the Chamber of Commerce
Representative of the Central Cooperative Union
Representative of the Swazi National Council
Representative of the Swazi Milling Company
Two Representatives of the Swaziland Agricultural Union
Representative of the Retail Outlets
Representative of large farmers
10.       Producer prices are fixed by the government; price levels are
established in relation to prices in the Republic of South Africa, and include
a subsidy to the farmer, introduced between 1970-72. Prices are fixed accord-
ing to a formula, which takes into account: (a) prices of imported maize,
and (b) SMC production and marketing costs; this is to compensate SMC for the
fact that maize from South Africa is cheaper than from Swaziland. If the
proportion of local maize actually purchased by SMC exceeds that used in the
formula estimate, SMC is reimbursed by the Government; conversely, if imports
are higher than expected, SMC is obliged to refund the Government accordingly.
Over the past few years, prices to producers in Swaziland have been 12-20%
higher than in the Republic of South Africa. Subsidies are funded through a
maize stabilization fund, controlled by the Maize Advisory Board. The price
at which Swaziland purchases maize from South Africa includes a subsidy by the
South African government designed to benefit South African consumers but
accorded to Swaziland also by bilateral agreement. Thus, SMC obtains maize for
the Republic of South Africa at prices are which significantly lower than it
actually pays to Swazi producers.
11.       Retail prices of maize meal to consumers are also controlled,
through the same process as producer prices.
12.       The price of maize for 1976 has been fixed at E 6.90/bag plus an
E .30 subsidy; this represents an increase of 14.5% over 1975. Retail prices
are fixed at E 8.42/bag, a 9% increase over 1975. This is equivalent to
E.08 /kg (US$.09) to producers and E .09/kg (US$.10) to consumers.
Other Traditional Food Crops
13.       Only small quantities of the food crops produced by Swazi farmers
are marketed through commercial channels. Swaziland Milling Company purchases
sorghum, groundnuts and beans, but in small quantities. SMC purchases and
packages between 40-100 tons of beans a year; about 900 tons are imported
from South Africa.   About 100 tons of sorghum are purchased a year; 3,500-
4,000 tons are imported from South Africa. Only insignificant amounts of
groundnuts are purchased by SMC. Most production by Swazi farmers is consumed
by farm families. Prices are established by market forces, and are not
controlled; however, prices are related to South Africa prices, where a
floor price scheme operates.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
Cotton
14.       Virtually all cotton production from both Swazi Nation Land and
freehold title land is sold on the commercial market. Cotton is purchased
by four ginneries:
(a) Swaziland Cotona (Manzini)
(b) Cotona, Hectorspruit (Eastern Transvaal, South Africa)
(c) Barbeton Cotton (Eastern Transvaal, South Africa)
(d) J. L. Clark, Pongola (Northeast Natal, South Africa.)
15.       The Cotona cotton ginnery was established in 1965 at Matsapa (the
industrial estate near Manzini), and purchases seed cotton in competition
with the three nearby South African ginneries named above. The Cotona
ginnery presently purchases about 70% of total local production and 80% of
Swazi Nation land production. Up to 1973, cotton was purchased at several
collection depots in addition to the ginnery itself. Since 1974, however,
the ginnery has weighed, graded and purchased all cotton offered to the
company on its premises at Matsapa. Recently CCU has become involved in
cotton buying; they buy from farmers at local depots and transport bales
to Cotona's plant.
16.       All cotton lint and cotton seed derived from seed cotton production
in Swaziland is sold to manufacturers in South Africa. Prior to 1974, an
agreement existed whereby members of the South African Cotton Textile
Manufacturers Association undertook to purchase at seasonally set prices,
a minimum of 60,000 bales lint (of all grades) from all members of the South
African Customs Union 1/. In the range between 60,000 and 175,000 bales (50%
of total manufacturing requirements) the spinners were not obliged to purchase
the local crop, but if they did, they still had to buy at the established
prices. Annual Customs Union production before 1974 had normally been between
80 - 90,000 bales. In 1974, however, production increased to 300,000 bales,
and 19,000 bales remained unsold at the end of the season. As a result the
agreement with the spinners collapsed. At present, they have undertaken to
buy only Grade A lint at an agreed price. 2/
1/   South Africa, Botswana, Lesotho and Swaziland.
2/   The manufacturers agreed to purchase all locally produced "saleable"
cotton (Grade A) at a fixed price up to the end of March 1976. It
is estimated that this will amount to about 75% of total Custom Union
production.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
17.       The price paid for seed cotton is directly related to lint prices
in South Africa, which are largely determined by world market prices. Prices
were as follows:
Year                 Seed Cotton             Lint
1975            E.20 - .25/kg            E 1.11/kg
1976            E.38/kg                  E 1.13/kg
Cotton prices are fixed in South Africa by a cotton committe including repre-
sentatives of South African producers, ginners and textile manufacturers
(spinners); Swaziland has an observer. The South African Government is able
to fix the price of inputs because it controls imports. In negotiation of
seasonal prices with the South African Government, spinners have argued that
they should have not to pay more for local cotton than the landed world market
price. Local prices have therefore been closely geared to world prices
(C.I.F. Liverpool) with an allowance for freight charges (12 c/kg for lint or
4 c /kg equivalent for seed cotton). Lint prices for the period 1970-1975 are
shown in Table 2. They are based on the world market price for cotton lint
averaged on the November/March Liverpool price index for dirk (strict middling
1-1/26h staple lint).
18.       A number of factors led to the lint price falling between 1974 and
1975. These included:
(a) The world depression in the textile industry;
(b) The large increase in production in Southern
Africa, from 84,000 bales in 1972 to 220,000
bales in 1975);
(c) Dumping of cheap textiles from overseas on the
South African market in mid-1974 forcing spinners
to cut back on production;
(d) Imports from Rhodesia into the South African
market, probably at discount prices.
However, a slow recovery is anticipated in demand and price in the medium term
because world cotton resources are fairly low, and a recovery in the world
economy is indicated, while some countries traditionally growing cotton on a
large scale are presently forced to convert sizable acreages from cotton to
food crops, due to their rapidly increasing population. For the 1975/76
season ginners have already announced a minimum final price of E.26/kg for
seed cotton. Swaziland supplies about 15-20% of total lint used by South
Africa spinners.
19.       In the past, marketing problems in Swaziland have deterred small
farmers from producing cotton. These included a lack of adequate marketing
facilities, inadequate transport, long waits at ginneries, and difficulties in



ANNEX 4
Page 6
obtaining payment promptly. Itinerant cotton buyers have emerged, largely as
a result, and have operated successfully. They work illegally, in defiance of
Cotton Buying Regulations introduced by the Ministry in 1974, with the main
object of protecting small growers; itinerant buyers tend to pay lower prices;
many are in fact large cotton growers themselves. The need to improve market-
ing is recognized, and the intervention of CCU is one indication that the
problem is on the way to being resolved.
Tobacco
20.       All tobacco produced in Swaziland is, by law, sold through the
Swaziland Tobacco Cooperative situated in Nglangano, in southwestern Swaziland.
The cooperative is controlled by freehold title farmers, and membership
is limited to farmers who produced over a substantial quantity of tobacco a
year. Members are entitled to discounts on inputs and to dividends. In
1974-75, tobacco was produced by about 250 growers, of which only about 34
were members of the Tobacco cooperative.
21.       Tobacco produced in Swaziland is principally low grade pipe and
chewing tobacco. Most is now grown in the south, although it can be produced
elsewhere. Farmers dry tobacco in barns, then grade it, tie it into hands,
bale it, and transport it to the cooperative headquarters. There it is
weighed and assessed in grades. Tobacco is stacked for two weeks for fermen-
tation, then finally graded.  Subsequently it goes through the proctor machine
which dries and humidifies the tobacco before final baling. Farmers are paid
for the crop in three separate payments.
22.       The Tobacco Cooperative sells tobacco to manufacturers in South
Africa; Swaziland's allocation is determined by the South Africa Tobacco
Board.  When a sufficient quantity is available, the South African Board
instructs the cooperative where tobacco should be delivered, thus allocating
quantities of each grade among South African manufacturers. A small proportion
of tobacco is sold locally.
23.       The Ministry of Agriculture is considering establishment of a second
buying center in the center of Swaziland. This would assist smallholders in
that region in marketing their crop.
24.       Prices are set by the Tobacco Cooperative at a meeting each March,
in reference to South African prices which are pre-set for each grade by the
Tobacco Industry Control Board. It appears that prices now paid by South
African buyers are fully in line with world market prices. In 1975, the price
paid for grade IV pipe tobacco (which most Swazi farmers marketed) was E1.2
per kg (US$1.40).
Rice
25.       Most rice produced in Swaziland is sold to the Republic of South
Africa; approximately 10% of the crop is retained for local sale to urban
retailers. In 1974 approximately 3,259 tons were exported and 350 tons
retained for internal consumption. Nearly all rice is milled locally.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
26.       Rice prices quoted in August, 1975 were as follows:  long grain
to Johannesburg 11.5 c/kg (unpolished), 15 c/kg (polished); short grain
(paddy) to Johannesburg, E 227.70/ton (net freight and commission). This is
equivalent to E 325/ton (polished). Approximately 10% of rice produced by
farmers under a Republic of China supported scheme is sold at roadside stalls
at a premium price. The rice sold is long-grain (polished) and the current
price average E 400/ton or E 20/50 kg bag.
Vegetables and Fruits
27.       Marketing is a principal difficulty in the way of increasing vege-
table production. An organized and efficient marketing system is essential
to transport perishable commodities to urban centers. Standardization of
grades is also required. Only a limited number of growers transport their
own produce to the main urban centers and it is evident that before any
continuity of supplies can be assured, the transport system will require
improvement and some center for wholesale marketing needs to be established.
28.       Production of vegetables in frost free areas during winter has
considerable export potential. Johannesburg, Pretoria, and Durban are large
urban markets accessible to Swaziland, and at present substantial quantities
of winter vegetables are sold at these outlets. For example, the forecast
potato production by the end of 1975 of 10,000 tons would require sales
in South Africa of approximately 5,000 tons; a part of this could be produced
in the Lowveld during the winter months.
Other Crops
29.       Other important crops produced in Swaziland are grown principally
on Freehold Title Land; most are destined for export to South Africa or
other markets. Tables 8-11 give data on agricultural exports and processing
of agricultural products.
Marketing of Inputs
30.       A subsidiary of SMC, Farm Chemicals, is the principal importer
of agricultural inputs for Swaziland. Farm Chemicals is 50% owned by the
South African Fertilizer Industry. Virtually all inputs are imported from
the Republic of South Africa, where prices are comparable to or below world
market prices. Farm Chemicals has an agreement with the Government to sell
fertilizer at prices that are lower than the South Africa wholesale price;
the margin is now roughly 2%. However, South Africa subsidizes fertilizer
retail prices for all South African farmers by about 6%. Consequently, actual
retail fertilizer prices in Swaziland are higher than those in South Africa,
and there is some unauthorized trade in fertilizers in the vicinity of the
borders. Farm Chemicals has recently established a bulk fertilizer bleeding
plant in Swaziland; the agreement with the Government on price levels will
continue; i.e., price will be fixed in relation to landed South African
prices. Prices in Swaziland come into effect when South African prices are
gazetted in January of each year. CCU has recently become involved in procure-
ment and distribution of agricultural inputs . Orders are placed following
meetings of an interministerial marketing and inputs committee; CCU places



ANNEX 4
Page 8
orders with Farm Chemicals which in turn orders supplies in bulk from South
African suppliers; the suppliers deliver direct to CCU depots and CCU then
handles sales to farmers.
B. Marketing and Processing of Livestock Products
Live Cattle Marketing
31.       An estimated 50,000 to 60,000 cattle are marketed annually by
Swaziland producers, representing a total marketed offtake of about 10%.
Producers sell live cattle through two principal outlets: (a) organized
auction sales in Government or private sales yards; and (b) private sales
to cattle buyers, normally at dip tanks. Most cattle are sold directly for
slaughter or export, but some are consigned to fattening ranches, whence
they are sold for slaughter. Table 3 summarizes data on livestock production.
Table 4 gives data on cattle marketing and slaughters.
32.       Cattle auctions have been organized for about 30 years.  The volume
of cattle handled has varied considerably. Initially, one third of all cattle
sold passed through auction yards, and in the 1960's about one fourth. In
1972, only 7% of marketed cattle were sold at auctions, while in 1975, about
7,000 animals were sold at auction. Auctions are held at Government sale
yards or at private yards. There are 10 Government yards, where auctions are
normally held once a month: 100 sales were held in 1975, and 4,107 cattle
were sold; in 1975, 2,852 cattle were sold at 14 private sale yards. Tradi-
tional producers seldom use sale yards, except for cattle consigned to fatten-
ing ranches, largely because of trekking distances involved; further, it
appears that farmers prefer to negotiate prices directly with buyers. Most
cattle sold at auction are, therefore, fat cattle from Government ranches.
The Swaziland Meat Corporation (para 36) by agreement with the Government,
guarantees a floor price for all cattle auctionned, and serves as a buyer of
last resort.
33.       Over 90% of marketed cattle, including virtually all those sold
by traditional producers, are sold by private agreements, normally at govern-
ment dip tanks. Farmers sell cattle here without restriction, with prices
negotiated between buyer and seller. Data about these sales are poor.
However, it appears that the principal buyers are local butchers and traders,
intermediaries purchasing cattle for export, further finishing, or simply for
resale, and buyers for the Swaziland Meat Corporation. Traditional producers
prefer to sell cattle at the dip tanks largely because it is convenient.
34.       Most cattle sold by traditional producers are over five years
old: farmers normally sell cattle only when they need cash, for school
fees, festivals, or other purposes. Consequently, offtake is relatively
low compared with what it could be, even with present production coeffic-
ients. The continuing presence on Swazi Nation Land pastures of animals



ANNEX 4
Page 9
which are not productive contributes substantially to the problem of over
stocking. The Government is seeking to encourage producers to sell cattle
through utilization of fattening and SISA ranches. Further, the agreement
with the Swaziland Meat Corporation to guarantee a floor price for cattle
forms part of the Government effort to encourage increasing sales by farmers.
Although intensive extension efforts in Rural Development Areas appear to be
resulting in increased cattle sales by producers, the national picture is less
favorable, and cattle sales in fact declined in 1975.
35.       Cattle Prices:  The weighted average sale price of cattle per
head at local sales between 1970 and 1974 was recorded as follow:
Year          Price
1970        E 60.26
1971        E 59.66
1972        E 75.00
1973        E 94.00
1974        E128.00
1976 prices for cattle averaged E 180-200 per head. The SMC floor price is
fixed at EO.40 per kilo liveweight; the estimated average weight at sale is
estimated at 170 kg. Prices in Swaziland are largely governed by price
movements in the Republic of South Africa, and by the world market position
for meat.
36.       Live Cattle Exports:  Approximately 12% of all cattle marketed
are exported on the hoof to the Republic of South Africa. Most go by rail
from Lavumisa/Gollel, in southeast Swaziland, to Durban. The South African
Meat Board fixes a quota for imports of Swazi Beef.  This quota was 175 head
of cattle a week in mid 1976. The quota is allocated among Swazi producers
by the Swaziland Meat Board. In practice, most cattle exported are those
produced by freehold title farmers. In 1975, 6,820 cattle were exported to
Durban and Johannesburg, a further 550 animals were sold to farmers in the
Republic of South Africa, and six animals were exported to Mozambique.
Average prices obtained for cattle in the Republic of South Africa do not
differ significantly from those in Swaziland; there tends, however, to be
a premium for quality at Durban, while prices for poor quality animals
tend to be higher in Swaziland. The Ministry of Agriculture is systematic-
ally collecting and analyzing price data, and hopes thus to advise producers
where the best price can be obtained at any given time.
Meat Processing
37.       Cattle are slaughtered at the export slaughterhouse operated by
the Swazi Meat Corporation in Matsapa and by local butchers located throughout



ANNEX 4
Page 10
Swaziland. The abattoir has a capacity of about 35,000 head a year; in
fact this figure is seldom attained. Table 5 gives data on plant through-
put. The Swaziland Meat Corporation has been obliged to import live animals
from the Republic of South Africa in order to keep plant operations at a
minimum economic level during the winter months. Swaziland imports in 1975
were about 8,000 head of cattle, of which 4,300 went directly to the Matsapa
abattoir for slaughter. The plant produces a full range of meat products.
Throughput in 1975 was the lowest recorded for the past 8 years, 16,880 head.
Local butchers slaughtered an estimated 36,000 head of cattle in 1975.
38.       Of meat produced by the Swaziland Meat Corporation, an estimated 10%
is consumed in Swaziland, while the remainder in exported. Per capita consump-
tion of beef is estimated at 18 kg a year. Meat products also are exported,
with the exception of bone meal, which is being consumed increasingly in
Swaziland itself. The United Kingdom is the principal market for Swazi
beef; other meat is exported to South Africa, and Zambia was formerly a buyer
of Swazi beef. Hides are exported to Iraq and South Africa, and carcass
meal to South Africa. Table 6 shows exports of beef, while Table 7 shows
exports of animal products.
39.       Swaziland has exprienced problems in marketing beef since the
EEC imposed import quotas and instituted levies on imported beef, because
the UK has traditionally purchased about 95% of Swazi meat exports. The
Government has now negotiated an agreement with EEC providing for a quota
of about 3,000 tons of meat a year; further, 90% of the levy imposed on
imported beef is under the agreement retained by the seller (the Swaziland
Meat Corporation), on the understanding that it would be paid to the Government.
The Government has agreed with the Swaziland Meat Corporation that in lieu of
payment of the levy, the Corporation will guarantee a floor price for cattle
purchases, and agree to buy all cattle offered to it.
40.       The Government is aware that new export markets should be explored,
to diversify exports and to safeguard against future difficulties with
imports to the EEC. The Annual Report of the Veterinary Service for 1975
emphasizes the need to establish a livestock or meat board to regularize
exports and supply of cattle for slaughter. A study of beef and meat marketing
within Swaziland and overseas would be useful. An agreement was recently
signed with Gabon to supply meat in future.
41.       Prices received for Swazi beef exports reflect closely world market
prices for beef; Table 11 shows value of beef exports.
Other Livestock
42.       Few other animals are marketed or slaughtered through commercial
channels; most are slaughtered and consumed on the farm or by local butchers
An estimated 15% of the total goat and sheep population is slaughtered each
year. Chickens are also widely consumed in both rural and urban areas.
None of these categories of livestock are exported, except in very small
quantities.



ANNEX 4
Page 11
Marketing and Processing of Dairy Products
43.       Only a small fraction of milk products consumed in Swaziland are
marketed and processed through commercial channels. The modern, largely
urban-centered dairy industry is focused on Swaziland's one milk plant,
located in Mbanane, which is now owned and managed by the Swaziland Dairy
Board. The total recorded supply of milk and milk products (including
imports) was valued at E 2.1 million in 1974-75, and was equivalent to about
14 million liters of milk. Of this, about 11 million liters were commercial
supplies and 3.0 million liters, World Food Program Aid. The total supply
constitutes only 28% of the FAO recommended average intake of dairy products.
44.       The Mbabane milk plant is supplied with fresh milk (a) collected
from Swaziland producers, mostly in the Lowveld, and (b) imported from the
Republic of South Africa. In 1975-76, about 1,000 liters a day were sup-
plied by local producers, and 4,000 liters a day imported. Local production
is concentrated in the summer months, November-May, leaving a large import
requirement for the rest of the year. About 11% was supplied by an estimated
20 freehold title farm producers; the remainder was provided by Swazi farmers.
45.       The Swaziland Dairy Board is improving collection facilities in
the Middleveld and Lowveld, providing collection depots, cooling centers, and
collection trucks. The Mbabane milk plant sold a total of 1,256,000 liters
of liquid milk in 1974-75. It was established in 1969 as a privately owned
milk pasteurizing plant, on leased premises. The company made considerable
losses, however, and in 1974 the Dairy Board purchased the plant. Average
daily sales of liquid milk and sour milk "emasi" increased from 3,000 liters
in May, 1974 to 6,300 liters in May, 1976. It is projected that, given
population growth,rising per capita income, and consumer preference for
liquid over powdered milk, demand will increase to 12,000 liters per day
by 1979-80.
46.       Milk prices are not controlled by the Dairy Board.  Prices are
high compared with Africa, although imported milk is "industrial milk",
often over-priced and of poor quality. Producer milk prices were fixed
at 67 cents per gallon; with premiums for quality the average price is 72
cents per gallon. Imports of dairy products to Swaziland were valued at E 1.9
million in 1974-75, representing 2.1% of total imports.
47.       The Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA) has recently
agreed to finance a dairy development project. This would include con-
struction of a new plant and development of collection facilities. The
intention would be to supply all of Swaziland's milk demand, and to export
surpluses to South Africa, which is projected to face a shortage of milk
in the near future. The potential for dairy development in Swaziland is
good, given the favorable animal health situation, climate, and pasture.
Supplementary feeding will be required, however.

48.       Swaziland has important processing industries based on agricultural
products, principally produced on freehold title land. Tables 8-11 summarize
agricultural products processing and exports.

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Institutions Involved in Rural Development
Introduction
1.        The principal institutions of the Swaziland Government and of the
Swazi Nation which are directly involved in rural development programs are
described in this Annex, as follows:
A.   Government in Swaziland:  Historical and Political
Background.
B.   Central Government Institutions and Local Administration.
C.   The Swazi National Council and the Central Rural Develop-
ment Board.
D.   The Ministry of Agriculture.
E.   Financial and Agricultural Credit Institutions.
F.   Other Institutions in Rural Development.
G.   Coordination of Rural Development Programs.
2.        The institutional framework for policy and decision-making and
for program implementation in the field of rural development is particularly
complex in Swaziland. Three principal factor explain this complexity:
(a) The juxtaposition of traditional and modern authority which characterizes
government in Swaziland directly affects decision-making for rural develop-
ment. This is because rural development principally concerns Swazi Nation
Land, where the Swazi National Council exercises final authority in most
matters, but where the modern government has responsibility for initiating
and managing virtually all services and development programs. Consequently,
a dual decision-making process is involved at both the planning and execution
stage; (b) Since rural development can be defined as the full range of services
and programs aimed at assisting the rural population, the administration of
rural development plays a dominant role in central government policy and
decision-making. About 90% of Swaziland's population is rural and the develop-
ment of the rural sector holds the highest priority for the Swazi government.
Consequently, decisions on problems of rural development are often made at a
high level, are frequently brought up at Cabinet level and by the Swazi
National Council, and may have to be resolved by the King; and (c) a large



ANNEX 5
Page 2
number of institutions and agencies have responsibilities relating to rural
development. Coordination among these agencies has become increasingly
difficult, and the task is further complicated by the numerous donor programs
in support of many rural development activities.
A. Government in Swaziland: Historical and Political Background
3.        Swaziland has a long history of rule as a traditional monarchy
under the central authority of the King or Ngwenyama. From 1906 to 1968,
however, Swaziland came under colonial rule, and was administered as a British
Protectorate.   During this period, the King was reduced to the status of a
Paramount Chief who took orders from the British Resident Commissioner, and
State and social policy were in the hands of the British. Generally speaking,
the British administrators in Swaziland did not fully practice the celebrated
policy of indirect rule. A large number of Europeans settled in Swaziland,
criminal jurisdiction was withdrawn from the Swazi King, and it was expected
that Swaziland would eventually be incorporated into the Republic of South
Africa; as a result, a type of dual rule developed. The British administra-
tion set up a national Police force (1907) and contented themselves with
issuing proclamations and enforcing them directly. Although they often
consulted with the Swazi Paramount Chief, in effect the power of the tra-
ditional Swazi authorities was curtailed, and their responsibilities were
limited to the collection of taxes and to matters which pertained strictly
to the traditional sector, provided that tradition was not "repugnant to
natural justice."
4.        The most important result of the Colonial period, in terms of
the Swazi Social System, is that it introduced a dualism which took the
form of a two-legged pyramid in the social, cultural, political, religious,
intellectual, and economic parts of the social order, the two arms of which
are the traditional and modern. This continues to this day and the King
presides atop this pyramid. It also introduced, for the first time in Swazi-
land, a new form of legitimacy, that is, legal authority which rested on
the acceptance of the principles that legal norms may be established outside
of traditional authority, that the person in authority, the superior who may
be the King or British Administrator, is himself subject to an impersonal
order of law (in other words, the King's powers were limited by law), that the
persons who obey the authority to do so in their capacity as members of the
State, and their obedience is to the law and not the individual who represents
it, and that no personal loyalty is required to the man in authority; rather
there is loyalty to an impersonal country, a code or a flag - or even a set of
laws. This was a radical departure from Swazi norms and seriously undermined
traditional authorities; the beginnings of political unrest and political
agitation in Swaziland can be traced to this period; Swazi political "agita-
tors" questioned not only the Colonial presence in Swaziland, but also the
basis of legitimacy in Swazi society, including the right of the King and the
aristocracy to exercise absolute power.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
5.        Swaziland achieved independence in 1968.  The Swazi nation accepted
a constitution drawn on the Westminster model. The monarchy was reinstated
and the King became Constitutional Head of State. Two houses of Parliament
were created, the members to be elected by the general population on the
principle of one man one vote. A two party system was institutionalized
with the majority party being the ruling party. The effect of this was
to legalize the opposition (a fundamental departure from Swazi tradition)
and to establish the right of commoners to a share in power and government.
Finally, a civil service was introduced, appointed by a civil service commis-
sion according to well established bureaucratic rules and standards. Tradi-
tional institutions were retained, however, alongside these "modern" instru-
ments of government; the principal of these is the Swazi National Council
(para 14).
6.        The Independence constitution established a popularly elected
Government in a country which had traditionally been ruled by the King and
the Aristocracy. An immediate result was that many people who previously
were commoners now assumed positions of power, influence, and wealth,
sole prerogatives of the King and the Aristocracy under the old order.
This posed particular problems since the new criterion for holding power was
education, which the King, the Chiefs, and the Aristocracy had previously
disdained. The legalization of the opposition was the most difficult issue for
the King, because opposition was seen as "umbango", or illegitimate contest
for power which rightfully belonged to the King and the Aristocracy. Trouble
soon arose, and in order to contain opposition, the King's party deported a
number of dissidents. In resulting court cases, the King's party lost, and
the King and the aristocracy felt that their powers had been subverted by the
Constitution and by the judges who found against them in court. This led
directly to the suspension of the Constitution and the takeover of the Govern-
ment by the King on April 16, 1973. In the preamble to the takeover procla-
mation, it is stated, inter alia, that:  "the Constitution has permitted the
importation into our country of highly undesirable practices alien to and
incompatible with the way of life of our society and designed to disrupt and
destroy our own peaceful and constructive and essentially democratic methods
of political activity; increasingly this element engenders hostility, bitter-
ness and unrest in our peaceful society." Suspension of the Constitution and
the King's takeover reflected an attempt by the traditionalists to contain
change.
7.        Since the suspension of the Constitution, the King has exercised
full authority over all aspects of Swazi government, in both the traditional
and modern institutions. A long promised new constitution has not appeared.
Decision-making can be more expeditious than under the Parliamentary



ANNEX 5
Page 4
system, with decisions taken by the King-in-Council (the King with the
Cabinet). However, the King's decision is often sought for matters of detail
as well as policy issues, which results in numerous delays in implementation
of programs, and for the failure of civil servants to deal effectively with
administrative problems from fear of displeasing the King. On the other hand,
the King's broad powers and generally enlightened approach to leadership offer
significant advantages, as the King can introduce changes which, under most
other circumstances would be unthinkable. The King, under the traditional
system, alone has the right to change tradition, and only he can sanction all
activities affecting Swazi Nation Land.
8.        The King has considered and approved ODM-financed rural development
projects in the past, although in some cases there have been delays in bring-
ing matters to his attention. Once the King's sanction is made known, however,
programs have advanced rapidly, and both chiefs and civil servants have
striven to carry out his will. During the appraisal mission for the proposed
Project, the King specifically considered the Project proposal, and the
mission was informed that he stated that it had his full support.
B. Central Government Institutions and Local Administration
9.        The modern Government comprises a Cabinet of Ministers appointed by
the King, and a civil service. The present Secretary to the Cabinet also
serves as Secretary to the Central Rural Development Board of the Swazi
National Council. Ministers and other permanent secretaries tend to be
political appointiees, with civil servants in other posts. The ministries
most directly concerned with rural development are the Ministry of Agricul-
ture, the Ministry of Finance, the Ministry of Commerce and Cooperatives,
the Ministry of Health, and the Ministry of Education. The Ministry of
Power, Works and Communications is a key service agency with responsibility
for road development and maintenance, the Public Works Department which
constructs most government buildings, and the Central Transport.Organization,
a centralized government motor vehicle and equipment pool. The Ministry of
Local Government is responsible for local administration, while the Ministry
of Establishments and Training oversees all civil service matters and both
overseas and local training programs.
10.       Swaziland shares with many developing countries the problem of
serious manpower shortages, particularly at intermediate levels. At present,
expatriates fill many posts; for example, over 200 posts are supported by the
UK, while the US Peace Corps has over 150 volunteers in Swaziland, and many
other donor agencies provide technical assistants to fill established positions.
However, the government is starting to resolve the problem, and many Swazis
are now in training program that should enable them to fill most key government
posts within a few years.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Local Administration
11.       For purposes of territorial administration, Swaziland is divided
into four districts: Manzini, Hhohho, Shiselweni, and Lubombo. A district
commissioner is responsible for administration within each district; he
is supported by a senior distict officer for the sub-disrict, a part of
each district administered by a sub-office of the district (thus each district
has one sub-district). The Ministry of Local Administration is responsible
for all territorial administration, which is concerned principally with
maintenance of law and order and assistance to traditional authorities in
administrative and judicial matters. Most ministries of execution are repre-
sented at district level, and the heads of each office comprise the district
team, chaired by the District Commissioner; it normally meets once a month.
The role of the District Commissioner is that of a coordinator of all govern-
ment activities within his district; since basic planning and budget decisions
are made at a central level, his role is necessarily somewhat restricted.
12.       The District Commissioner administers a budget allocated by the
Ministry of Local Administration, which principally covers administrative
expenditures together with district road maintenance and other local respon-
sibilities. There are no local budget resources at present. The District
Commissioners administer some funds for local, self-help type projects. At
the initiative of the community, chiefs raised funds from their followers for
specific purposes, and the Government provides additional funds required and
technical assistance. The King has insisted unequivocally that such projects
must have local support, demonstrated in a fund for construction and mainte-
nance. Contributions may be in cash or in kind. The most popular projects
are spring protection, small irrigation schemes, school expansion, clinics,
bridges, markets, and sports grounds. The District Commissioners are actively
involved in the design, funding, and execution of such projects.
13.       There is at present no local government system within the "modern"
government, and no local elected or representative bodies. The principal
instruments of local government on Swazi National Land are the chiefs. Owing
in part to Swaziland's small size and tradition of a strong executive, adminis-
tration tends to be highly centralized, with virtually all administrative
and political matters referred for decision to the Central Government.
C. The Swazi National Council and the Central Rural
Development Board
14.       The Swazi National Council (SNC) is the supreme socio-political
body of the Swazi nation. It meets once a year, between July and August,
at Lobomba, the King's ritual and ceremonial capital (located several
kilometers east of Mbabane). It is analogous to a national parliament but
it is also the Swazi highest court of appeal. In theory, every adult male
Swazi can attend and participate in the deliberations of the SNC; in
practice, due largely to geographic factors, attendance tends to be limited



ANNEX 5
Page 6
to those with special functions, the aristocracy, and those who live near
Lobomba. The effective body which conducts day-to-day SNC business is
a small executive committee, composed mostly of members of the aristocracy
and headed by an executive secretary who keeps the minutes and records and
manages the offices of the SNC at Lobomba.
15.       The SNC is, in the Swazi dual structure of government, the tra-
ditional leg of the socio-political pyramid. It is charged with main-
taining and protecting Swazi law and custom and with examining, approving
and supervising all proposals which concern land use, and ensuring that
these are in keeping with customary law, rights and obligations. It can
thus be viewed as the citadel of Swazi traditionalism and conservatism.
16.       Tinkundlas:  The principal communications link between the Swazi
National Council and the rest of the country are the "Tinkundlas," which are
meeting places in the different rural areas throughout the country. Each
"Tinkundla" encompasses several chiefs and chiefdoms; there are 24 in
Swaziland. Meetings are convened at these "Tinkindla" whenever there are
matters of regional or national importance to be discussed. Such meetings
are presided over by a special King's appointee. Meetings at "tinkundla"
are attended by local adult males and their chiefs. A new trend in Swaziland
is to use the "tinkundla" as a focal point not only for socio-economic
organization but also for the organization of rural social services.
Thus, for example, the development plan indicates that the RDAs should be
centered around them. It is also planned that in future they will be
used increasingly as rural employment centers for rural youth.
17.       The Central Rural Development Board (CRDB):  Largely because of
disappointments and problems arising from pre-independence development schemes
for rural areas, the King as Ngwenyama of the Swazi Nation created by pro-
clamation in 1954, a Central Rural Development Board to oversee all future
programs affecting the rural population (Appendix 1). The principal functions
of the Board, as defined in the order, are to report to the Ngwenyama on the
condition of resources, including water, soil, grass and timber on land held
by the Nation, and on settlement schemes. The CRDB was to recommend steps to
be taken to protect resources and improve their utilization. The CRDB
approves all plans for resettlement schemes and agricutlural development
proposed by the local population, and oversees their implementation, including
enforcement of decisions taken. An explicit function of the CRDB is to
encourage the practical involvement of the local population in rural develop-
ment projects.
18.       CRDB members are appointed by the King and they are an effective
institutional link between the Agricultural Department and the King, the
Swazi National Council and the chiefs. The Board is responsible directly



ANNEX 5
Page 7
to the King. The CRDB in practice examines carefully and approves, often
with modifications, all detailed plans for development on Swazi Nation Land.
19.       Rural Development Officers:  The CRDB appoints Rural Development
Officers (RDO's), who act as its field officers. They work very closely
with extension officers staff of the Department of Agriculture. The RDO's
have clearly specified functions, namely, to hold meetings and discussions
with chiefs and other traditional leaders about the introduction and imple-
mentation of rural development projects. They act as facilitators and take
the political responsibilities from the extension officers, whose functions
then become strictly technical. The RDO's have acted to enforce CRDB deci-
sions in several instances; most notably, when the King decreed that grass
strips should be established on all arable land, it was the RDO's who enforced
the order, to the point of taking sanctions against recalcitrants. In those
cases where grazing controls have been introduced, the RDO's have assisted in
enforcing regulations. They have been effective, since most are trained
extension officers, who also have the full authority of the King behind them.
There are now eight RDO's; they are employees of the CRDB, but are paid from
the budget of the Ministry of Agriculture.
20.       The Tibiyo Fund:  A further branch of the Swazi Nation is the
Tibiyo fund. "Tibiyo" are minerals and "Tibiyo Fund" refers to a fund created
by the King and the SNC into which mineral royalties are paid by all com-
panies engaged in mining in Swaziland. The fund is not part of the Government
Treasury but is separate institution controlled by the SNC. The funds so
collected are used by the SNC to buy back Swazi land from private, expatriate
farmers; this land becomes part of Swazi Nation Land but has been set aside
for commercial development. There are thus two competing land-buying opera-
tions in the country: the ODM operation, financed by the British Government,
for rural development purposes and the "Tibiyo" Fund operations which also
buys land for the Swazi Nation but which, so far, has used most land for
commercial development. A small SNC committee, which reports to the King,
controls the Tibiyo Fund. A new manager of the Fund has recently been appoin-
ted; his task is to introduce effective management on the growing number of
Tibiyo Fund farms and thus to make them profitable enterprises.
D. The Ministry of Agriculture (Chart I)
Department of Agriculture
21.       The Director of Agriculture (formerly Chief Agricultural Officer-
CAO) is responsible for the following sections: extension and crop produc-
tion, the rural development area program, the land development section,
fisheries, forestry, and home economics.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
22.       The Agricultural Extension Service is organized on a district
basis, with a central crop production unit providing specialized assistance.
A deputy director, agriculture directs administrative aspects of the service,
while the crop specialists provided technical guidance. Each district has
a senior extension officer (SEO) who is responsible for supervision of
extension staff; they also handle political and social problems that arise
with chiefs. Under the SEO's are extension officers and assistant extension
officers (AEO's); AEO's have a diploma in agriculture, and it is the Govern-
ment's policy that eventually they will serve as the field extension staff.
At present, field officers (FO's), trained under the now-defunct certificate
program, are the principal field workers, despite steady attrition in their
numbers.
23.       The Crop Production Unit is based in Manzini; at present, its
role within the Ministry and linear relationships with other departments are
not entirely clear. The Unit was established as part of an FAO-UNDP funded
crop development project (Annex 2), and aims at developing specialized services
for crop promotion, and at upgrading extension services. The project was not
entirely effective, and the Unit has suffered from the abrupt withdrawal of
several technical assistants following UNDP's budget crisis in early 1976.
The Unit is headed by a "project manager" (the project's co-manager, a local
employee); it includes staff funded by UNDP, ODM, the Peace Corps, other
volunteers, and Ministry of Agriculture employees. The principal sections and
programs are as follows:
(a) Horticulture: The largest section, with six expatriate
officers; it has established fruit nurseries and has a
program to develop fruit and vegetable production, principally
under irrigation. Onions, potatoes and fruit trees are
emphasized.
(b) Irrigation: Oversees small-scale irrigation schemes (about
14), studies and develops new schemes, and assists farmers with
irrigated farming. The section includes one irrigation engineer
adn three Peace Corps workers;
(c) Tobacco: The tobacco agronomist, with a Swazi SEO as his
assistant, has five specialized extension staff and has launched
an ambitious scheme to expand tobacco cultivation;
(d) Cotton: The UNDP cotton agronomist departed, and the program is now
focused principally on cotton pest control. Some demonstration
plots have been established;
(e) Grain and Legumes: The agronomist has concentrated on iden-
tification and introduction of new species of hybrid maize; the
UNDP specialist recently departed and has been replaced by a
pineapple agronomist.



ANNEX 5
Page 9
(f) Mechanization: A mechanization officer is responsible for
developing tractor pools;
(g) Grain Storage: The officer (ODM-funded) has developed a
small specialized extension staff and has launched an active
program to improve storage facilities and practices on
farms;
(h) Soil Testing: A soil testing and analysis service has been
established at the UBLS campus; current emphasis is on Ph
testing.
Thus, the Unit includes a wide range of specialists and services, some
of which tend to operate as independent units, others more directly in
support of district extension services. There is a need for clearer
definition of the unit's role, and for better coordination with other
Ministry activities.
24.       The Rural Development Area Program is a separate unit currently
headed by a senior agricultural officer (SAO) funded by the UK. The SAO
(RDA's) oversees all Department of Agriculture staff working in the four
on-going rural development areas, and has full responsibility for planning,
execution and coordination of development activities in thee areas. These
areas are for most intents and purposes outside the jurisdiction of SEO's
at district level, although they may intervene when political difficulties
arise.
25.       The Land Development Section established in 1972, has the largest
staff and budget of any unit of the Ministry. It is responsible for all
development works including soil conservation (the unit's original title),
terracing, construction of dams and waterways, bush clearing, fishpond develop-
ment, and feeder roads. The Unit is supported by USAID technical assistance
and a loan for equipment and workshop development. The Section has a staff of
almost 300 including 2 engineers, a transport and maintenance control officer,
and five qualified unit managers. The Section has five units based at differ-
ent locations in Swaziland, which carry out field operations; each is under a
unit manager. In principle, the Land Development Unit acts as a contractor
for all other departments of the Ministry for physical development activities.
Program and budget are established on an annual basis, with the Director of
Agriculture responsible for coordination of activities. The Section has
experienced problems with maintenance and broken down equipment.
26.       Fisheries:  A fisheries section was established in 1972, with
assistance from a UNDP-funded expert. A pilot program to provide rural
fish ponds for farm consumption and for sale has proved remarkably
effective. The service includes one FAO expert, two Peace Corps Volunteers,
and field assistants. A Swazi counterpart is being trained in the USA.



ANNEX 5
Page 10
27.       Forestry:  The Forestry Section is headed by a forestry officer;
a UNDP expert departed recently. The Section's objectives are to promote
afforestation on Swazi Nation Land, to oversee legislation on forestry,
and to look after protected flora. A village woodlot program aims at
providing sufficient structural timber and fuel. A further objective
is to rehabilitate wattle scrub, which has become virtually a weed. The
Section includes a forestry officer (a Peace Corps Volunteer) and five
assistants.
28.       Home Economics:  This Section aims at promoting home craft, home
improvement, and nutrition, and is directed to rural women. Gradually the
program has been extended to include more directly productive tasks such as
poultry keeping, dairying, and fish ponds. The staff includes 26 women, with
two technical assistants, most of whom have limited training. New staff
members, however, are diplomates from UBLS.
29.       Rural Youth:  A new unit within the Agriculture Department seeks
to promote awareness of agriculture among youth, in programs comparable to
and inspired by the American 4-H Club system. Staff includes two volunteers
and five local staff.
30.       The Agriculture Department also manages farmer training centers,
which come within the jurisdiction of SEO's of each district.
Department of Veterinary Services
31.       The Department has two principal divisions:  animal health and
animal production. The Animal Health Division includes 14 veterinarians,
three of them Swazis. They are responsible for headquarters work (3),
the veterinary laboratory (1), meat inspection at the Swazi Meat Corpora-
tion abattoir (1), training (1), and field work (8). There are about 180
middle and lower level staff employed by the division. Each district has
a district veterinary officer who oversees animal health inspectors and
veterinary assistants. The veterinary assistants are principally responsi-
ble for operation of government dip tanks, inspection of private dip tanks
and spray races, and for vaccination and other animal health programs.
32.       The Animal Production Division is of relatively recent origin
(established in 1967), and is very small compared with animal health
services. Animal production was neglected until recently, with programs
limited to an ineffective breeding program, sporadic efforts to destock,
and management of holding grounds, theoretically intended to relieve
pressure on Swazi Nation Land pastures, but in practice servicing principally
freeholder farmers and more progressive Swazis. At present, the division
includes only four posts at headquarters, which are concerned with organiza-
tion and operation of a livestock extension service, and with development
of fattening and SISA ranches, and breeding stations. The division's staff
relies heavily on an FAO livestock project (Annex 2) aims at building
up the division. The division oversees ranch and breeding station managers



ANNEX 5
Page 11
who run these facilities with considerable autonomy. Livestock extension
activities have to date been confined to rural development areas, with emphasis
on destocking and on introduction of rational herd management. The division
directs the national breeding program, aimed at upgrading the local Nguni
cattle; other activities include analysis of cattle and meat price data and
dissemination of this information to farmers. Dairy development is also
included within the division's responsibilities.
Land Planning Section
33.       The Land Planning Section is responsible directly to the Permanent
Secretary of the Ministry. Formerly a key section of the Ministry which took
the initiative for most rural development activities (rural development areas
and the land development section came under the section in the past), the
Section is now more specifically a service unit, albeit with an essential
role. The Land Planning Section has been principally responsible for defi-
nition and selection of rural development areas, establishment of priorities
among them, and for preparation of specific physical development plans. It is
these plans, specifing division of arable and grazing areas, location of
buildings, and siting of homesteads, which are reviewed by the chiefs and the
local population and must receive the approval of the CRDB. The section
includes a senior land planning officer, a land use planning officer, three
soil scientists, an irrigation engineer, and a technical officer for detailed
topographic studies.
Economics Section
34.       The Economics Section is responsible for project preparation and
monitoring and for overall planning for the Ministry; it also reports directly
to the Permanent Secretary. The Section includes a senior agricultural
economist, a livestock economist, an agricultural economist, and several
economists. The Section prepares project submission documents and the annual
capital budget of the Ministry.
Administration and Finance
35.       A centralized service for administration and finance reports
directly to the Permanent Secretary.
Coordination of Ministry Activities
36.       The principal vehicle for planning of Ministry activities is the
Projects Committee, which includes the heads of all departments and sections.
The Committee meets once a month to review all proposed and on-going projects,
and makes recommendations to the Permanent Secretary and to the Minister; no
project can be approved without the Committee's approval, and it examines any
proposal that involves deployment of funds. The Permanent Secretary can
overrule the Committee's decisions.



ANNEX 5
Page 12
Reorganization of the Ministry
37.       A reorganization of the Ministry of Agriculture was under considera-
tion by the Permanent Secretary during appraisal. The principal proposals were:
(a) To transfer Animal Production to the Department of
Agriculture, and thus to promote a unified field extension
service covering both crops and livestock; this has
the full support of the Permanent Secretary.
(b) To bring all field staff in rural development areas under
the direct authority of the SAO RDA's, through the local RDA
project managers;
(c) To transfer the land planning and economics sections to
the Department of Agriculture; the Permanent Secretary
did not support this proposal.
This reorganization might require Cabinet approval if it required major
changes in ministry structure or creation of additional posts. The issue of
Ministry organization should be discussed during the loan negotiations to
clarify government's proposals.
E. Financial and Agricultural Credit Institutions
38.       The banking system consists of the Swaziland Development and Savings
Bank (SDSB), two commercial banks, and the newly created Monetary Authority.
The 1975 Financial Institutions Order lays down the general rules to be
followed by the banking system. The Order prescribes minimum capital require-
ments and liquidity requirements and empowers the Monetary Authority to
regulate the banks allocation of resources.
39.       The 1974 Monetary Authority of Swaziland Order provides for the
establishment of the Monetary Authority and regulates its activities. The
Monetary Authority issues Emalangeni (the Swazi currency) and promotes mone-
tary stability. The authorized capital of the Authority is E 1,000,000
(US$1,150,000). The Authority is managed by a Board of Directors consist-
ing of a Governor, a Deputy Governor and three Directors appointed by the
Minister of Finance. The King, in consultation with the Prime Minister,
appoints the Governor. The Authority is authorized to maintain external
reserves. The Authority serves as a Registrar of Financial Institutions;
it may open accounts with commercial banks to which it may offer discounts,
rediscounts and advances. The Authority may regulate interest rates on
loans and deposits, and reserve requirements on deposits held by financial
institutions. The Authority is the Government's fiscal agent in international
transactions and administers exchange controls.



ANNEX 5
Page 13
40.       Two commercial banks operate in Swaziland.  They have, in total,
nine branches and 21 agencies throughout the country. The two banks are
incorporated in Swaziland and they are jointly owned by British banks (60%)
and the Government of Swaziland (40%). The Swaziland Development and Savings
Bank (SDSB), formerly Swaziland Credit and Savings Bank (Annex 7), has an
authorized capital of E 5,000,000 (US$5,750,000) of which E 3,000,000 (US$3,
450,000) is paid in. The Government contributed a substantial amount of
SDSB's capital; grants from the UK and US provide the balance. Government
officials or appointees sit on SDSB's Board of Directors. SDSB has five
branches and three field offices, and a staff of over 100. The SDSB is the
principal institution in Swaziland responsible for providing agricultural
credit.
41.       Other financial institutions operating in Swaziland include the
Swaziland Royal Insurance Corporation, the National Provident Fund and some
savings and credit cooperatives. In 1974, the Government in cooperation
with foreign insurance companies organized the Swaziland Royal Insurance
Corporation; the Government's share is 51%. The Corporation has a monopoly
of insurance business in the country and much of its risk is reinsured abroad.
The National Provident Fund recently began operations and it provides social
security coverage to both Government employees and private sector employees.
The cooperatives are regulated by the Cooperative Societies Act of 1964.
F. Other Institutions
The Central Transport Organization
42.       In 1970, a Central Transport Organization (CTO) was established
within the Ministry of Works, Power and Communication. CTO is a centralized
vehicle, equipment and plant pool for all government agencies; it is responsi-
ble for procurement of vehicles and their eventual replacement and for main-
tenance. CTO also inspects and tests all passenger and commercial vehicles
registered in Swaziland. The Government has over 1,000 vehicles ranging
in size from two-door passenger cars to ten-ton trucks, and 180 pieces of
earthmoving and construction equipment. Vehicles average approximately
10,000 km per year. CTO headquarters are in Mbanane, with workshops in
Manzini, Nhlangano, Siteki, and Piggs Peak.
43.       CTO holds title to the vehicles and equipment pool and leases
individual items to each Ministry. Vehicles are leased on a short-term or
long-term basis. In either case, CTO charges the user ministry a consultatory
charge which currently is E 0. 88 per liter of gasoline consumed. The charge
for equipment is a daily rental plus fuel cost. Disagreement exists as to
whether a Ministry or CTO should retain title to a gifted vehicle.
44.       CTO has facilities for routine servicing of automobiles and some
maintenance and overhaul facilities are available for gas-powered engines up
to 2,5000 cc., diesel engines up to 3,000 cc. and standard transmissions.
These facilities are being upgraded with the additions of specialized equip-
ment such as dynamometers. Maintenance of heavy machinery and equipment is
the responsibility of the Ministries. Nearly all CTO users claim that CTO's



ANNEX 5
Page 14
service performance is unsatisfactory. Accurate estimates are not available,
but users claim that at any one time over 50% of vehicles are laid off. In
order to improve performance, CTO policy is to reduce the average age of the
vehicles to approximately two years and that of the equipment to approximately
3-1/2 years.
45.       CTO handles most government vehicle procurement.  Any item costing
over E1,000 must be purchased through the Tender Board; the Minister of
Finance may, however, authorize direct purchases of items costing over E1,000.
The Accountant General of Swaziland is chairman of the Tender Board. In the
past, a minimum of one year has elapsed between the time of tender warrant and
delivery. A substantial majority of the suppliers have been South African.
CTO's management believe that the procurement of spare parts has been the
biggest problem they have experienced. For this reason, they have requested
contractors as part of their supply offer to enter into a two-year contract to
supply spares and service, exchange units and carry out inspections and tests
as required by CTO. The reaction to this request has been mixed: some major
suppliers have refused to enter the agreement, others have accepted it.
46.       CTO has a staff of 300 including 80 mechanics:  some positions
are unfilled. CTO employs about 20 Swazis as mechanics; the others are
Portuguese or South African. In keeping with the program of upgrading the
existing staff of skilled vehicle and plant mechanics, in service training is
being organized and CTO recruits artisans from local training establishments.
47.       CTO has three principal departments, under a general transport
manager; mechanical engineering, personnel, and financial control. CTO has
not operated efficiently in the past. Delays in procurement of vehicles and
in maintenance or repairs are lengthy, and have posed serious problems for
ministries dependent on CTO services: for example, the 1975 Ministry of
Agriculture Annual Report indicates that trucks were out of service for over
six months, seriously detracting from the effectiveness of efforts to encourage
use of fattening ranches. Renewal policies have been ineffective in the past.
A team of Canadian consultants is now studying CTO organization and problems
and will propose a program to improve services. Efforts to strengthen CTO are
also being undertaken under the aegis of IBRD-financed highway projects in
Swaziland.
Public Works Department (PWD)
48.       The Public Works Department includes a Building Branch, responsible
for construction and maintenance of government buildings, and a Roads Branch.
The Building Branch includes a Principal Building Officer, two senior archi-
tects, four architects and assistants, three engineers, a quantity surveyor,
and clerks of works at five regional offices. Works in 1975 were valued at
E3 million, of which two thirds represented new construction and one third
maintenance. An annual program is established in consultation with the
Ministry of Finance. Private contractors undertake about 3/8 of all jobs.
The Roads Branch builds and maintains all gazetted roads. An ODM-financed
project is underway to develop a District Road network.



ANNEX 5
Page 15
Statistics Office
49.       Collection and analysis of agricultural statistics are the responsi-
bility of the Chief Statistical Officer, who heads a division responsible
to the Ministry of Finance. Agricultural statistics are based on an annual
census of Freehold Title Land farms, conducted by mail, and by an annualsample
survey of Swazi Nation Land agriculture; here, enumeration is conducted in
primary sampling areas, using crop cutting experiments. The Statistical
Office conducts this survey with a staff of one statistician, one statistical
officer, and 22 enumerators; local administrators and extension workers assist
with field work.
G. Coordination of Rural Development Programs
50.       The principal organ responsible for coordination of all rural
development programs is the Cabinet. A Rural Development Committee of the
Cabinet has been established to assist in coordination. It includes permanent
secretaries of key ministries responsible for rural programs, notably Agricul-
ture, Commerce and Cooperatives, Education, Public Health, and Finance. The
Committee meets quite frequently to consider specific project and policy
proposals and problems.
51.       Coordination of inter-ministerial programs affecting rural areas
is largely informal. The most effective vehicle for coordination appears to
be a Marketing and Inputs Committee, including representatives of the Ministries
of Agriculture and Commerce and Cooperatives, and SDSB and CCU. The committee
makes decisions on policies and specific programs for marketing of produce in
RDA's and on CCU orders of agricultural inputs. Participants have extolled
its effectiveness.
52.       The appraisal mission discussed at length the Government's views
on overall rural development strategy, including coordination of on-going
programs, and long-term objectives for development of institutions and infra-
structure. On both counts, more explicit definition of problems and objectives
is required, while coordination, handled to date on a rather informal basis,
could pose serious problems in future. The wide range of rural development
activities financed by numerous donor and lending agencies (Annex 2), would
clearly benefit from improved coordination at both policy and field implemen-
tation levels. Regarding long term objectives, some of the implications of
current policies for future development have not been thought through. For
instance, RDA's will soon cover about 60% of Swazi Nation land, yet their role
in territorial administration and as a focus for infrastructure development
have not been defined; policy for areas outside RDA's also should be clarified.
These problems are crucial because the proposed Project is part of a broad
program and cannot succeed in isolation. The Government is aware of these
problems. A continuing dialogue on overall objectives for rural development
and on coordination of rural sector programs should form part of IBRD's super-
vision of the Project.
September 15, 1976

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Order by Ngwenyama in Libandla - Order No. 4 of 1954
Creating the Central Rural Development Board
The Ngwenyama, by virtue of Swazi Law and Custom and in
terms of section 9 of Proclamation No. 79 of 1950,
has been pleased to publish this Order providing for
the appointment of a Central Rural Development Board,
and setting out its functions and duties
1.        The Board appointed by the Ngwenyama, known as the Central Rural
Development Board, is hereby empowered to act in terms of this Order.
2.        The Chairman of the Board shall submit to the Ngwenyama a report
dealing with the condition of the resources, i.e., water, soil, grass and
timber, on land held by the Nation. The report shall cover among other
things -
(a)  method of settlement;
(b) present division of land into arable and grazing
areas; and
(c) present grazing control methods.
3.        The Board shall recommend to the Ngwenyama the steps to be taken
for the protection of these resources and for the improvement of grazing
control methods.
4.        Where the recommendations of the Board have been accepted by the
Ngwenyama, the Chairman with the approval of his Board may issue orders
providing for -
(a) the removal of kraals to selected sites;
(b) the division of land into arable and grazing
areas;
(c) the rotation of grazing;



ANNEX 5
Appendix 1
Page 2
(d) the burning of grass;
(e) the protection of particular catchment areas; and
(f) the removal of livestock to selected areas in the
territory.
5.        W4henever action has been taken in terms of paragraph 4 (f), it
shall be the duty of the Chief of the area concerned, to ensure that the
number of livestock in such area does not exceed the number permitted by the
Board.
6.        Any person contravening he provisions of this Order, or of the
Orders issued in terms of paragraph (4) hereof, shall be guilty of an
offence and liable on conviction to a fine not exceeding t25 or to imprison-
ment with or without hard labour for a period not exceeding six months.
BY ORDER OF THE PARAMOUNT CHIEF OF SWAZILAND
17th November, 1954.

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Training and Manpower for Rural Development
Extension Officer Training
1.        The principal source of supply of trained agricultural extension
officers (for both crop and livestock production) is the Faculty of Agri-
cultural of the University of Botswana and Swaziland (UBS), which is based at
the Luyengo campus in Swaziland. In addition to providing courses towards a
B.Sc. degree, UBS also offers a diploma in agriculture after two years of
training. In the past, the Faculty offered a one year certificate course.
However, as a result of recommendations made by an Academic Planning Mission
in 1970, the certificate course was phased out and replaced by the two year
diploma course. This recommendation, which was endorsed by Government,
was based on the opinion that the certificate training was not of a suffici-
ently high standard to produce extension personnel capable of working effec-
tively in the field.
2.        Unfortunately the output of the diploma course has been constrained
by a lack of availability of suitably qualified people (Cambridge level pass).
The result is that at present Swaziland is suffering from a considerable
shortage of extension officers. The estimated 1976 shortfall is 45 agricul-
tural extension officers. Broad estimates of the required annual intake of
students over the next five years in accordance with the FAO/SIDA funded
project (strengthening the faculty of Agriculture) to expand the present
facilities at Luyengo is as follows:

Diploma Program
General Agriculture                 10                   20-40
Animal Health & Management        6-10                    8-10
Rome Economics                    8-10                    8-10
