4.        Although it is generally agreed that a diploma level of training
is desirable for extension officers, it is apparent that the present short-
fall (and any additional shortfalls resulting from the requirements of the
proposed Project) could be met by instituting a new certificate course.
Proposed New Certificate Level Training Course
Background
5.        There is at present a critical shortage of field officer staff in
the Ministry of Agriculture. This situation is compounded by the field



ANNEX 6
Page 3
officer requirements projected under the proposed expansion of the RDA
program. The following proposals were prepared jointly by the Ministry
of Agriculture and the University of Botswana and Swaziland with a view
to ensuring an adequate supply of field officers over the next 10 years.
Training Proposals
6.        The Government proposes that the diploma course be continued as
planned and that in addition a one-year certificate course be commenced in
March 1977. The certificate course will act both as a basic training for
field staff and as a preliminary qualification for proceeding to the ongoing
diploma course. The certificate graduates will be expected to work in field
for two years. They may then be recommended to sit the introductory course
for the diploma, upon successful completion of which they will undertake two
years diploma study.
Field Officer Supply and Demand Projection (Table 1)
7.        The following assumptions were made in Table 1 projections:
(a) 50% of the projected agriculture diploma level output will be
recruited outside the Ministry of Agriculture or used within
the Ministry of Agriculture for specialist or non field officer
posts. (The proportion coming into the Ministry of Agriculture
has been lower than this in the past.)
(b) A 10% annual loss of field officer staff will be experienced.
(c) 50% of the certificate course graduates will be accepted onto
the diploma course two years after they obtain their certi-
ficate.
(d) The facilities established for the certificate course will be
used for in-service training and other short courses when
space allows. It is assumed that 10% of the total field
officer staff will be in receipt of in-service training at
any one time.
The projections in Table 1 indicate that a field officer/in training facili-
ties will be required for 35-45 students per year over the next 10 years.
8.        The commencement of the course at the earliest possible date (March
1977) will mean that an outstanding shortfall of 7 staff remain in 1977/78.
It is possible that this shortfall could be met by volunteers or alternatively
be accepted and made up in the following year.
9.        EDF has agreed to provide financing for the course, in addition to
other in-service and farmer training activities, and has funds available for
the purpose.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
10. Outline of Proposals:
(a) Minimum Entry Qualifications
Minimum entry qualifications would be Junior Certificate, that
is 10 years formal schooling.
(b) Course Duration
The course would start at the end of April, with an introduc-
tory session at UBS. Students would spend one month as
counterparts of extension officers in RDA's or other government
projects. Course work at Luyengo would begin at the end of May
and continue for a full year. The course would occupy 44 weeks
over 5 semesters of 12, 8, 8, 8 and 8 weeks respectively.
(c) Course Content
The course would be based on the former certificate course
which occupied 60 weeks. Emphasis would be placed on
practical orientation and at least 60% of course would be
spent in practical periods.
(d) Staffing
The course would require extra senior and junior staff.
One senior staff would be required to take charge of the
course. He would be assisted by 3 diplomates whose chief
responsibility would be to set up and supervise the practical
classes. All staff would, if possible, be recruited from
within Swaziland. Suitable candidates for the senior staff
positions would be officers with the qualifications and ex-
perience of the present principals of the farmer training
centers. In the event that secondment from the Ministry of
Agriculture or elsewhere was not possible technical assistance
would be required. In either case the senior staff should be
in post by January to ensure that the course is adequately
prepared.
(e) Financial Implications
A certain amount of the existing facilities would be adequate
to cater for the new course. Notably hostel accommodation
should be sufficient since the shortfall resulting from the
withdrawal of Lesotho students can be utilized. Other faci-
lities are, however, lacking. Tentative cost estimates are
summarized below:

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Credit and Cooperatives
Introduction
1.        Agricultural credit to Swazi Nation Land farmers has been available
from private sources, the Swaziland Development and Savings Bank (SDSB), and
the Cooperatives System. SDSB is principally Government owned and it lends to
the large commercial farmers as well as business and industry. A small percen-
tage of SDSB's loan portfolio consists of small seasonal loans to Swazi
farmers. The Cooperative movement is receiving increased emphasis from the
Government of Swaziland as a more practical way to increase the direct involve-
ment of the Swazi farmer in the rural development process. The expansion of
rural credit on Swazi Nation Land will have to draw on the existing resources
of SDSB and the Cooperatives System. This annex describes the existing
agricultural credit structure and makes recommendations on how it might be
strengthened to serve the increasing financial needs of the farmers on Swazi
Nation Land.
A. Swaziland Development and Savings Bank
Background and Organization
2.        The Swaziland Development and Savings Bank (SDSB) was established
in 1965 under the name of "Swaziland Credit and Savings Bank", to help meet
the financial needs of the Swazis in agriculture, small industry, housing
and commerce. The authorized share capital was R 400,000 (US$460,000).
The U.K. Colonial Development and Welfare Fund and U.S.A.I.D. contributed
SDSB's initial capital. In addition, the U.K. Exchequer has made several
medium-term loans to SDSB. The Swazi Government helped repay the U.K. loans
and deposited some of its liquid resources with SDSB to fund its operations.
A December, 1973 King's Order in Council changed the name of the Swaziland
Credit and Savings Bank to Swaziland Development and Savings Bank, reaffirmed
that the institution's main purpose was the promotion of economic development,
and raised authorized capital to R5,000,000. At March 31st, 1975 paid-up
capital was E3,000,000 (US$3.5 million). The SDSB was thus initially establi-
shed principally as an agricultural credit institution, very much under the
arm of the Ministry of Agriculture. As the institution developed, it broad-
ened its activities to other sectors. Table 1 shows SDSB financial state-
ments.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
3.        SDSB has a 13-member Board of Directors.  The Permanent Secretaries
of seven key ministries are ex-officio members; the Prime Minister appoints
one Director on recommendation of the Swazi National Executive; the Minister
of Finance appoints the remaining five Directors as well as the Chairman of
the Board. The Board appoints SDSB's General Manager, who is the Chief
Executive Officer. Whether or nor he is a member, the General Manager parti-
cipates in all Board meetings.
4.        The loan department and three support departments report to the
General Manager. The support departments are: Personnel, Branch Audit
and Financial Controls. The operations department is divided into an Agricul-
tural Loan Section and a Housing and Business Loan Section. SDSB has five
branches, located in Mbabane, Manzini, Nhlangano, Siteki and Pigg's Peak, and
three field offices in Mathanjeni, Mahlangatsha and Ntonjeni. Each branch
is staffed by a Manager, an Accountant, several tellers, loan clerks and
two to four Field Credit Officers. All branch managers are Swazis.
Credit Policies and Procedures
5.        The Agricultural Loan Section of the Loan Department handles all
agricultural loans. Interest rates are established by the Board of Directors.
SDSB charges an interest rate of 6% on seasonal loans ranging in size from E50
to E500. The maturity date of seasonal loans normally falls on September 30
of each year. On all other agricultural loans, SDSB charges the same interest
rate as on business loans, currently 10.5%. Housing loans bear interest at
7.5%. A 2% penalty rate applies to all loans in case of late payment. All
loans must be secured by hypotec or cession of hire-purchase contract.
Depending on quality, the collateral's value typically ranges from 150% to
200% of the loan proceeds to the borrower. Swazi Nation Land farmers cannot
pledge land as collateral on loans because they do not own it; cattle is the
only practical collateral for a large majority of Swazi Nation Land farmers.
Swazi farmers who own no cattle, reportedly about 30%, are therefore excluded
from credit at present.
6.        SDSB makes three to nine month cash advances against cattle entering
the Government fattening ranches. The amount financed is now set at 80% of
the value of cattle entering the ranch, and the interest rate charged is
10.5%. This was recently increased from 50% of the value of cattle. SDSB
makes seasonal loans to farmers and rural shopkeepers, five-year loans to
finance tractors and other farm equipment, ten year loans to finance forestry
operations, and cattle loans with maturities up to thirteen years; maturities
on land acquisition loans range between ten and fifteen years. SDSB often
finances up to 100% of the purchase price of land or equipment.
7.        Each Branch Manager has authority to approve loans up to E500
maturing in a year or less. The Agricultural Credit Supervisor, who is the
Senior Credit Officer in the Agricultural Loan Section, has credit authority
for loans up to E5,000. The General Manager approves loans up to E5,000, and
the Board of Directors approves loans in excess of E10,000.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
8.        The loan loss reserve is computed by doubling the five-year average
of actual write-offs. SDSB has no automatic write-off policy. Management's
;^ite-off recommendations are entirely subjective.  The Board of Directors
must approve any write-off in excess of E10.
9.        Small Seasonal Loans:  1/  In order to borrow any amount up to
E500 for farm inputs from SDSB, a Swazi farmer must be recommended by the
Field Extension Officer and by the Local Advisory Committee; 2/ his applica-
tion must then be approved by the Branch Manager. The prospective borrower's
initial contact may be established directly through the Field Extension
Officer, the SDSB Credit Advisor, a Farmers' Association, or a cooperative.
The credit decision is based on the borrower's previous record, the viability
of his farm plan, and the security offered as collateral. If the loan is
approved, the farmer receives a Purchase Authority which he can use to buy
inputs. This process is time-consuming and expensive, and the procedures are
cumbersome, including substantial paperwork and a wide range of individuals.
Long delays in the approval process are due to the non-availability of field
extension staff and the inefficiency of the Local Advisory Committees, which
must meet on each application, although they seldom know the applicants.
Meetings are infrequent, irregular and incompletely attended. SDSB's manage-
ment recommends that the Local Advisory Committees be replaced in their credit
advisory functions by the Tinkundlas, which are groups of four to seven chiefs
who meet periodically to discuss various comimunity matters (Annex 5).
10.       SDSB's management has further recommended two proposals to stream-
line credit procedures and increase small farmers' credit usage: the estab-
lishment of lines of credit, and support of the Government's policy of promo-
ting indirect lending through cooperatives. The line of credit would be
available to farmers with a satisfactory past credit record, and would normally
be based on multiples of E50 per acre, per head of cattle. The farmer would
be encouraged to estimate his total requirements for the season, which would
be the limit up to which he could draw, if the facility were approved. Within
the approved limit, the borrower would be able to draw down, repay, and redraw
for a second crop. Every crop year, he would submit a farm plan. The Local
Advisory Committee's approval would be needed only at the time of establishment
1/   The Small Farmer Credit Scheme is supported by a loan of US$750,000
approved in May, 1974 by USAID. This loan is intended for small scale
loans and carries interest at 2-1/2% to 3%, for 40 years, with 10 years
grace. Less than one third of these funds had been disbursed through
the 1975-76 season.
2/   The Local Advisory Committee comprises the District Commissioner,
Senior Extension Officer, senior cooperative officer, and two private
farmers.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
of the line of credit. Each line of credit would have a review date to
coincide with the crop being financed, and the renewal of the facility would
be conditional upon full paydown on the line of credit. Management's aim
would be to process credit line renewals within one working day. Management's
second proposal is that SDSB work to support Government efforts to develop an
indirect lending program which would utilize the cooperatives network. Under
this program, primary societies' cooperative committees would approve credit
sales, assisted by an SDSB credit adviser. The individual loans would then be
bulked by CCU which would warehouse them and fund them at SDSB; CCU would
charge the farmer 6% interest, pay SDSB 5%, assume the credit risk, and handle
most of the paper work. Some steps have already been taken to promote use of
Cooperative for small agricultural loans.
The Loan Portfolio
11.       At fiscal year end, March 31, 1975, SDSB's secured loans outstanding
net of loan loss reserve were E8,808,000 (US$10.1 million), a 28% increase
over fiscal 1974. Undisbursed commitments at March 31, 1975 were E880,000.
The loan loss reserves was E241,000 (US$277,000) or 2.74% of the oustanding
loan portfolio. E3,250,000 (US$3,736,000) or 37% of total loans was to
the agricultural sector, 30% to the business sector, 23% to finance housing
and the remaining 10% for various other purposes. Of approximately E3,000,000
(US$3.4 million) agricultural loans outstanding at March 31, 1976, about
E2,000,000 or 68% consisted of loans to the commercial sector to finance
farm purchases, forestry operations, cattle breeding, and seasonal loans
over E500. Approximately E600,000 or 20% of total agricultural loans consisted
of mechanization loans. The remaining 12%, E360,000 (US$414,000) was about
equally divided between cattle advances and seasonal loans under E500 to Swazi
farmers. The large loans are principally to expatriates, with a growing
minority of land acquisition loans to wealthier urban Swazis. Table 2 gives
further detail on the loan portfolio.
12.       SDSB has been financing tractor purchases by expatriate farmers
and wealthier Swazi farmers. Of approximately 185 tractors financed, 85
were Swaziland produced Tinkabis and the rest imported. Cattle advances
are becoming increasingly popular and are expected to further expand as the
amount that SDSB lends for each head was recently increased to 80% of cattle
value. Advances are currently outstanding against approximately 2,500 head of
cattle, representing over one third of the holding capacity of the Impala and
Lavumisa fattening ranches.
13.       During the 1975-76 season, SDSB made about 1,500 seasonal loans
not exceeding E500 (US$575) to Swazi farmers. The number of loans made in
the 1975-76 season was substantially unchanged from that of the prior season,
while the average size of loans increased from E105 in the 1974-75 season
to E125 in the 1975-76 season.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
14.       Management reports that more than 34% of the total number of loans
and 24% of the total Emalangeni amount disbursed by SDSB to Swazi Nation Land
farmers for seasonal loans are delinquent. Delinquency rates for loans made
under the Cotton Package Scheme, a program instituted in 1972 by the Ministry
of Agriculture to promote cotton production, are believed to be higher; the
Government guaranteed half the amount of each loan made under the Cotton
Package Scheme, and have indicated that they will pay SDSB for cotton loan
defaults, although they have not yet done so. SDSB management has observed
that there is a positive correlation between the ratio of loans to credit
advisers and delinquency rates. SDSB has recently overcome an initial reluc-
tance by its Board of Directors to use bill collectors. The belief was that
the practice would create a bad image for the bank and, therefore, limit
credit acceptance among Swazi farmers. Early results of the increased collec-
tion effort are reported to be encouraging.
15.       More detailed portfolio data should be available from 1977 on, upon
installation of a computerized management information system.
16.       SDSB's management believes that the Cotton Ginneries now finance
seasonally twice as much to small farmers as SDSB, charge interest rates
upwards of 14%, and experience low delinquency rates. The Ginneries sell
on credit, to their captive producers, seeds, fertilizers and chemicals,
the value of which they deduct from the proceeds of delivered cotton. The
Tobacco Cooperative finances a major portion of the tobacco producing farm-
ers at higher interest rates and lower costs than SDSB. SDSB's management
believes that the Tobacco Cooperative and the Ginneries derive their advan-
tages from their freedom from cumbersome loan approval procedures and com-
plex collateral considerations. SDSB finances primarily maize, beans,
groundnuts which are low return crops, or vegetables which are high-risk
crops; conversely SDSB's financing of high return crops like cotton, tobacco
and sugarcane, is relatively unimportant.  SDSB's management believes that
the emphasis on low return and high-risk crops is a major factor affecting
the quality of the small farmers' loan portfolio.
Staff and Training
17.       SDSB employs over 100 staff.  SDSB management believes that its
field force is of unusually high quality among Swaziland government and
commercial insitutions. All branch managers are Swazis. Some SDSB staff
have been hired away from the Ministry of Agriculture in recent years.
Salaries are competitive with the private sector and emphasis is placed
on in-service training. During fiscal year 1975, 19 new employees entered
a five-week credit training program organized by SDSB. Ten junior staff
members received refresher training as they were being prepared for higher
responsibility positions. One senior staff member is attending a three-
month banking course at the State Bank of Pakistan. A Swazi branch manager
attended a management training seminar in Sweden. Shortage of middle man-
agement level staff, however, is limiting SDSB's ability to expand, and the
problem is likely to persist for some time.



ANNEX 7
Page 6
B. Cooperatives
Background
18.       In 1963, the Ministry of Agriculture established the Department of
Cooperatives to promote the organization of cooperatives, and oversee their
operations. Initially, the Department of Cooperatives concentrated its
efforts on the conversion of existing farmers' associations into cooperative
societies. Due to limited human and financial resources, progress at first
was very slow. Larger farmers initially joined South African cooperatives,
and the earliest Swazi cooperatives comprised almost exclusively large farmers.
From 1962-64, the Department concentrated on enforcement of legislation;
cooperative extension efforts began on a pilot scale in 1964-65. Since
independence, however, the registration of cooperatives has increased substan-
tially: 14 new cooperatives were registered in the years 1963-68 and 73 in
the years 1969-75; of these, 10 and 37 respectively were farmers cooperatives.
Table 3 gives data on present registration of cooperatives and memberships.
19.       The first cooperatives were primarily seasonal input supply organi-
zations, and provided limited, albeit useful services to members. The need
for more broadly based producer cooperatives soon became apparent, largely as
a result of marketing problems.. Cooperative development was envisaged as an
essential part of the ODM-financed Rural Development Program, and the first
multi-purpose cooperatives accessible to small Swazi farmers were established
in RDA's. In 1974, a decision was made to promote multi-purpose cooperatives
throughout Swaziland, as a result of the response of farmers to cooperative
activity in RDA's. Since 1974, the Government has sought to utilize cooperatives
as a vehicle for: (a) providing inputs to farmers; (b) marketing cash crops
produced by Swazi farmers; and (c) providing credit to cooperative members.
In all three areas, development is still at a rather preliminary stage, but it
is clear that cooperatives will play an important role in future rural develop-
ment programs in all three areas. The Government of Swaziland regards the
cooperative movement as an important means for increasing the involvement of
the Swazi farmer in the economic development process and facilitating the
transition from dependence on subsistence crops to production of crops for
cash. The Government has committed significant resources to cooperative
development, and has successfully approached several multilateral and bilateral
donors for financial and technical assistance in the field of cooperative
development.
The Department of Cooperatives
20.       At the time of independence, the Department of Cooperatives became
part of the Ministry of Commerce and Cooperatives. The Commissioner for
Cooperative Development heads the Department of Cooperatives and has senior
responsibility for the organization and supervision of all cooperatives.
The Department staff consists of a Deputy Commissioner and four senior



ANNEX 7
Page 7
Cooperative Officers. Each senior Cooperative Officer is responsible for
the administration and supervision of cooperatives in each of the four
districts into which Swaziland is divided. Each senior Cooperative Officer
has a field staff of Cooperative Officers, Assistant Cooperative Officers
and a District Marketing Officer. Three support departments report to the
Deputy Commissioner; they are Marketing, Audit, and Training, and each is
headed by an Assistant Commissioner. The District Marketing officers report
to the Assistant Commissioner of marketing on technical matters, but work
closely with the senior Cooperative Officers in the districts. The Audit
department has a staff of accountants and bookkeepers who audit the
cooperative societies. The Training department will in future organize
training in the Cooperative Development center, now under construction (see
para 24).
The Central Cooperative Union
21.       As early as 1968-69, the need to coordinate cooperative activity
became apparent, and district unions of cooperatives were formed. In 1971, a
Central Cooperative Union (CCU) was registered, with membership open to all
primary societies; most have chosen to join CCU. CCU is an autonomous body,
run by an elected Board of Directors. At present, the Government provides
considerable support (financial and personnel) and close guidance. The
Department of Cooperatives audits the CCU. There is close cooperation at
the working level between Ministry and CCU staff, and CCU managers are civil
servants seconded by and paid by Government.
22.       CCU has begun to play an active role in procuring and distributing
inputs required by farmers. The Ministries of Agriculture and Commerce and
Cooperatives, CCU and SDSB are represented on a National Farm Inputs and
Marketing Committee; the Committee decides every year quantities of farm
inputs to buy for the entire nation. CCU places bulk orders to supply the
inputs with the Swaziland Milling Corporation, a private trading company,
which purchases the inputs in South Africa. SDSB finances CCU's inventory
purchases at their usual business terms; the Government guarantees CCU's
borrowings. The suppliers deliver the inputs to nine main depots run by CCU
located throughout Swaziland. Inputs are sold for cash or purchase authorities
to individual farmers at the main depots. The main depots sell to cooperative
members as well as non-members. Cooperative members get a discount of approxi-
mately 3% off the sales price of inputs. The primary societies distribute
inputs to their members, who generally pay cash. The Cooperatives sell a
small amount of inputs on credit. CCU plans to play the same national coordi-
nating role for crop marketing as it now does for input supplies.
23.       CCU stands in need of improvement of its management and controls.
Current data on CCU finances and operations is incomplete and unreliable.
CCU's management hopes to produce shortly satisfactory financial statements
for the fiscal year ended May 31, 1976. Financial information for control and
reporting purposes has been insufficient to date. The total inventory value
in the nine main depots at May 31, 1976 was estimated by CCU at E271,000



ANNEX 7
Page 8
(US$311,000). Sales at the nine main depots for the year ended May 31, 1976
were estimated by CCU at E162,000 (US$186,000); this figure does not include
inter-cooperative sales. Delays in the delivery of supplies were believed to
be the primary cause for the relatively high level of unsold inventory.
Purchasing mistakes, transportation breakdowns, and the unavailability of
timely farmers' credit may also be causes for the inefficient merchandising
of farm inputs.
C. Assistance to Cooperatives and Agricultural Credit
24.       Bilateral and multilateral assisance has been made available to
Swaziland's cooperative movement in the areas of training, finance and
marketing. Major assistance activities are summarized below:
(a) ILO/SIDA Project: This project has provided funds for the
establishment of a Cooperative Development Center in Mbabane
and technical assistance. The building complex, which is
expected to be completed in 1976, will be the headquarters of
the Department of Cooperatives as well as the training center
for CCU and Department of Cooperatives staff. The project
investments cover staff and operating costs for the first
three years including training and salaries of the primary
cooperative societies' secretary-managers.
(b) 0DM Loan: The UK's Overseas Development Ministry (ODM)
has agreed to make available to CCU a E500,000 (US$575,000)
loan at concessionary terms. The loan will replace an over-
draft of E416,000 outstanding to SDSB and provide CCU with
the working capital it needs to support its input supply
operations.
(c) Part of the USAID loan to SDSB for small farmers credit
(para 9) may be employed to assist CCU operations. USAID
has agreed in principle that SDSB could utilize this facility
to fund advances to CCU under a line of credit for the
purchase of inputs.
(d) ODM has agreed to provide a technical assistant to serve as
General Manager of CCU for three years.
(e) A cooperative marketing project currently under negotiation
with USAID would provide technical assistance, training and
inputs to assist the Ministry of Commerce and Cooperatives
and CCU in creating a cooperative-based marketing system for
small farmers. Over four years a USAID staff of eleven would
execute the project and train Swazi staff. The Swazi staff
would then take over and the USAID staff act as advisors for two
additional years.



ANNEX 7
Page 9
D. Analysis and Conclusions
Credit Usage
25.       SDSB is effectively the only insitutional source of seasonal lend-
ing to the small Swazi farmers. As Table 4 indicates, total bank loans and
advances outstanding to the agricultural sector in Swaziland were E21,069,000
(US$24.2 million) on March 31, 1976; small seasonal loans outstanding to Swazi
farmers were less than 1% of the total, approximately E180,000 (US$200,000).
These figures illustrate dramatically the dualism of Swaziland's economy;
Swazi farmers on Swazi Nation Land make up 70% of the country's population,
yet they are virtually excluded from institutional credit. Other sources of
agricultural credit are available to small farmers. The more important non-
bank sources are the Cotton Ginneries and the Cooperative system. Estimates
of the amount of credit made available from these sources range from E400,000
to E600,000 (US$460,000-690,000). No other significant sources of credit to
small farmers on Swazi Nation Land are apparent. A rough estimate of small
farmers' credit usage on Swazi Nation Land would be in the neighborhood of
E700,000 (US$805,000).
26.       Most farmers appear now to choose, or are forced, to purchase inputs
for cash. Of CCU total sales of inputs, about 73% involved seasonal credit.
However, CCU's share of the input market on Swazi Nation Land is estimated at
about one third, with the remainder dominated by 'Farm Chemicals', an affiliate
of Swaziland Milling Company. Data showing what percentage of Farm Chemicals
sales is to commercial farmers as opposed to Swazi Nation Land farmers is not
available. Swazi Nation Land farmers probably account for less than half Farm
Chemicals sales. In summary, farmers on Swazi Nation Land probably use credit
to finance 50% to 60% of total inputs purchases; allowing a margin of error
due to poor data, Swazi Nation Land farmers' current usage is likely to be in
the neighborhood of E750,000 to finance purchases of about E1,500,000 worth of
farm inputs.
27.       The incremental input demand during the five years of implementation
of the proposed Project can be estimated at about E110,000 per year, or
E550,000 by the year five. Thus, a conservative estimate of incremental input
credit demand by Swazi Nation Land farmers would be approximately E85,000 per
year during the project years or E425,000 in five years.
SDSB - Evaluation
28.       SDSB has been in operation for over ten years.  They are experienced
in agricultural lending and have developed substantial technical and credit
expertise. The quality of the management information systems is fair and
is in the process of 'eing upgraded. SDSB has produced audited financial
statements for several years; they show management's ability to sustain
considerable institutional growth while maintaining profitability and a
satisfactory financial condition. While the ultimate assessment of the
quality of the loan portfolio would depend on its analysis, which is beyond
the scope of this report, the lending policies appear conservative and the
loans adequately collateralized. Liquidity at March 31, 1975 was high. Table
1 shows a summary of financial statements for the four fiscal years ended
1975. SDSB has a well qualified and trained staff.



ANNEX 7
Page 10
29.       Among Swazis, SDSB appears to have a commercial bank image. This
may simply reflect the attitudes of rural poor towards money and banking; it
may also arise from a largely expatriate top management or what are viewed
by the public as onerous security requirements. SDSB's management hopes to
solve the security related problems with the passage of legislation introduc-
ing the 'deed of hypothecation' (chattel mortgage). Farmers, now believe that
the value of security SDSB requires is high in relation to the amount lent.
SDSB's small loans approval process is lengthy and cumbersome. SDSB's small
seasonal loans have suffered from a lack of flexibility to suit borrower's
needs: (i) The September 30 maturity date is arbitrary and may not coincide
with the particular crop being financed, and (ii) the computation of interest,
6% flat, does not reflect the amount actually borrowed or the period the loan
is outstanding.
30.       SDSB has the general expertise to administer a successful agricultural
lending program to Swazi Nation Land farmers and any further institutional
expansion of agricultural credit to small farmers should draw on this expertise.
Small seasonal loans, however, represent about 2% of SDSB's total loans
and, consequently, cannot now be a high management priority. This lack of
focus explains in part why, in spite of a very low interest rate (6%), SDSB
satisfies less than one third of the Swazi Nation Land farmers estimated usage
of small seasonal credit. SDSB management supports a substantial increase in
interest rates to reflect the cost of administering credit.
31.       Interest Rate Policy:  The Government has directed SDSB to charge 6%
interest on seasonal input loans up to E500. It appears that the full finan-
cial implications of this directive were not considered at the time of the
decision. In fact, effective interest rates have been higher because SDSB
charges the 6% interest on a 'flat' basis regardless of the actual amount
drawn down and the period a loan was outstanding. It is nevertheless clear
that interest revenues do not cover the cost of the small farmers' credit
and SDSB now accepts that the more profitable business loans support the
small seasonal loans losses. SDSB should determine the real cost to SDSB
of the small seasonal lending program, and provide such information to the
Government. The Government should then determine explicitly how the cost
must be shared among the-Government, SDSB and the Swazi farmers.
Cooperatives - Evaluation
32.       The cooperatives' main advantage over SDSB in agricultural credit
expansion is their familiarity to the Swazi farmer. Cooperative members
know each other personally. The current loan approval procedure, though not
necessarily faster than SDSB's, is simpler. When a cooperative lends to a
member, it accepts personal guarantees of other members as security. The
Secretary Manager of a Cooperative has the right to seize cattle of defaulting
borrowers. However, only members may borrow from a cooperative, and membership
is only about 4% of Swazi Nation Land farmers. The cooperatives' funds are
limited, and the cooperatives do not have yet the financial and management
expertise necessary to administer a credit program. Proper bookkeeping and
record keeping procedures have not yet been instituted. Secretary - Managers



ANNEX 7
Page 11
require training. CCU's central coordinating and support role has not yet
been clearly established. As a result, cooperatives have provided very little
credit to small farmers, and virtually none to those who do not have access to
other credit sources. In order to play a more significant role in agricultural
credit in the near future, the cooperatives will need to draw on the technical
expertise of institutions such as SDSB. In addition, some modifications in
credit policies or law may be required to enable cooperatives to borrow from
financial institutions on the basis of the collective guarantee of its members.
Recommendations
33.       Swazi Nation Land farmers currently use approximately E700,000
in small seasonal input credit. The sources of this credit are: (i) about
60% private, primarily cotton ginners; (ii) about 26% SDSB; and (iii) about
14% the cooperatives. Cumulative incremental demand for this type of credit
would be about E425,000 over the five Project years. These figures are
estimates extrapolated from incomplete data. The Cotton Ginners charge 14%
interest, and more for credit; the cooperatives charge 10% to 12%, and SDSB
charges 6% nominal interest rate; effective SDSB rates charges may be higher.
34.       SDSB has the management and credit expertise necessary to channel
small farmers' credit. However, they do not relate to the Swazi farmers
as closely as the cooperatives. SDSB approval processes are cumbersome and
their lending conditions are not flexible enough to meet the farmers' needs.
The cooperatives have simpler approval and security procedures and are more
familiar to the Swazi farmers. They are not well equipped, however, to
supervise and control borrowers and administer credit programs. Therefore,
cooperation between SDSB and cooperatives in strengthening small farmer credit
will be essential.
35.       To build on the existing strengths of both institutions would
offer the best chances of success to the small seasonal lending program on
Swazi Nation Land. The following proposals are recommended by the mission:
(a) SDSB should undertake a study to gain better knowledge and
understanding of small seasonal credit needs on Swazi
Nation Land. The aim should be to meet a substantial por-
tion of the total demand, in order to reduce the overhead
cost per loan;
(b) SDSB should more clearly identify human and financial re-
sources now committed to small farmers' credit. A separate
department, separate bookkeeping, separate revenue and ex-
pense records, separate assets and liabilitias management
would provide better cost information to management. On the
basis of that information, management could make more rational
decisions on the pricing of loans and recommendations to the
Government on the extent of subsidies;



ANNEX 7
Page 12
(c) In order to insure the timely availability of credit in the
RDA's, the basic credit infrastructure should consist of
field staff, vehicles, and housing. Credit advisors would
generate new loan business, and advise the Ministry of
Agriculture extension staff and the cooperatives' secretary
managers on all credit matters; they would oversee loan
collection. Credit Advisors' housing could function as
a rural mini-branch or loan production office. Credit
Assistants could be model customers and their main purpose
would be credit demonstration and education of other
farmers;
(d) Close coordination with the cooperative system would enhance
SDSB's credit penetration among farmers. At the central le-
vel, SDSB should share with CCU its general knowledge of
the small farmers' credit sector and provide technical as-
sistance to CCU in implementing and monitoring the direct
lending operations of the cooperatives. At the field le-
vel, each credit advisor should be assigned to three to
six cooperatives as a consultant on credit matters, and
should visit them periodically to offer his assistance;
(e) Much of the success of the RDA program depends on timely
provision and use of inputs. As a substantial amount of
inputs would be purchased on credit, the extension offi-
cers should insure that farmers secure the necessary cre-
dit at the appropriate time;
(f) At least once a year, SDSB should report to the Ministries
of Agriculture and Finance and Planning on small farmers'
loans, specifying number of loans, amounts outstanding,
maturities, interest charged, past due loans, write-offs,
a detailed account of revenues and expenses of its small
loans department. The report should also include its
analysis of the sector as a whole, a budget for the fol-
lowing year with a recommendation on the interest rate to
be charged. The Government, in approving or rejecting
the interest rate recommendation, should indicate how the
budget deficit, if any, would be covered.
November 22, 1976

ANNEX 8

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Project Area
1.        This annex describes the ten rural development areas (RDA's) where
development under the proposed Project would be concentrated. The definition
and concept of rural development areas are also discussed. Further, a brief
description of RDA's where ODM-financed activities are underway or planned is
presented. The map shows limits and location of all current and proposed RDA's.
A. Rural Development Areas
2.        The Swaziland Government's rural development programs are effectively
limited to Swazi Nation Land areas; rural development areas (RDA's) are areas
of Swazi Nation Land designated for intensive development effects. To date,
definition and selection of RDA's has been the responsibility of the land plan-
ning section of the Ministry of Agriculture. Areas selected have been those
with heavy concentration of population and good development potential; further,
areas with good communications and coherent physical configuration have been
sought. A population of about 15,000 has been viewed as optimal, and evidence of
interest by the population in development a further desirable trait.
3.        Development programs are underway in four RDA's, covering about 6%
of communally held Swazi Nation Land. In each area, a project manager admin-
isters development activities, supervises Ministry of Agriculture staff, and
coordinates (to a varying extent) activities of other Ministries. An addi-
tional 10 RDA's have been defined. In total, the 14 areas would cover about
60% of communally held Swazi Nation Land, leaving about 40% of these areas
not directly involved in the Ministry of Agriculture's intensive development
programs. The Government proposes that 8 of the new RDA's be designated
"minimum input" or "low input" areas, signifying that in the initial phase
development activities will be more restricted than in "maximum input" or
"intensive" RDA's. The proposed Project would include these 8 RDA's and two
areas designated for intensive development. In both intensive RDA's, some
land use planning has already been undertaken, and in one, Mpolonjeni,
significant development activities focused on livestock were undertaken in the
past, although they have slowed in recent years because funds expired.
4.        RDA's have no legal or official administrative status, except as
agricultural administrative units. RDA's do not follow administrative,
political or geographic boundaries. Swazi Government officials describe RDA's
as an administrative unit which is not intended as a permanent jurisdiction.
Rather, they perceive RDA's as a mechanism for developing services and infra-
structure, which would then revert to the normal administrative hierarchy,
under the jurisdiction of field organizations of responsible ministries.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
B. Rural Development Areas Included in the Project
5.        Ten RDA's comprise the Project Area.  They are as follows:

Further details are given in Table 1.  Table 2 summarizes the current estimated
cropping pattern. A description of characteristics of each RDA is given below:
(i) Lubombo-Mpolonjeni
6.        General:  The RDA is located in two ecological zones - on the Lubombo
plateau, and in the eastern Lowveld (Mpolonjeni). The Lubombo area includes
all Swazi Nation Land on the plateau and is subdivided into four sections (see
map): Lomahasha section, Majombeni section, Sitataweni section, and Tikuba
section. Mpolonjeni stretches south of the main Siteki-Manzini road, bounded
on the east by the Foot and Mouth Cordon and the west by Freehold Title Land.
There are eleven chiefdoms within the RDA.
7.        Holdings:  About 4,296 hectares in Mpolonjeni and 3,863 hectares in
Lubombo are classified as individual holdings. Communal land covers 35,487
hectares in Mpolonjeni and 55,042 ha. in Lubombo. Settlement in Lubombo is
concentrated on the arable lands, located on the western fringe of the plateau.
Population pressure and shortage of arable land in Lubombo are problems.
8.        Topography:  There are three relief zones:  the Lowveld zone, rang-
ing between 160 and 285 m. with undulating topography; the Lubumbo escarpment,
rising sharply from the Lowveld to approximately 750 m.; and the Lubombo
plateau, ranging in altitude from 500 to 750 m, with undulating topography.
The Lowveld zone is essentially comprised of Karoo basalt, a basic volcanic
rock which weathers relatively easily. The Lubombo plateau is formed from
rhyolite which is more resistant to weathering.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
9.        Climate:  Climate differs significantly between the two zones, as
rainfall increases about 25 mm. for every 30 meters increase in altitude.
Average annual rainfall at Wisselrode (155 m. asl.) is 533 mm, and at
Siteki (653 m. asl.) 864 mm. Annual variability of rainfal increases
as annual rainfall decreases. A figure of 600 mm of summer rain is the
estimated lower limit for successful commercial maize and bean production;
irrigation is thus required for these crops in Mpolonjeni. Annual mean tem-
perature at Wisselrode is 21.80C and at Siteki 19.40C.
10.       Soils 1/:  The association of soils found in the Mpolonjeni area
differs from the Lumbombo plateau. Soils in Mpolonjeni consist of red, brown
and black clay loams and clays of the R, C (LV), K, and V sets, with large
areas of very shallow dry loams of the S (LV) set. Soils in the area have
been surveyed at the semi-detailed level by I.C. Baillie (1968). The red
and brown clay loams and clays of the 'R' and 'C (LV)' sets are neutral to
slightly acid soils, which average between 50 and 80 cms. in depth. The
underlying basalt is usually weathered and permeable allowing a further 20
cms. for rooting. Soil structure is generally well developed and profiles
generally moderately well drained (the brown clays being less permeable than
the red clays). The soils are nomally 90% base saturated, with calcium dom-
inating the exchange sites. Brown clays often have a calcereous subsoil. The
black clays are characterized by a black montmorillonitic clay texture with a
well developed vertisolic features. The 'K' set averages between 70 - 90 cms.
in depth, whilst the 'V' Set profiles are generally over 150 cms. Soil struc-
ture is well developed often with a surface "mulch". Drainage is imperfect,
but downward drainage is facilitated by deep cracks. The soils are highly
base saturated with increasing amounts of sodium, particularly in the lower
lying 'V' Set soils. In their natural condition, they are only slightly
saline. The widespread shallow 'S(LV)' Set soils are shallow varients of the
red and brown clay loams and clays. Profiles are less than 35 cms. deep to
the underlying basalt. The basalt is often soft and weathered permitting an
additional 10 - 20 cms. of rooting medium. The soils have been known to grow
good sugar cane and reasonable cotton yields have been obtained under irriga-
tion (but with frequent and small water applications). Low total available
moisture capacity is a major limitation. The 'L' set red clay loams and the
'S(HV)' shallow red clay loams of the Lubombo are derived from deeply weathered
rhyolite. They are slightly acid (pH 6.0), well structured, and moderately to
well drained soils. The 'L' Set averages 80 - 120 cms. in depth and overlies
soft weathered rhyolite. Base saturation is about 60%.
11.       Land Use Potential:  Potential land use in the RDA has been classified
as follows:
(a) Dryland/irrigated cropping: 15,093 hectares; land is well
suited to rainfed cotton in Mpolonjeni and a cotton/maize
rotation in the Lubombo. Continuous cropping is possible
using correct fertilizer recommendations and soil moisture
conservation measures. Mean maize yields from demonstration
plots (1957-68) on the Lomahasheni clay loans were 1800 Kg/Ha;
1/   Appendix 1 summarizes soil set characteristics.



ANNEX 8
Page 4
however, these yields were obtained mainly from open pollinated
maize varieties except during the latter years, and the Loco
variety of cotton. The new improved varieties of maize (e.g.,
SR 52) and cotton (Albacala) should give higher yields.
(b) Dryland cropping: requiring more intensive soil conservation
measures. 11,640 hectares. This land is significantly
lower in its production potential because of limitations
of total soil moisture capacity and rooting depth, or
because of difficulties of working the soil (heavy clay
texture);
(c) Grazing areas: 50,678 hectares. This land is suitable for
extensive grazing only. Soils are very shallow and slopes
are generally steep (between 15 - 40%). Occasional rock
outcrops occur. Carrying capacities of the pastures are
estimated at 1 Livestock Unit to 4 hectares in Mpolonjeni
(including arable land) and 1 per 2.65 hectares on the
Lubombo;
(d) Other land: 27,178 hectares. This land comprises the very
steep and rocky slopes of the Lubombo gorges and escarpment.
In the deep gorges there are areas of very dense indigenous
forest. The livestock carrying capacity is very low and it
is recommended that the areas of forest be fenced off and
conserved because of their high scientific value.
12.       Availability of orthophotomaps, aerial photographs and maps:  No
orthophotomaps are yet available for the RDA. The latest aerial photography
available is the 1971 national coverage at a scale of 1:30,000. The western
and central parts of Mpolonjeni are covered by photography at a scale of
1:15,000. Enlargements of the 1:30,000 aerial photograph at a scale of
1:15,000 were produced for the Nation Census (1976).
13.       Land Utilization and Production Patterns:  Land used for holdings in
Mpolonjeni amounts to 10.8% of the area or 4,296 hectares; that on the Lubombo
amounts to only 6.4% of the area or 3,763 hectares. Land utilization is shown
below:
Cropland               Fallow        Woodland    Other

The proportion under fallow in the Lowveld is the highest of all regions,
whilst in the Lubombo it is the lowest; this illustrates the higher land
pressure on the Lubombo. Area under individual crops is estimated as follows:

14.       Pasture and Livestock:  The Mpolonjeni area has been classified as
Dry Acacia savanna. In its natural state, it is mostly open parkland of Acacia
nigrescens, Schlerosaya caffra, with thickets of Acacia gillettiae on the
black clays along dry watercourses. Grasses include Themeda triandra
variety trachyopathea and Panicum maximum. The areas of the old Mpolonjeni
and Ngeina R.D.A. were fenced off into five grazing areas in 1968. A well
organized system of rotational grazing exists. The pastures are classed as
"sweet". On the Lubombo, vegetation is classified as mixed bush and savanna.
Pastures range from sweet to moderately sour. Some fencing of grazing areas
was done in the 1940's, but most is in a poor condition. Overgrazing has
occurred in some areas. In the deep ravines, dense indigenous forest
occurs with Podocarpus sPp. dominating in parts. Total cattle numbers were
estimated as follows in 1974:

The area available for grazing in Mpolonjeni is estimated at 33,737 hectares;
pastures are present carrying 1 A.U. per 3.04 ha. Tikuba has approximately
23,965 ha. of good grazing and 19,687 ha. of poor grazing. Stataweni has
2,600 ha. of good grazing land, and 1,600 ha. of poor grazing land. Good
pastures are at present carrying 1 A.U. per 1.96 hectares. The Lomahasha/
Majambeni block has 4,150 ha. of good grazing and 4,090 ha. of poor grazing
land. Good pastures are at present carrying 1 A.U. to 0.91 hectares.
15.       Communications:  Mpolonjeni is served by the main Siteki - Big Bend
road and by the district road from Mplonjeni to Phuzamoyo, as well as a road
linking the Engeina area east of the Nkutshane Stream. A track links Magwanya
to the top of the Lubombo near Tikuba; it is in bad condition during the sum-
mer rains. The Lubombo foot and mouth cordon road runs the whole length of
the bottom of the Lubombo scarp. On top of the Lubombo a district road
links the Tikuba area. A district road links Siteki with Statweni. There is
no direct link on the Lubombo with Siteki and the northern sectors of Lomahash
and Majambani. The main road to Lomahasha from Manzini links these areas to
Siteki. Two district roads link Lomahasha and Majambeni with the main road.
The nearest railhead for Tikuba and Mpolonjeni is Mpaka and that for Lomahasha
is Mlaula. This single track line links Manzini and Maputo. Bus services
link Lomahasha, Tikuba, Mpolonjeni and Stataweni with Siteki and Manzini.
16.       Social Services:  Four secondary schools serve the RDA, two located
in Siteki, one at Lomahasha and one at Ebenezer Mission. Mpolonjeni has
11 primary schools, Tikuba 9, Stataweni 2, and Lomahasha 4. There
are five rural health clinics in the area, with two additional clinics in
Siteki itself. They are served by the Good Shepherd Hospital and Health
Center in Siteki, which has a capacity of 100 beds. Clinics are located in
Lomahasha, Shewala, Ngoina, Ebenezer and a new clinic recently opened at
Tikuba. The Good Shepherd Hospital has expanded its public health and hy-
giene education activities with visits to clinics and schools throughout the
Lumbombo District by a Public Health Team. Particular aspects covered are
child care, nutrition, hygiene, and misunderstandings of baby food advertise-
ments.
(ii) Mahamba-Zombodze
17.       General:  The RDA is situtated on the south-western border of Swazi-
land, in the Nhlangano sub-district of the Shiselweni district. It is bounded
in the north-west by the Mkhondo river; in the north by freehold title land
on the outskirts of Nhlangano town; in the east by the Mhlosheni Hills (most
of which are also freehold title land), and in the south and south-west by the
border with the Republic of South Africa. Nhlangano, the nearest town, lies
three kilometres beyond the northern boundary; Manzini is 102 kilometres from
Nhlangano. The area includes parts of the following Chiefdoms - Jabulani,
Dluvunga, Embilaneni, KaZombodze, Eugwenyameni, Emampondeweni, Esikhoseni
(Mtombe). Population is unevenly distributed, areas of moderate to high
density alternate with areas where the population is low or even non-existent.



ANNEX 8
Page 7
18.       Map and Airphoto Cover:  The area is covered by 1:50,000 scale
sheets, 1:5,000 scale orthophotomaps (1973), 1:15,000 contact prints, and
1:30,000 contact prints.
19.       Topography:  The RDA falls into four sub-regions:
(a) The Mozane River lowlands area: the Mozane rises in the
vicinity of Mahamba and flows south-east along the inter-
national boundary before entering the Republic. The south-
eastern part of the RDA is drained by the Nyamane River and
its tributaries which eventually joins the Mozane south of
the border zone. Along the Mozane, the lowland forms a
relatively narrow strip between the international boundary
and the hills to the north (in the vicinity of Nhlangano).
To the south-east, in the midsection of the Nyamane catch-
ment, the lowland area is more extensive and includes parts
of the lower Nyamane and Zombodze districts and most of the
Chibidze district;
(b) The central uplands: to the west and south of Nhlangano is
an area of highveld of between 1070 and 1200 meters; slopes
are generally moderate to gentle;
(c) The Mkhondo River Valley: the south-eastern side of the
Mkhondo valley forms the north-western "arm" of the RDA.
Slopes are moderate to gentle with steep slopes being con-
fined to the Jabulani and Luphuveni districts in the north;
(d) Mahamba Hill: is a small isolated, steep sided ridge
trending in a north-west-south-east direction; altitude
reaches 1285 meters at the highest point.
20.       Climate:  There are no meteorological stations inside the RDA, but
records are available for Nhlangano; average annual rainfall for Nhlangano for
the period 1934-1970 was 856 mms. The drought hazard (i.e., the percentage of
years in which less than 500 mm's rainfall can be expected) is estimated at
between 40% and 60% throughout the area, with the exception of the highveld
enclave in the west, where it is approximately 35%.
21.       Soils:  Soils are derived from two main types of parent rock in the
area. In the Mozane lowlands, sandstone, shale and karoo coal predominate
with the soils adjacent to the river and its tributaries being of the I set
and G set classification. 1/ In the south-east (Chibidze) A set soils predomi-
nate in the interfluves. In the upland areas where coarse-grained granite is
dominant, the U set soils predominate with small pockets of M, Q, 0 and JH sets
also occurring.
1/  Appendix 1.



ANNEX 8
Page 8
22.       Water Resources:  The RDA lies within the catchments of two main
rivers - the Mkhondo, which forms the north-western boundary and the Mozane
to the south. Larger tributaries to these rivers rise in the uplands around
Nhlangano and include the Mlambomude (Mkhondo) and the Mbukwane, riatimatima,
Ngidangoma and Nyamane (Mozane). All the rivers and most of the streams are
perennial. There is marshy land in the vicinity of the Mozane and its
tributaries.
23.       Land Use Potential:  According to the Land Capability Map of Swazi-
land (Murdoch, 1968) the RDA is classified as follows: -
Capability                          Area
(hectares)
Dryland or irrigated cropping                     5,122
Dryland cropping, more intensive soil
conservation measures required                    7,442
Wetland, winter grazing only                      2,418
Permanent grazing                                 4,828
Total       19,810
More detailed surveys will be required before detailed land use planning can
be carried out. Table 2 shows areas under various crops.
24.       Pasture and Livestock:  Grazing land available in summer months
amounts to approximately 14,021 hectares. The most extensive pasture type is
Upland Tall Grassveld with Highland Sourveld occurring on the highveld areas -
Mahamba hill and the central uplands. Upland tall grassveld is found in
the Nhlangano area. Grassland is sour and fairly open, and the main grass
species are Tristachya hispida and Hyparrhenia filipendula which provide
approximately eight months of palatable and nutritious grazing each year.
Highland sourveld consists of open, fairly short grassland dominated by
Themeda triandra (Rooigras) which is very sour and provides only six months of
useful grazing. Total cattle numbers were estimated at 14,571 in addition to
5,244 goats and 2,246 sheep. Recommended stocking rates are one A.U. to
2.65 hectares, compared with the present rate of one A.U. to 1.06 hectares;
the area is thus seriously overstocked, with soil erosion, malnutrition, and
poor calving rates a result. In small areas the situation has been alleviated
by use of unutilized freehold title farms for grazing.
25.       Communications:  Gravel roads link Nhlangano with the South African
border at Mahamba (15 km), Piet Retief (a further 35 km), Hlatikulu (27
km) and Manzini (75 km beyond). A district road constructed in 1972 gives
access to the central part of the RDA.



ANNEX 8
Page 9
26.       Social Services:  In 1972 there were 19 primary schools in the area
enrolling 5,249 pupils. There are three secondary schools. A clinic and
British Red Cross Society Center are located in Nhlangano, and a new clinic has
recently opened in the RDA itself.
27.       Extension Services:  There is one all-purpose field officer and two
tobacco field officers in the RDA, and one domestic science demonstrator.
There are four small fertilizer depots. Four cooperative societies are regis-
tered and there are two credit unions.
(iii) Bekinkosi-Mliba
28.       General:  Situated in northeast Manzini District, between the White
and Black Umbuluzi Rivers, the RDA is bounded on the west and east by freehold
title land. Luve, focal point of the area, is 32 km. from Manzini. Five
chiefdoms cover the RDA. Settlement is relatively uniform with higher densities
on good land of Bekinkosi and near Luve.
29.       Topography:  Two main topographic features characterize the area:
the dolerite Lutortya Hills, approximately 12 kilometers in length and running
in a north-south direction, and undulating land to the east and west of the
Lutortya Hills.
30.       Climate:  Mean annual rainfall decreases from about 800 mm in the
west, to 700 mm in the east. Foyers is representative of the western section,
and Mpisi the eastern section. Aspect appears to be more important than
altitude, with southeast facing slopes generally receiving more rain.
Temperatures drop in June and July when occasional ground frosts may occur in
the lower river valleys.  The annual mean temperature is about 22 C, with a
high of 26 C in January-February and a low of 15 C in June-July.
31.       Soils:  The Bekinkosi area has been surveyed at a semi-detailed
level "- ,uirdoch (1966) and the remaining area at reconnaissance level by
Murdoch (1968). There are three major soil types in the area:
(a) Deep red loams found in the Bekinkosi area, consisting
of deep, well-drained acid sandy loams to Kaolinitic
clays. The soils are highly leached and of the 'M set. 1/
If fertilizer recommendations are followed, yields of up
to 5,000 kgs of maize, 1,000 kgs of cotton, and 1,500 kgs
of shelled groundnuts, per hectare, can be attained.
(b)  Deep sandy soils:  these are either grey, pink or red
loamy sands to sandy clay loams. The grey soils (E set)
are excessively permeable with low moisture retention
capacity. The pink sandy loams (J set) and red sandy
1/   Appendix 1.



ANNEX 8
Page 10
loams (L set) are more fertile and have better moisture
holding capacity. Yields should approach 90% of the M
set on the J set soils, and 100% of the M set on the L
set soils. Yields on the E set soils can only be expected
to be 40% of the HI set potential.
(c) Shallow soils: grey, sandy loams overlying decomposed
gneiss (Orrin series). These soils are used at the Luve
Research Plot and provided rainfall is adequate, yields
should approach 90% of the M set soils. Soil erosion is
a problem.
32.       Water Resources;  The Black Umbuluzi which forms the northern
boundary is the main perennial river in the RDA; the White Umbuluzi tra-
verses only a small part of the area in the south. River tributaries only
discharge during the September - June period. A number of small dams are
located on tributaries of the Black and White Umbuluzi Rivers, the largest
on the Stokoto Stream with a capacity of 2.47 million cu. meters; however,
this dam is located at the western edge of the area, with freehold title land
downstream of the dam. Groundwater potential is thought to be moderately
good. The Department of Geological Survey has recently carried out a ground-
water survey in the area.
33.       Land Utilization and Production Pattern:  Of the estimated 4,000
hectares of arable land, 3,244 hectares are under crops and 584 fallow. Maize
is the principal crop and cotton increases in importance in the lower rainfall
areas. Table 2 shows areas under crops.
34.       Pasture and Livestock:  Vegetation is classified as dry, tall
grassveld and consists of fairly open grassland, with local concentrations of
Dichrostachys savanna woodland. Scattered trees such as Ficus spp and Sclerocarya
birrea ("umnganu") are common. The most common grasses are Hypehemnia spp,
but in the overgrazed areas shorter species such as Aristida congesta spp
barbicollis, Heterepogen contortus and Cynodon dactylon occur. The pastures
are regarded as moderately sour, with a carrying capacity (in good condition)
of approximately 1 A.U. per 3.2 hectares during the 8 month growing season.
There are an estimated 21,075 cattle in the RDA (1974 Cattle Census),
in addition to about 1,235 sheep and 9,057 goats. Present stocking rate is
estimated at 1 A.U. per 1.25 hectares. The recommended maximum stocking rate
is 8,766 A.U., as opposed to 18,587 at present. The bull-cow ratio is 1:9.5.
35.       Communications:  One main all-weather gravel road, in good condi-
tion, dissects the area, and there are three minor district roads. All are
maintained by the roads branch of PWD. The nearest rail head is Matsapa, 40 km.
from Luve.
36.       Social Services:  The RDA is served by two secondary schools.  There
are eight primary schools, with 1,321 pupils in total; five schools are run by
missions, three by the community. The 1966 census recorded 4,534 school age
children (5-19) in the area. There are three clinics, two government and one
mission. There are four small fertilizers depots.



ANNEX 8
Page 11
(iv) Mahanga-Hluti
37.       General:  Situated south of the Ngwampisi River, the RDA is bounded
by the Swaziland-South African border in the south and freehold title land in
the east and west. The RDA is divided into two portions by a narrow band of
freehold title land, and the northern portion is futher subdivided by freehold
title land. Settlement is concentrated along river valleys, with secondary
concentrations near Hluti, Sivulu plateau, and along the main Mhloshemi -
Lavumisa road.
38.       Topography:  There are two main topographic regions: Middleveld
plateau remnants bordered by steep river valleys with tributaries leading to
the Ngwavuma River in the north and the Pongola River in the south, and gently
sloping land in the east of the region leading to the Ngwavuma and Sitolo
Rivers.
39.       Climate:  The RDA is situated in the transition zone between the
Middleveld and Lowveld, with rainfall decreasing with altitude. Slope
aspect controls rainfall to a large degree. Drought hazard is greater in
the higher rainfall areas. Rainfall averages between 700 and 850 mm.
40.       Soils:  The area was surveyed at reconnaissance level (Murdoch,
1968) and three soil groups identified:
(a) Granite derived soils, generally acid, free draining loams
of poor chemical and physical fertility. Response to
fertilizer and lime applications is not always noticeable;
(b) Dolerite derived soils which consist of red, moderately
acid clay loams of good physical structure. Where drainage
is restricted, black soils of low permeability have developed;
(c) Soils derived from Ecca sandstone and shale. These have a
marked duplex profile with a slightly acid sandy top soil
overlying a dense clay pen. Some of these have been classi-
fied as "H" set and others as "Z" set soils. 1/ The "H" set
soils are lightly textured but moisture retention capacity
is good because of the dense clay sub-soil.
41.       Water Resources:  The water resources of the Ngwavuma Basin have not
been studied in detail; the only study to date was carried out by Davies
and Waller in 1972, but more detailed studies are underway as part of an
Af.D.B. supported feasibility study. The estimated net mean annual runoff at
the Kentucky Road crossing is 128.5 cusecs. However, because of downstream
commitments no run of river water is available for irrigation unless obtained
by dam storage. The other major streams are the Mantambeni, a tributary of
the Ngwavuma, and the Manziwayo and Ncotjane streams, both tributaries of the
Pongola River. The Mantambeni offers potential for irrigation given adequate
1/   Appendix 1.



ANNEX 8
Page 12
storage. Most streams emanating from the Middleveld plateaus are perennial.
No dams have been constructed in the area. Groundwater is available on the
granites, particularly where deep weathering has taken place. On the Ecca
sandstones potential aquifers are very poor due to their being broken up by
impervious dolerite dykes.
42.       Pasture and Livestock:  There are two principal types of pastures
in the RDA:
(a)  Tall grassveld, generally found above 450 meters.  The
principal grasses are HyperrherAa dissoluta, H. Filipendula,
Eragrostis recemosa and Sporobulus capensus. The pastures
are described as moderately sour; and
(b) Lower Broadleaved Savanna; the most common trees are
Pterocarpus rotundifolius, Cambretura spp, and Terminalia
sericca. The grasses are only slightly sour with the
dominant species being Panicum maximum, Themeda triandra
and Hyparrhenia spp., with Pogonarthria squarrosa as an
invasion species on overgrazed land.
Total cattle numbers are estimated at 19,590, in addition to 11,379 sheep and
goats. Present stocking rate is one A.U. per 1.45 hectares, compared with a
recommended rate of 1 A.U. per 2 hectares (total of 13,840 A.U.s). The
bull:cow ratio is estimated at 1:6.6.
43.       Communications:  Three main gravel roads serve the area; a number
of tracks are normally impassable during rainy periods. The nearest
railheads are at Piet Retief (90 km) and Lavumisa/Gollel (40 km), both in
South Africa.
44.       Social Services:  The area is well served by five secondary schools.
The 22 primary schools in or adjacent to the RDA enroll 2,504 pupils; there
are 21 mission schools, one community school and 12 government-aided s.nools
In 1966, 5,767 school age children were recorded.  Three clinics are L,cated
within or just outside the area. Hluti is the major center for the a-- -. A
main depot for inputs is under construction, and there are 5 small fertili.2r
depots.
(v) Masala-Vikizilula
45.       General:  The RDA is bounded on the South by the Usutu River, the
east by Freehold Title Land, and in the west by the Bulunga Hills. Settlemen-
is concentrated along the Bulunga hills, and the Mimneni-Mzimpofu watershed,
and around Sipofaneni-Phuzamoya.
46.       Topography:  In the west the Bulunga Hills rise to 750 m, and in the
north-east, the Vikizijula Hills rise to about 600 m. North of the Usutu
River, the landscape becomes dissected, with frequent granite tors or kopjes.



ANNEX 8
Page 13
The underlying geology consists of a grey coarse grained quartz diorite and
granodiorite in the west, which gives way to dolerite intruded sandstones and
shales in the east. The Bulunga Hills are formed by a coarse grained pluton.
47.       Climate:  The RDA falls into the western Lowveld climatic zone.
The meteorological station at Sipofaneni recorded average mean rainfall of 648
mm (1935-70). The drought hazard (number of years out of 100 the station is
likely to receive less than 500 mm and less than 750 mm) is 20% under 500 mm.
and 60% under 750 mm.
48.       Soils:  The southern half of the area has been surveyed at a
semi-detailed level by Baillie (1968) with the remaining portion only at
recor2vaissance level by Murdoch (1968). The soils reflect to a strong
degree the underlying geology. Over the grandodiorites and granites are deep
light textured sandy soils with low moisture holding properties and high
erosion risk. On the Ecca sandstones and shales, a heavy sodic solenetz-
like clay soil is fpund. Because of its very low permeability and high
sodium levels soils are regarded as unsuitable for irrigation.   Where
U1le-i,- intrudes sandstone, a relatively shallow (50 to 100 cms) black
c.y of restricted permeability has been found which is considered suitable
for irrigation and dryland cotton production. An extensive area of this
soil is found between Sipofaneni and Phuzamoya.
49.       Water Resources:  Water resources of the Usutu River were inten-
sively studied by the UNDP Usutu Basin study (UNDP 1970). The Usutu is the
only perennial river in the area, with the Mzimnene and the Mzimpofu drying
up during the dry season. A potential dam site has been identified on the
Mzimpofu River approximately 8 kms upstream from its confluence with the
Usutu River. The mean annual runoff of this river is estimated at 23,000
acre feet. On the granites and grandodiorites groundwater potential appears
to be quite good. On the sandstones, potential aquifers are broken up
by numerous dolerite dykes and groundwater in these areas is normally
brackish.
50.       Land Utilization and Production Pattern:  Despite adverse climatic
conditions, maize is the most important crop in the area; cotton is the next
most important crop, followed by groundnuts, sorghum, jugo beans and beans.
Area under individual crops is shown in Table 2.
51.       Pasture and Livestock:  Vegetation is classed as being mostly "Lower
Broadleaved Savanna" with hillside bush on the steeper slopes of the Vikizijula
and Bulunga Hills. In the lower areas to the east this merges into Acacia
savanna. The lower Broadleaved Savanna, where it has not been cultivated, is
typified by broadleaved trees such as Combretum spp, Terminalia sericea, and
Pterocarpus rotundifolus. Grasses comprise the Hyparrhenias, Panicum maximum
and Themeda triandra. Pastures are slightly sour. Overgrazing, particularly



ANNEX 8
Page 14
around Sipofaneni, has caused denudation of the grass cover and invasion of
thorn scrub. Total cattle numbers were estimated at 11,847 in 1974 in addition
to 4,296 goats and 211 sheep. Total carrying capacity has been estimated at 1
A.U. per 4 hectares; The area is, therefore, overstocked by about 4,615
A.U.s. The bull to cow ratio is 1 : 10.5.
52.       Communications:  Two district roads serve the area, the Sipofaneni -
Manzini road and the Sipofaneni - Big Bend road; both are all weather gravel
roads, with the latter scheduled for tarring in 1976. There is one further
district road. Apart from these roads, all others are merely dirt tracks,
often impassble after heavy rain. The railhead is at Phuzamoya which links
Manzini and Maputo. There are proposals to link Phuzamoya with the Gollel
(South African) railhead which is linked to Durban and Richards Bay. Telephone
links with Manzini and Big Bend are located at Sipofaneni. Daily bus services
link Vikizijula with Manzini and Big Bend.
53.       Social Services:  There are two Secondary Schools in the area; both
are Government aided Mission Schools. There are six Primary Schools, three
run by missions, and three by the community. Two clinics serve the area.
(vi) Nkambeni-Madlagampisi
54.       General:  The RDA is located in southeast Hhohho District, bounded
on the north and west by the Komati River; in the south by the Black Umbuluzi
River, and in the east, by the S.I.S. Estate. There are two Chiefdoms - the
larger under Chief Madzanga, and the smaller under Chief Nalamlela.
55.       Topography:  Three regions have been distinguished these include
hill ranges, known respectively as the Lutfotfwane Hills, the Hlobane Hills
and the Nkambeni Hills in the center of the RDA, undulating slopes to the
north and south of the hill ranges, and alluvial terraces bordering the Komati
River in the north. The terraces are considered important for their irrigation
potential.
56.       Climate:  Rainfall decreases and variabiity increases from west to
east. Mean annual rainfall at Balegane station averaged 729 m, and at Tunzin
Station 665.1. Drought hazard is 18% risk of less than 500 mm. rainfall.
57.       Soils:  Three major soil types have been identified: 1/ -
(a) Alluvial soils adjacent to the Komati River. These are B set
soils, deep, red or dark brown permeable loams of high
potential for irrigation. The brown loams have poor moisture
retention capacity but would be suitable for irrigated
citrus production. The red loams are suitable for mixed
cropping under irrigation and for dryland cotton and groundnut
production;
1/   Appendix 1.



ANNEX 8
Page 15
(b) Poorly drained clay pan soils of the N set. Texture
of the topsoil is poor and sub-soil is impermeable.
Crop yields on these soils are poor; and
(c) Grey and pink granite sands on the foothill slopes.
These soils have low moisture retention properties
but with careful management crops such as cotton,
tobacco, beans and maize will produce well.
Land has been classified according to suitability for different farming systems
as follows: -
