58.       Water Resources:  None of the minor rivers are perennial, and are
generally dry in most years from June to September. There are a number of
small stock dams, and the potential for more exists, and four springs
have been protected for domestic purposes. Irrigation potential on the
Komati is considered significant. Exact flow statistics for the river are not
available but minor studies have been carried out at Madlangampisi, e.g.,
U.N.D.P. study (1963) and suggestions have been made that there is potential
for irrigated cotton.
59.       Land Utilization and Production Patterns:  An estimated 99% of
holdings grow maize. Cotton is the next most important crop followed by
groundnuts, sorghum and beans. In this low rainfall area either irrigated
maize or cotton, or dryland cotton and grounduts offer the best potential for
expansion. Area under individual crops is shown Table 2. Irrigated sugar cane
is grown at Sihoya, where a quasi-Government scheme operates 140 hectares. A
number of smallholders each growing approximately 1.6 hectares of sugar cane
are included in the scheme.



ANNEX 8
Page 16
60.       Pasture and Livestock:  Upper broadleaved savanna occurs on the
sandy foothill slopes around the Nkambeni Hills. Common trees include Ficus
spp and Dichrostachys spp. The most common grasses are of the Hyparrhenia
spp. These are now being replaced by shorter species due to intensive grazing
pressure. The pastures are described as moderately sour. The gentle sloping
spurs with clay pan soils give rise to lower broadleaved savanna. Where
ground has not been cultivated, trees such as Combretum spp., Terminalia
sericea (on the deep sands), and Pterocarpus rotundifolius are the most
common. Grass species include Panicum maximum, Hyparrhenias, and Themeda
triandra, and are described as only slightly sour. Pasture carrying capacity
is estimated at 1 A.U. per 4 hectares. Grazing in summer is confined to the
non-arable parts of the area. Arable blocks are generally enclosed with
thorn-tree hedges. There are no improved pastures. Total cattle numbers
were estimated at 15,950 in 1974, in addition to 5,538 goats and 460 sheep.
The bull to cow ratio is 1 to 16.6. The recommended stocking rate for the
area is 1 A.U. per 4 hectares, indicating that the area is overstocked by an
equivalent of 6,133 A.U.'s.
61.        Communications:  The RDA is served by three main gravel roads in
moderate condition.  Manzini is 67 km. from Madlagampisi.   Internal roads
are very poor. Public bus services link the RDA with Piggs Peak, Manzini,
Mhlume, and the Lomati Valley. The nearest railheads are at Mlaula on the
Swaziland railway to Maputo and Hectorspruit on the South African Rail-
way to the Witwatersrand Area.
62.        Social Services:  One secondary school (enrollment 682) serves
the RDA and eight primary schools. In 1966, there were 3,039 children of
school age (5 - 19 years) in the area. One clinic is located inside
the area at Balegane and a second across the Komati River from Sihoya.
The Balegane Clinic is a Government aided Mission Clinic and the other is a
Government Clinic. The nearest hospital is at Manzini. There is one small
fertilizer depot. There are no registered cooperatives in the area but
farmers are able to draw input supplies from the C.C.U. depot at Mayiwane or
from the co-operative shed at Endzingeni.
(vii) Sandleni-Lugolweni
63.       General:  The RDA is situated in the Hlatikulu sub-district of the
Shiselweni district, and is bounded to north and south by the Mhlatuze and
Ngwavuma rivers, with the eastern boundary closely corresponding the middleveld-
lowveld junction. The hills of Makhosini and Qolweni rise to 1086 and 1138
meters, and form small highveld enclaves. Population is fairly evenly dis-
tributed, except in the hilly districts.
64.        Topography:  Topography in the area is complex but can be broadly
subdivided as follows:
(a) The north-east uplands which reach an altitude of 1086
meters at Makosini;



ANNEX 8
Page 17
(b) The south-west uplands, centered around Qolweni Hill,
extend west to include the Lugalweni Hills. Altitude
at Qolweni Hill is 1138 meters;
(c) An area of lower country separating the above two upland
areas and running in a south-east/north-west direction.
Slopes in this area are moderate;
(d) An area of lower country in the extreme south-west of
moderate slope.
65.       Climate:  There are no meteorological stations in the RDA.  Average
annual rainfall in Hluti for the period of 1905-1970 was 829.5 mm. Drought
hazard is estimated at between 60 and 80% in the Ngwavuma valley and between
40 and 60% elsewhere of less than 500 mm. rainfall.
66.       Soils:  The area contains a variety of soil parent rocks.  The
uplands of the northeast are underlain by coarse-grained porphyritic granite
and those of the southwest by coarse grained granite with sizeable dolerite
intrusions. In the southwest the situation is complicated by the presence
of a dolerite sill, which forms the marked escarpment bordering the Lugolweni
hills on the west and south and completely encircles the Qolweni massif at
its lower limit. The lower country drained by the headwaters of the Mhlatuze,
in the northwest, is underlain by quartzites, shales, and schists. These rocks
extend eastwards into the northwestern part of the lowland belt separating the
two main hill masses (i.e., into the Upper Pongolwano valley) but are replaced
in the middle of this belt by coarse-grained granite. This in turn gives way
to undifferentiated lavas in the lowlands drained by the Ntshanini and its
tributaries, in the south-east. The lowlands of the Ngwavuma valley in the
south-west and the two valleys which separate the Lugolweni hills from the
main Qolweni massif, are underlain by coarse-grained granite like the neigh-
boring uplands. A strong correlation exists between soil type and steepness
of slope. In the rugged hill country of the north-east and south-west soils,
almost without exception, belong to the U set. 1/ Elsewhere, given moderate
slopes, a variety of soil types are found. Sizeable blocks of 0 set soils
occur throughout the lower areas and the Q and M sets are common. Small
areas of L and R set soils occur in the south-eastern corner between the
Ntshanini and Ngwavuma rivers. Other soils are of very limited extent.
67.       Water Resources:  The Mhlatuze (a tributary of the Great Usutu)
and the Ngwavuma are the largest rivers and bound the area on the north and
south respectively. The headwaters of the Mhlatuze rise in the north-western
corner of the area. The Ngwavuma is normally perennial but may cease to flow
at the end of a particularly dry year. The Ngwavuma basin is being studied
by and Af.D.B. supported feasibility study. The estimated net annual runoff
at, the Kentucky road crossing (in the south-eastern corner of the RDA) is
128.5 cusecs. Numerous small streams flow from the uplands to join one or
other of the above-mentioned rivers; the Pongolwani and Ntshanini are two
of the most important tributaries in the area.
1/   Appendix 1.



ANNEX 8
Page 18
68.       Pasture and Livestock:  Three pasture types occur within the area
with the most widespread being tall grassveld species. They are replaced by
shorter species under overgrazing and are considered moderately sour, giving
approximately eight months productive grazing. At high altitudes in the
Qolweni hills, moist tall grassveld is found. This consists of a tall,
moderately sour grassland of Hyparrhenia species, and shorter, sourer spe-
cies such as Rendlia altera. The cover is easily damaged by overgrazing
and erosion is often severe on steep, overgrazed slopes. The useful graz-
ing season extends from October to April or May. In the Ngwavuma valley,
upper broad-leaved tree savanna occurs with the most common grasses of the
Hyparrhenia species such as Aristida congesta, Aristida barbicollis (steek-
grass), Heteropogon contortus (speargrass) and Cynodon dactylon (couch
grass). Grazing is moderately sour. Total cattle numbers were estimated
at 18,128 in 1974, in addition to 10,287 sheep and goats. Grazing land in
the RDA supports an average of one A.U. per 1.9 hectares, as opposed to a
recommended stocking rate for the Middleveld of one A.U. per 2.65 hectares
of pasture land; overstocking is thus 6,420 A.U.s. The bull to cow ratio
is 1 : 7.
69.       Communications:  The western and eastern boundaries both follow
main gravel roads for part of their lengths, either of which can be used to
reach Manzini. By the western route, via Grand Valley and Sidvokodvo, the
center of the area is 100 kilometers from Manzini. By the eastern route, via
Sithobela and Sipofaneni, Manzini is 118 kms distant. Another main gravel
road crosses the area from west to east, dividing it into two more or less
equal halves and linking the above-mentioned routes. Main gravel roads
also link the area with Hlatikulu (24 kms from the western boundary); and
the South African border at Onverwacht (24 kms from Kentucky bridge, in the
south-eastern corner of the area). A district road links the north-western
part of the area with the Sithobela - Madubeni RDA.
70.       Social Services:  There were 13 primary schools in the RDA in 1972,
with a total enrollment of 1,530 pupils. Several other primary schools lie
just outside the boundaries. There are secondary schools inside the area at
Ntshanini and Newhaven. There is a government aided Mission clinic at New-
haven and a government clinic on the south-eastern edge of the RDA. The
nearest hospital is at Hlatikulu. There is one main input depot and two small
fertilizer depots.
(viii) Siphofaneni - Maphobeni
71.       General:  The RDA is situated in the Western Lowveld, south of
the Great Usutu River and to the east of the Sinceni Hills. The nearest urban
center is Sipofaneni-Phuzamoyo, located just north of the Usutu River. Manzini
is 48 kms. to the northwest, Big Bend 25 kms. to the east and Sitobela 25 kms.
to the south. Three Chiefdoms under Chiefs Hhabela Shongwe, Madelenya Camede
and Mhau Ngeampalala are found within the RDA. Population is concentrated
along three main axes: the Usutu River, the Mhlatuzane River, and along the
main Siphofaneni-Sitobela road.



ANNEX 8
Page 19
72.       Topography:  Four topographic regions can be distinguished:
(a) Alluvial flats along the Usutu River with well drained
soils of the "L" and "W"' sets,
(b) A Dolerite Bovane Ridge south-east of the Usutu which is
rock and boulder strewn.
(c) The Lenono Platform, a dissected plateau more than 300
meters a.s.l.;
(d) Central Valleys and rises in the eastern part of the area
with slopes of between 3 and 10%.
73.       Climate:  Rainfall in the RDA increases from east to west, as
demonstrated by two rainfall stations, at Siphofaneni and St. Phillips, where
average rainfall is recorded at 696 mm and 605 mm respectively. Rainfall
variability is quite marked within the area. Low and variable rainfall and
high evaporation rates mean that rainfed cropping has low potential. Drought
hazard is 20-32% chance of less than 500 mm. rainfall.
74.       Soils:  A semi-detailed soil survey of the area was carried out by
Murdoch and Andriesse (1964). In addition, a detailed survey of the irrigable
area near the Usutu River was undertaken by Loxton (U.N.D.P. 1970). The soils
reflect to a marked degree the underlying geology. The area is underlain by
the Ecca sandstones and shales, which have been injected by numerous dolerite
dykes and sills. The Ecca sediments give rise to duplex and solonetz-like
soils, whose essential characteristics are a light textured topsoil underlain
by a heavy and often sodic clay pan. The non-sodic claypan soil has a deeper
sandy topsoil, with a mottled sandy clay subsoil. Possibly because of its
ease of cultivation and its moisture retention properties, this soil has been
extensively used for rainfed cropping in the area. The sodic claypan soil, on
the other hand, has only a thin light textured topsoil, overlying a non-mottled
grey and often sodic subsoil. Soils overlying the dolerite sills are red,
brown and black clays. All are well supplied with nutrients, but the black
clays present problems of drainage and ease of cultivation. However, because
of their moderately high clay contents, they have good moisture holding
capacities. On the steeper slopes, shallow grey sandy loams and shallow red
clay loams are found. The shallow grey soils have little potential for
cropping, but the shallow red clay loams can be used where the underlying
dolerite has been weathered sufficiently to provide a rooting medium.
75.       Water Resources:  The water resources of the Maphobeni Area (includ-
ing the Usutu River) have been intensively studied by the U.N.D.P. Usutu
Basin Study (1970). The Usutu is the only perennial river in the area, with
the Mhlatuzane drying up periodically. Because of the numerous dolerite
dykes and sills which break up the sandstones, groundwater reserves are very
poor and often brackish. A number of small stock watering dams have been
built on the smaller streams. Recent studies have shown that even small dams
(5 to 7 m dam walls) cost in the region of E.20,000 and are now only located
where water is required for dip tanks.



ANNEX 8
Page 20
76.       Land Utilization and Production Patterns:  An estimated 99% of
holdings grow maize. Cotton is the next most important crop, followed by
pumpkin (undersown with maize), groundnuts, sorghum, jugo-beans and beans.
Area under individual crops are shown in Table 2.
77.       Pasture and Livestock:  Four types of pasture have been recognized
in the area. The first is found on the rocky and bouldery ground of the
Bovane Ridge and the granite residuals to the west; grass is thin or non-
existent, and aloes predominate. The second is found on the deep granite
sands in the western part of the area; Eragrostis superba, Digitaria smutsii,
and Panicum maximum are the most common grasses, but Aristida barbicollis and
Cynodon dactylon invade where there has been overgrazing and indiscriminate
burning. Basal cover is very low, (40%), and the pastures are said to be
slightly sour. On the duplex soils of the "H" and "Z" sets the common grasses
are similar to the granite sands plus Seteria Sphacelata and sometimes Themeda
triandra. Invasion species on overgrazed lands include Sporobolus pyramidalis
and Pogonarthria squarrose. These soils and pastures are extremely sus-
ceptible to erosion along cattle tracks and around dipping tanks. The fourth
pasture type occurs on the dolerite soils, and Themeda triandra, Panicum
maximum, and Eragrostis superba provide sweet grazing. Total cattle numbers
were estimated at 9,761 in 1974, in addition to 4,898 goats and 286 sheep.
Pastures at present carry one A.U. per 2.5 hectares as opposed to a recom-
mended carrying capacity of approximately 1 A.U. per 4 hectares.  The bull
cow ratio stands at I : 15.
78.       Communications:  The area is served by two district roads of good
standard all-weather gravel. Apart from these roads, all others are merely
dirt tracks, often impassable after heavy rain. The nearest rail head is at
Phuzomoyo 7 kms west of Sipofaneni which links Manzini and Maputo. There
are at present preliminary plans to link Phuzamoyo with the South African
rail system at Lavumisa, which would give direct access to Richards Bay and
Durban. Telephone links with Manzini and Big Bend are found at Siphofaneni
and Sinceni. Daily bus services link Mapobeni and Sipofaneni with Big Bend
and Manzini.
79.       Social Services:  There is only one primary school in the RDA with
teaching to Standard 2, for 40 pupils. The nearest Secondary Schools are
found at Sinceni, St. Phillips, and Phcnjwane. The 1966 census indicted
1,085 children of school age (5 to 19 years). Although there are no clinics
in the area, three are located just outside the boundaries, at Siphofaneni
(Government), Sinceni (Government), and St. Phillips (Government Aided-
Mission). One input depot is under construction and one society is registered
in the area.
(ix) Siphocosini-Motshane.
80.       General:  The RDA includes two separate areas of land situated
west of Mbabane. The two blocks - Motshane to the north and Siphocosini
to the south - are separated from each other by an east-west corridor, still



ANNEX 8
Page 21
under private ownership. The eastern boundary of Motshane is 10 kilometers
from Mbabane. The area includes parts of the following Chiefdoms: Luvuno,
Mpetsambalo, Zembe, Mvandazi, Mhau, and Mnt. Lutho. In Sipocosini the popu-
lation is concentrated in the south-eastern corner, particularly on a belt of
relatively low land on the right bank of the Little Usutu River. Elsewhere
population is thinly scattered. In Motshane an east-west trending belt of
relatively dense population occurs in the north, where steep hills fall to
meet the lowlands of the Motshane valley. Elsewhere the population is thinly
scattered.
81.       Topography:  The central and southern parts of Motshane consist of
rugged hill country rising to 1,662 meters at Nduma. The highest altitudes
occur along the northern edge of this upland, and the steepest slopes occur
where it drops away to the Motshane valley in the north and west. In the
southern part of the upland zone slopes are gentler and valleys more open.
This region is drained southwards to the Motshane River by the Mtambene River
and its tributaries. The Sipocosini section extends south-eastwards along
the valley of the Little Usutu River for a distance of 26 km. The area
includes variable-sized blocks of upland to the north and south of the River.
In the upper, middle and lower parts of the valley there are considerable
areas of open, relatively gentle sloping country.
82.       Climate:  The RDA receives most rainfall in summer.  There are no
rainfall recording stations inside the area but two lie just outside - Oshoek
to the north west (average 968 mm. p.a.) and Mbabane to the east (average
1,377 mm p.a.). Variations in altitude, aspect and relief within the area
cause significant variation in annual rainfall.
83.       Soils:  1/  The soils of the Motshane area were surveyed and mapped
by Murdoch as part of the National Soil Reconnaissance (1963-68). In the
lowlands of the Motshane valley, the soil parent rock consists of serpen-
tinites and talc-chlorite schist, with an area of mixed metamorphic rocks
in the extreme west. In the hill country of the center, east and south,
the parent rock is coarse grained granite. U set soils are the most common
type on the uplands, although in the south center (in the vicinity of the
Mtambeni and its tributaries) there is a sizeable area of mixed soils,
mainly of the N, JH, 0, ZH, SH, TH, A and CH sets. In the lowlands of the
Motshane valley a variety of soils has developed on the serpentinite and
metamorphic parent rocks. Along the river itself and its tributaries I set
soils occur in long narrow strips. In the northern part of the area these
are backed by G set soils on the gentle lower slopes, with N set soils tak-
ing over where the slopes steepen towards the upland to the south. In the
western part of the lowland the I set soils are backed by a narrow strip of
A set, and again N set soils form a broad band on the intermediate slopes
between upland and lowland. Other soils occurring in the lowland areas
include JH, ZH and M sets. There are three types of parent rock in the
Sipocosini area - coarse-grained granite in the south-east, gabbro in the
1/ Appendix 1.



ANNEX 8
Page 22
center, and mixed metamorphics in the north-west. Granite outcrops are found
in the north-western corner. There is little correlation between soil type
and parent rock. The blocks of hill country are underlain by both gabbro and
granite, where U set soils predominate within a few small patches of 0 and N
sets. On the more gently sloping, lower lying ground in the Little Usutu
valley a greater variety of soils occurs. Of particular importance is the
relatively large area of M set and N set soils around Sipocosini itself, in
the south-eastern corner of the area. Further up the valley the M set is
less common. In Sigangeni district (near the center of the area) soils
belong mainly to the N, TH, M, SH, and I sets. In the part of the valley
adjacent to the international boundary the SH, 0, A, N, Q and TH sets are
the most common. A land capability map is available for the area.
84.       Water Resources:  In Motshane, from the highest land (in the
center of the area) streams radiate outwards in all directions. Those
flowing north, west and south are tributary to the Motshane, which is the
largest river. The Motshane enters the area on the northern boundary and
flows in an arc around the central upland - westwards, southwards then
eastwards. The Motshane and its main tributaries (the most notable being
the Mtambani-Amanzimnyama) are perennial. The Little Usutu River flows
through the Sipocosini area from north-west to south-east and dominates
the drainage. Immediately below the confluence with the Motshane the mean
annual flow of the Little Usutu is 137,000 acre-feet, and immediately above
the flow is 72,000 acre-feet.
85.       Land Utilization and Production Patterns:  An estimated 98% of
holdings grow maize, with other important crops being potatoes, beans and
groundnuts. Potential for expansion of the potato acreage is significant.
Area under different crops are shown in Table 2.
86.       Pasture and Livestock:  Two main pasture types occur within the RDA.
"Mountain sourveld" is found on the higher hills in central and eastern
Motshane and on hills in the extreme south-eastern corner of Sipocosini, where
the rainfall exceeds about 1,270 mm a year; here is found open, short, very
sour grassland. The grasses provide palatable and productive grazing only
during the period September to March. Pioneer species invade with overgrazing
but the cover is not easily destroyed. Themeda triandra (Rooigras) is normally
dominant, but with heavy grazing it is replaced by such species such as
Eragrostis plana and Sporobolus capensis. Elsewhere (over the greater part of
the area) the pasture is "high land sourveld". This is very similar to
mountain sourveld, an open fairly short grassland, dominated by Themeda
Triandra where it has not been overgrazed. Grass cover tends to be slightly
less dense than in the previous type. This pasture is also sour, providing
useful grazing only during summer. Total cattle numbers were estimated at
6,461 in 1974, in addition to 3,153 goats and 600 sheep. Grazing land in
the RDA supports an average of one A.U. per 2.8 hectares, opposed to a recom-
mended stocking rate of one A.U. to 1 - 1.8 hectares; on average the area is
not overstocked. The bull to cow ratio is 1 to 8.



ANNEX 8
Page 23
87.       Communications:  The Motshane area is crossed by the main tar road
linking Mbabane with the Republic of South Africa via the Oshoek border post.
The eastern boundary is 10 kms from Mbabane, and the northern boundary 6
kms from Oshoek. The all-weather gravel road to Piggs Peak (now being
tarred) branches off the Mbabane-Oshoek road within the area. Motshane
is linked to Sipocosini by a track which is at present being upgraded to
district road status. The lbabane-Mhlambanyathi road is a main gravel
road and crosses the south-eastern corner of the Sipocosini section. The
terminus of the railway is just outside the northern boundary of Motshane,
and the line crosses the south-western corner of this area and the south-
eastern part of Sipocosini. Mgwili station lies within the Motshane section
and Siphoco station within the Sipocosini section. Electricity transmission
lines linking Mbabane with the Kadake iron ore mine cross the north-eastern
corner of Motshane, and lines linking Mbabane with Mhlambanyathi cross the
south eastern part of Sipocosini.
88.       Social Services:  Only one Primary School lies inside the Motshane
section but others are close to the boundaries of the RDA. There is a
Secondary School at Motshane itself, and Waterford School is just outside
the area to the southeast.  There are four Secondary Schools in Mbabane.
The nearest medical facilities to Motshane are in Mbabane, where a wide
variety of health services is available including a Government Hospital,
private clinic, private doctors, private dentist and an Oxfam Nutrition Unit.
The nearest medical facilities to Sipocosini are at Mhlambanyathi where the
Usutu Pulp Company runs a clinic with a doctor. The British Red Cross Society
also has a center here. There is a main input depot and one small fertilizer
depot.
(x) Sithobela - Madubeni.
89.       General:  The RDA, situated south of Siphofaneni on the western edge
of the Lubombo District, is bounded by the Mhlatuzane and Mhlatuze Rivers,
which flow parallel to one another in a north-easterly direction to join the
Great Usutu. Only a very small part adjacent to the south-western boundary
lies above 460 meters. Sithobela, which is more or less in the middle of the
area, is 27 kms south of Siphofaneni, the nearest center, and 75 kms from
Manzini. Sipofaneni is on the railway line. The area includes parts of the
following Chiefdoms - Mbelebeleni, Ngobelweni, Kamkoke. Compared to Swazi
Nation land elsewhere, the area is thinly populated. Slightly higher densi-
ties occur around Sithobela, and along the road linking Sithobela with Kubuta,
but there are large areas devoid of population - notably the hills on the
southern boundary of Mbhoke District, and the country to the east of the
Sithobela-Siphofaneni Road.
90.       Topography:  The RDA covers part of the interfluve between the
Mhlatuzane and Mhlatuze Rivers, from the mail watershed the land slopes
gently downwards on either side into numerous small valleys which are
tributary to the main rivers. In the south-western part of the area the



ANNEX 8
Page 24
interfluve is subdivided into northern and southern halves by the MItimpili
river, a major left bank tributary of the Mhlatuze. The northern half
include& Sitobela Hill, an isolated sharp-crested ridge rising to 523
meters. The southern watershed follows the crest of a narrow, steep-
sided ridge on the south-western boundary which rises 708 meters, and
forms the only substantial hill country in the area.
91.       Climate:  There are no rainfall recording stations inside the
area, but records are available for Siphofaneni, 15 kms outside the northern
boundary, where mean annual rainfall is 648 mm.
92.       Soils:  The soils of the area have been surveyed and mapped at the
semi-detailed level by Murdoch and Andriesse between 1955 and 1968. In the
north-eastern part of the area the parent rock consists of sandstone, coal
and carbonacous shale of the Ecca Series, with large, irregularly shaped
intrusions of dolerite and granodiorite. A few kilometers to the east of
Sithobela a sharp change occurs along a north-south line (thought to be a
fault-line). To the west of this line the rocks are mainly granodioritic
gneiss and biotite-hornblende gneisses, with relatively small dolerite
intrusions and a number of north-west - south-east trending diabase dykes
one of which forms the spine of Sithobela Hill. In the extreme south the
hills of the Mbhoke District are based upon quartzite and shale, phyllite
and sericitic schist of the Mozaan Series. U set soils cover the hills ad-
jacent to the southern boundary and the high ground around Sitobela Hill.
The 0 set is common, occurring where slopes steepen as the land falls to
the Mhlatuzane and Mhlatuze Rivers and their main tributaries. The ZL set
is confined to the outcrop of Ecca strata in the north-east, where it and
the H set are the two most widespread types. Smaller patches of good arable
soils (L & R sets) occur particularly in the north-eastern half of the area.
Blocks of the SL and G sets are scattered throughout.
93.       Water Resources:  The M4hlatuzane and Mhlatuze are the main rivers
of the area, and bound it on the north-west and south-east respectively.
They are both tributary to the Great Usutu. Numerous small tributaries of
these two rivers rise inside the RDA, with the Mtimpili tributary being the
most important.
94.       Pasture and Livestock:  Pastures belong to the Lower Broadleaved
Tree Savanna category. Except where cleared for cultivation, the vegetation
is a savanna, typified by broad-leaved trees such as Combretum spp., Terminalia
sericea, and Pterocarpus rotundifolius, and tall grasses such as the Hyparrhenias
Panicum maximum (Lubabe) and Themeda triandra.  This pasture is only slightly
sour and provides palatable and productive grazing for most of the dry season.
In common with other Lowveld pasture types, it is sensitive to overgrazing
and can be replaced by dense bush if mismanaged. Total cattle numbers were
estimated at 22,136 in 1974, in addition to 13,932 goats and 598 sheep.
Grazing land in the RDA supports an average of one A.U. per 1.7 hectares; the
recommended stocking rate for lowveld pastures is one A.U. to 4 - 5 hectares
throughout the year, so that the area is overstocked by 10,400 A.U.'s. The
bull to cow ratio is 1 to 14.



ANNEX 8
Page 25
95.       Communications:  Two gravel roads link the area with Manzini - one
via Siphofaneni and the other via Kubuta and Sidvokadvo. Gravel roads also
link the area with Big Bend and with the Onverwacht Border post in South
Africa. Siphofaneni is on the railway line and there is an airstrip at
Kubuta outside the western boundary of the area.
96.       Social Services:  There were four primary schools within the RDA
in 1974, and several other primary schools lie close to the boundaries. There
is a Secondary School at Singceni, a few kilometers outside the northern
boundary and a Government aided Mission clinic at St. Phillips.
C. Rural Development Areas Financed by ODM
97.       Table 3 summarizes the principal characteristics of Rural Develop-
ment areas supported by ODM-financing. The location of areas are shown
on the map. There are four functioning RDA's: Northern, Southern, Central,
and Mahlangatsha, while four extensions have been delineated. Total area
of of the 8 RDA's is about 64,000 hectares, and total population 32,000.

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Soil Classification
V.   The following are soil classifications referred to in this Report, based
on studies by G. Murdoch 1/:
(i)  A set soils are deep yellow loams, very acid, with imperfect
drainage. They are rated fair for dryland crop growing.
(ii)  G set soils are grey sandy loams resting on buried hard
iron concretions or a cemented iron pan. Beneath the pan,
which is of variable thickness, soils are usually mottled
and of heavy or medium texture. They are rated poor for
dryland crop growing.
(iii)  I set soils are marsh soils, ranging texturally from sand
to clay, mottled, and sometimes possessing a dark humic
top. Due to waterlogging they are rated unfit for dryland
crop growing.
(iv)  M set soils are deep red and orange loams (usually much
more than 300 cms. to hard rock) with moderate to low
humus content, well drained, and with few or no weatherable
minerals. They are rated good for dryland crop growing.
(v)  0 set soils are shallow (often less than 40 cms) and
siliceous, light in texture, low in humus and rapid
draining. They are underlain by hard rock and rated
poor for dryland crop growing.
(vi)   Soils belonging to the Q set are deep (more than 120 cms).
The texture is generally a gravelly loam though the top
30-40 cms. may be almost gravel-free. They are rather low
in humus, rapid draining and rated fair for dryland crop
growing.
(vii)  In the U set  more than 15% of the land surface (and often
more than 40%) consists of rock outcrops and/or boulder-
strewn ground. Soil occurs in small pockets and is mostly
thin, acid and mineral in nature. They are rated unfit
for dryland crop growing.
(viii)  JH set soils are grey loams lying on thick stone lines at
about 30 to 60 cms depth. Drainage is slightly impaired
and the soil is rated poor for dryland crop growing.
I/   G. Murdoch, Soils and Land Capability in Swaziland (Swaziland:  Ministry
of Agriculture, 1968).



ANNEX 8
Appendix I
Page 2
(ix) L set soils are usually deep, medium textured, slightly
acid and moderately permeable. They are rated good for
dryland crop growing.
(x) R set soils are dark red, heavy textured, slighly acid,
and moderately permeable. They are rated good for
dryland crop growing.
(xi) ZL soils consist of a dark grey sandy loam lying on
clay pan. They may be saline and are generally poorly
drained. They are rated poor for dryland crop growing.
(xii) H set soils consist of a grey sandy loam resting on
mottled clay pan. They are poorly drained and rated
poor for dryland crop growing.
(xiii)  The SL set consists of very shallow soils, rarely more
than 40 cms to weathering parent material and the same
thickness again to unaltered rock. They are medium to
heavy textured, well drained, often gravelly and rich in
weatherable minerals. They are rated poor for dryland
crop growing.

Rural Social Services
A. Rural Health
1.        The general standard of health care available in Swaziland's rural
areas is low, and medical facilities are seriously deficient. Prior to
Independence, clinics were operated only by the Swazi Nation and the Nazarene
Church. Death rate and life expectancy statistics indicate the magnitude
of the problem: in 1966, the crude death rate for the country was 20 per
thousand head of the population, and the life expectancy only 44 years. The
Government has made a significant effort in recent years to improve health
care; 37 clinics are operating, with emphasis on curative medicine. Clinics
normally have 2 nurses.
2.        Principal disease problems are respiratory (TB) and gastro-intes-
tinal diseases, bilharzia, and malnutrition. An estimated 70% of the school
age population has bilharzia. Diseases such as bilharzia, gastro-enteritis,
typhoid and tapeworm infection are largely attributed to the inadequacy or
absence of hygienic water supplies and safe methods of human waste disposal.
A National Rural Environmental Health Program has been instituted to educate
and assist rural communities to improve water and sanitation facilities.
Health education programs are conducted at Health Centers and Clinics and a
hygiene component is being included in the curricula of an increasing number
of schools. Family planning advice is available at all rural Health Centers
and Clinics.
3.        Disease control units are centered at Manzini with the emphasis
focused on control of tuberculosis, malaria, and bilharzia. Other disease
control activities include a national measles and polio vaccination campaign.
A Mobile Eye Clinic visits all clinics, health centers and hospitals in the
country. An objective in future is to develop visiting health services to
extend health care to a greater part of the rural population.
4.        Basic education in nutrition is provided by the Ministry of Health
through Rural Health Clinics and by the Home Economics Section of the Ministry
of Agriculture working through 177 Women's Associations with a total member-
ship of 6000 persons.
5.        To improve the present situation, the Ministry of Health outlined
the following three major objectives of a rural health program in the Second
National Development Plan (1973):-



ANNEX 9
Page 2
(i) Improvement of the overall standards of health by reducing
water borne diseases and diseases of insanitation in the
rural areas;
(ii) Maintenance and improvement of curative services available
to the rural populace;
(iii) Reduction of the national birth rate (which at present
stands as one of the highest in the world).
6.        Increased coverage of health services in the rural areas would be
achieved by:
(i) construction and staffing of new rural clinics;
(ii) improved staffing of existing clinics;
(iii) construction of 4 Rural Health Centers.
ODM has provided financial assistance for construction of clinics and health
centers.
7.        To achieve a lowering of the birth rate, the Ministry has recently
started a Family Planning Program, assisted by the United Nations Fund for
Population Activities (UNFPA). The Cabinet has approved the national plan,
although the SNC were negative. Initially 8 trained Mother and Child Care and
Family Planning (MCH/CP) nurses will be assigned to the 4 districts of Swaziland,
and the program will be expanded as more staff become available. Birth
control pills and devices will be sold at subsidised prices. The permission
of the husband was formerly required for contraceptives for women, but this
has been discontinued.
8.        At present the constraints on expanding and improving health ser-
vices to the rural population are a lack of trained staff and limited funds
to cover recurring expenditures. Regarding staff, the Government (with as-
sistance from the U.S.) is establishing a multipurpose health training cen-
ter, which will aim at producing 20 nurses per year. These nurses, trained
in mid-wifery and medical and surgical nursing, are the backbone of the
rural health program. By 1980 staffing problems should begin to ease.
9.        Regarding recurrent expenditures, health services in the rural areas
are heavily subsidised and although a small fee is charged per consultation
(E.20 per visit) it is not realistic to expect a rapid increase of these
fees.
10.       Within the proposed RDA  areas, there is a need for two Rural
Clinics (in Mpolonjeni and Nkonjane respectively) and minor additions to
a Rural Clinic financed by the US at Zombodze. All these clinics would
be constructed at the Project Development Center compounds. An integral
part of these clinics would be a section which would actively propagate
family planning measures. The Ministry of Health would be responsible for
staffing and recurring expenditures.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
B.   Rural Education
11.       Enrollments in both primary and secondary schools in Swaziland have
increased substantially in recent years, with the average growth rate for
primary institutions 6.4% per annum and secondary institutions 15.5X
per annum since 1962. Despite this, only 60% of those in the 7-13 years age
bracket and 30% of those in the 14-18 year bracket were enrolled in educa-
tional institutions at the beginning of 1975. Failure and resignation rates
during most years are reported as being high, while the standard of education
is generally poor in most rural areas. The Government's objective is to
provide sufficient primary schools for all school age children within ten
years; SIDA is assisting in this program. There are now about 420 primary
schools in Swaziland.
12.       The main educational objective of the Government at present is
to provide adequate schools and teachers and to make curricula more relevant.
Funds are being channeled into schools large enough to make full use of
additional resources, with the insistence that the respective communities
contribute as fully as possible to augment any Government assistance made
available. A school garden scheme now operates in 200 primary schools - the
aim being to increase the rural bias in the education of younger children.
13.       Problems at the secondary school level are similar with respect to
high failure and resignation rates, unsuitable school curricula and poorly
qualified staff. At junior levels the agriculture component of the curriculum
will be increased and greater emphasis will be given to craft subjects and
home economics training.
14.       Adult education in the rural areas has received greater attention
in recent years. The basic aims are:
(i) To raise the standard of functional literacy and
(ii) To raise receptivity levels for technical advice and
training.
The literacy program is being implemented through a National Adult Literacy
Campaign organized by a National Literacy Board. The program involves more
than 700 literacy classes per annum, each on a part time basis for a period
of 100 hours. Full use is being made of schools, work places, markets and
other community buildings. Communities have been encouraged to organize
their own classes.
15.       The second major component of the adult education program involves
training and advice in such fields as agriculture, home economics, health
etc. Pilot Community Learning Centers (Rural Education Centers) are to be
established for this purpose near or in some of the Rural Development Areas
under an IDA financed education project. Initially seven Centers are to be
established, namely at Big Bend, Dvokolwako, Ekuphakameni (Mahlangatsha
R.D.A.), Elulakeni, Entfonjeni (Northern R.D.A.), Ngwane and Vuvulane.
Each center will be based in or near a Junior Secondary School.



ANNEX 9
Page 4
16.       Because of the inter-ministerial approach to education in the rural
areas the Rural Development Committee (a sub-Committee of the Cabinet) has
assumed responsibility for guiding the organization of the Community Learning
Centers. At each Center a lower Co-ordinating committee comprised of represen-
tatives from the Ministries of Education, Agriculture, Health and Local
Administration and the Sebenta Training Institute will organize teaching
activities.
September 12, 1976

Project Agricultural Production Estimates
Approach to Analysis of Production
1.        During preparation of the proposed Project, Ministry of Agricul-
ture staff utilized a "management-oriented approach" for the analysis of
production costs and benefits in Project RDA's (para. 4). Technical para-
meters for each crop and each region were developed on the basis of research
results and experience in current Rural Development Areas. A computer program
was then devised which analyzed current and projected production in each
RDA, likely credit uptake, and "average" farm budgets. Programs were also
prepared to calculate herd projections for each RDA. The result was develop-
ment of detailed estimates of expected production under the Project, for each
year and for each RDA.
2.        The preparation report recognized that calculation of crop production
costs and benefits in Project RDA's is complicated by the number of variables
involved, which include: (i) significant differences in ecology among RDA's;
(ii) variation in individual crop input/output levels with the level of devel-
opment of farmers; (iii) variation in hectares devoted to individual crops
with the number of field officers involved in an area and the number of hec-
tares presently devoted to the crop; and (iv) differences in credit utiliza-
tion according to the type of crop and level of inputs applied.
3.         The mission concluded further that available data on current crop
production is poor, and most is based on 1970-71 sample surveys whose reliability
is questionable. Assumptions on progressive development of each crop are
problematic since they are not based on actual experience (reliable data is
not available) but on generally subjective criteria. Assumptions on credit
up-take are particularly uncertain. Finally, so many investments, Government
services, and price factors contribute to changes in farming patterns that it
is difficult to attribute changes specifically to one project. Nevertheless,
estimates of Project production should prove a useful guide for Project
management staff - the purpose for which they were primarily designed - and
should provide a data base which should serve a useful frame of reference for
subsequent Project monitoring and evaluation.
The "Management Oriented Approach" - Principal Assumptions I/
4.        The principal assumptions underlying the approach used to estimate
production in Project RDA's are summarized below:
1/   Based on "The Estimation of Crop Production Costs and Benefits in
Swaziland Rural Development Projects", Ministry of Agriculture, 1976.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
(a) Estimation of farm production costs and returns in
rural development project documents is often very
impressionistic, because of lack of detailed infor-
mation on present and future farming, and because of
complexities of calculations required where a number
of crops in different environments are considered;
(b) Village, farm or crop models are normally presented,
then extrapolated for the overall project; these are
necessarily impressionistic and, although adequate
for overall estimation of project benefits, can be of
limited relevance to project managers who must work
with specific areas having a certain ecology, cropping
pattern, and extension force;
(c) Therefore, an alternative approach was adopted for the
Project, including use of a computer program to adopt
a less impressionistic and more management oriented
approach;
(d) The approach relates expected production increases
to numbers of extension workers, farmers per extension
worker, extension programs, and estimated pre-develop-
ment areas under crops. Increases in area under crops
is assumed to be a function of the above factors;
(e) Data required includes: existing cropping pattern and
level of inputs used, crop uptake rates, input/output
data, and cost information.
Crop Production - Summary
5.        Increases of production were assumed to result less from
increases in acreage than from higher yields as a result of the use of
improved seed, fertilizer, storage, and better management. Hybrid maize
would replace part of the present maize acreage, and because of higher
yields would release some land for cash crops such as tobacco and cotton.
Further increases in production could result from irrigated production of
rice and vegetables.
6.        Crop margins:  Crop margins under Project conditions are estimated
as follows; prices are 1975-76 prevailing prices, which are close to farm-
gate prices:

11.       Estimated future farm income from crops could be of the order
of E245 in the intensive RDA's and E175 in the less intensive RDA's. The
financial costs involved, excluding crops like cotton and tobacco and excluding
farm labor, would be no more than E20. This means that for the less intensive
areas the gross margin would be around E153. In the intensive areas income
would be E245, giving a gross margin of around E225, which is similar to the
margins in the Middleveld where farms tend to be over 3 hectares.



ANNEX 10
Page 6
12.       Technical Basis for Production Estimates:  Assumptions employed
for analysis by the preparation team are summarized below. Crop technical
information (Tables 1-7) relates the expected average crop input to an aver-
age level of input for each ecological region. Three crop categories are
distinguished:
(a) 'Actively extended crops' such as hybrid maize,
cotton, tobacco and potatoes.
(b) 'Passive crops' which include those crops bene-
fitting from increased input availability and
general extension activity but not actively
extended by field officers, e.g. groundnuts,
beans and local maize.
(c) 'Other crops' which include such crops as
sorghum, pumpkins and jugo beans. It was
assumed that input and output levels and
area devoted to these crops would remain
unaffected by the Project.
For categories (a) and (b) input and output levels were assumed to increase
through time as a result of extension effort and increased input availability.
Input levels per crop were expressed as a percentage of the recommended level
for each ecological zone. Output levels are expressed in terms of the per-
centage of the expected maximum level of field production attainable when
inputs are applied at their recommended level. Crops in category (c)
were ignored since no changes in input or output were envisaged. They would
therefore have no incremental effect but income estimates were included in
the average farm budgets.
13.       Gross margin increases resulting from successive increases in
input and output levels indicate that it would be profitable to move to
higher levels of input use if the corresponding yield increases were ob-
tained. Data given in Tables 1-7 relate specifically to the less inten-
sive RDA's. For intensive RDA's, average crop inputs and outputs were
assumed increase in a similar fashion but a higher rate for the following
reasons:
-    More intensive staffing level
-    More intensive area management via on-site
project manager
-    Better use of suitable arable lands through
rationalization of land use and consolidation
-    Effect of tractor use on timeliness and quality
of cultivations
-    Soil conservation measures



ANNEX 10
Page 7
It was expected that improved area supervision and increased staffing in
the maximum areas would directly increase both the level of use and effec-
tiveness of application of crop input.
Cattle
14.       Incremental cattle production was estimated for intensive RDA's.
Five separate grazing areas were considered: four in the Mpolonjeni -
Lubombo RDA (Mpolonjeni, Tikuba, Sitataweni, and Lomahasha) and one con-
sisting of the Mahamba-Zombodze RDA as a whole. In order to estimate the
incremental income resulting from destocking where necessary and improve-
ments in herd performance and pasture condition, two herd projections were
prepared for each area: one for the situation without the Project (present
situation), and one for the situation with the Project (improved situa-
tion). Both projections start from the same base in year zero (the exist-
ing herd structure). The production parameters for subsequent years differ
for the two situation. Table 10 summarizes technical coefficients and input
data used in the herd projections.
15.       All areas except Tibuka are presently overstocked.  For these
areas it was assumed that in the absence of measures to destock or increase
carrying capacity the production parameters would remain at their initial
low levels. For the Tikuba area, which is not presently overstocked, a
higher level of performance was assumed initially. This would however
result in an overstocking situation by year 10 if the performance para-
meters were reduced accordingly.
16.       In order to realize improved performance parameters it would be
necessary to eliminate the present or future overgrazing by reducing stock
numbers. Cows and oxen above the normal 10% culling rate were therefore
assumed to be removed as necessary to move progressively to an acceptable
stocking rate by around year 15. It was assumed that a minimum number of
oxen must be maintained to carry out the required cultivations in each area.
An estimated 0.85 oxen would be required per household. Cow and oxen off-
takes in excess of the 10% culling rate indicates the order of destocking
required to reduce herd numbers to a satisfactory level. Cows in excess
of the 10% cull were priced higher than other cows because they would tend
to be younger animals and have a value as breeding stock as well as for
meat. Oxen in excess of the 10% cull were priced slightly lower than other
oxen to reflect the smaller size of these younger animals. It was assumed
that controlled breeding would result in higher value stock and for this
reason the price of all stock was increased by 2% per year above the original
prices from year 11. The carrying capacity of the land was assumed to be
marginally increased by rotational grazing and pasture improvement. It was
assumed that these measures would increase carrying capacities by 10% by
year 10.



ANNEX 10
Page 8
17.       In conclusion, mission analysis confirmed that the farm incomes used
in calculating the before and after Project benefits were based on sound
principles and they reflect a fair picture of the lower income group of Swazi
Nation Land farmers. The inputs necessary to produce higher yields are based
on experimental work and field trials, and yields expected under Project
conditions are within the bounds of probability. The rate of uptake by
farmers is to some extent based on experience gained in the present RDA's;
whether this is achieved or surpassed will depend largely on the capabilities
and number of the extension staff in the RDA's.
18.       Table 9 summarizes estimated production increases in Project RDA's.
These estimates are based on data developed during Project preparation, and
should be viewed as approximate. Nevertheless, it provides a useful basis
for analysis of Project benefits, and should assist Project managers in mea-
suring future increases in output in each area against initial expectations.
September 26, 1976

ANNEX 13
Page 1
SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Prolect Management
Monitoring and Evaluation
1.        Swazi officials recognize that data on progress and results
of on-going rural development projects are lacking, and plan to remedy
the situation through introduction of systematic monitoring and evalua-
tion in future. Monitoring and evaluation of progress under the proposed
Project would be the responsibility of the Economics and Farm Manage-
ment Section of the Ministry of Agriculture, which reports directly to
the Permanent Secretary. The Section has already initiated Project
monitoring activities in existing RDA's; survey teams were assembling
data on the southern RDA during Project appraisal.
2.        Agricultural statistics on overall Swazi Nation Land agricul-
tural patterns are the responsibility of the Statistical Office, within
the Ministry of Finance. The survey techniques employed do not, however,
at this stage allow for differentiation by specific areas (for example,
RDA's); new survey techniques (stratification) would be required in order
to permit the Statistical Office to extrapolate and compare relative
production increases in areas affected by, or not affected by Project
developments. It would appear, however, that the most effective way to
develop data on agricultural production under this and other projects
would be to build on current systems for assembling agricultural data
and improving them, rather than seeking to develop project specific data-
collection systems. In preparing proposals for monitoring and evaluation,
the Government should specifically address the issue of coordination
between activities of the Central Statistical Office, which should have
principal responsibility for production data, and the Economics Section
of the Ministry of Agriculture; sufficient resources to accomplish this
task should be available to the Statistical Office.
3.        The Ministry of Agriculture's monitoring and evaluation acti-
vities under the proposed Project would largely consist of assessment of the
Project's impact on farmers, and then the effectiveness of development and
extension programs. It would be directed by an agricultural economist who is
familiar with sociological issues, and would be focused on work by two survey
teams. Information would be gathered on agricultural practices and on
farmer attitudes affecting change or lack thereof. The surveys would be
specifically designed to furnish practical guidance to Project managers
on effectiveness of extension program and of extension staff.



ANNEX 13
Page 2
4.        In assessing Project benefits, Project management would be
able to use as a broad set of targets the production projections for
each RDA prepared during Project preparation (Annex 10). These indicate
expected areas under crops, yields, numbers of farmers growing cash crops,
and a rubric of progression in yields, all by year of development.
September 22, 1976



ANNEX 13
Appendix I
Page 1
SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Management Staff
Proposed Terms of Reference
Chief Project Coordinator
1.        Experience would be the most important criteria for the selection
of the Chief Coordinator, although a formal qualification in agriculture
would be important. It is expected that the post would be filled by an ex-
patriate for five years; the position is likely to be funded by ODM.
2.        At least 10 years experience in successful agricultural develop-
ment work in the field, including development orientated projects, prefer-
ably in Africa, would be required. A high degree of administrative ability
and experience in dealing with senior Government officials, especially in
committee work, and previous experience in extension in small farmer com-
munities, would all be necessary.
3.        The responsibilities of the Chief Coordinator would be the overall
management of Ministry of Agriculture components, and the coordination of
all Project activities with the activities of other Government agencies and
local authorities. His duties would include:
(a) Control of all program and Project funds, with
the assistance of the Financial Controller;
(b) Establishment of a cost analysis and record-
keeping system which would enable Project
progress and achievement to be monitored
and a meaningful evaluation of the Project
impact to be carried out;
(c) Responsibility for Project personnel mat-
ters, with the assistance of the Deputy
Coordinator;
(d) Coordination of all Project components;
(e) Reporting Project progress to Government
through written reports and through Proj-
ect committees; and



ANNEX 13
Appendix I
Page 2
(f) Training Project staff and ensuring that a
proper management training program is imple-
mented.
Financial Controller
4.        The Financial Controller should be a professionally qualified
accountant, with additional training in business management. He should
have had at least ten years experience in a senior position in a large
public corporation or commercial company. If possible he would have knowl-
edge of Government procedures on procurement and stores. Previous employ-
ment involving work with senior Government officials would be an advantage.
5.        His principal responsibility would be the control of all Project
financial matters on behalf of the Chief Coordinator. His duties would
include:
(a) Preparation of annual work programs, budgets,
Cash flows, and estimates of expenditure for
the Project and ODM-financed program;
(b) Ensuring correct and timely application for
withdrawJ'- from the loan account;
(c) Supervision of procurement of all Project
goods and services, and responsibility for
all stores and requisites, with the assis-
tance of RDA Project Managers;
(d) Internal auditing of Project accounts.
Deputy Coordinator
6.        The Deputy Coordinator would be a senior civil servant or have
had similar experience in private industry. He should be a Swazi national.
In previous employment he should have undertaken senior administrative re-
sponsibility and have obtained a thorough understanding of Government pro-
cedures.  His duties would include:
(a) Support of the Chief Coordinator on all
matters, including deputizing for him in
his absence;
(b) Responsibility for all Project personnel
matters;
(c) Non-technical aspects of the Project, particularly relations
with the CRDB;
(d) General Project matters.



ANNEX 13
Appendix 1
Page 3
Senior Rural Development Area's Manager
7.        The RDA manager would have direct responsibility for management
of RDA-level developments. RDA Project managers would report directly to
him and he would coordinate operations on a day to day basis. The manager
should have qualifications in agriculture, and at least two years experience
in a management position, preferably in a development Project in Africa.
Project Managers
8.        In each intensive RDA, a "Project Manager" would be assigned to
live in the area, and would manage and coordinate development programs
within the RDA. Managers could be either Swazis or technical assistants.
Qualifications should include training in agriculture and some management
experience.
Project Economist
9.        The Project Economist would be responsible for assessing the impact
of the project on the farmers in the Project area. He should have a Master's
Degree in farm management or agricultural economics and have had experience
in this field in a developing country. He should have knowledge of statis-
tical analysis and sampling procedures and it would be preferable if he were
familiar with systems of computerized data processing and analysis. He
would be responsible to the Senior Agricultural Economist in the Economics
and Farm Management Section of the Ministry of Agriculture. His duties
would include:
(a) directing the activities of two farm survey units within
the Project area;
(b) in conjunction with the Project coordinator, selecting
the areas to be sampled and the type and detail of data
to be gathered;
(c) selecting farmers for sampling by each unit;
(d) organizing the collection, processing and analysis of
the survey data;
(e) preparing reports and recommendations based on the results
of the above processing and analysis, and
(f) evaluating the impact of the Project by comparison of
observed data with the projected production costs and
benefits for specific management areas.
January 13, 1977

SWAZILAND
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Government Finance
The Auditor General
1.        Section 133 of the 1968 Constitution requires the Auditor General
to audit the Public Accounts of Swaziland and all officers, courts and author-
ities of the Government of Swaziland. The Auditor General submits his audit
reports to the Minister of Finance and Economic Planning, who presents them
to the Council of Ministers.
2.        The Auditor General is responsible to Parliament, and his activ-
ities are supervised by the Public Accounts Committee; the twelve members of
the Public Accounts Committee are members of Parliament and Senators. The
Auditor General has a staff of twenty-one: a Deputy Chief, three principal
auditors and junior auditors. The junior auditors are trained on the job.
Nineteen staff are Swazis and two are expatriates. The Ministry of Finance
approved twenty-eight positions on the current budget, but seven are unfilled
due to the lack of adequately trained staff.
3.        Each District is audited four times a year.  A survey of the North-
ern RDA was recently conducted and the auditors reviewed the accounts of all
Government entities involved, obtained the working papers from the project
and verified the physical existence of the assets as per specifications pro-
vided. The Auditor General has only had a marginal involvement in self-help
community projects, but he indicated that closer supervision would be war-
ranted.
Customs Union Revenues
4.        Swaziland shares in the proceeds of the Customs Union with Botswana,
Lesotho and South Africa. The revenue sharing arrangement covers all sales
taxes, selective excises, and taxes on imports into the common customs area.
Customs union receipts ranged from 57% to 28% of total Government's current
revenues in the last five fiscal years and were the principal single item of
current revenue in the four fiscal years ended 1975. The sugar levy and
income taxes were higher than customs union receipts in fiscal year 1976.
The arrangements for sharing the proceeds of the customs union with Botswana,
Lesotho, and South Africa are being renegotiated in 1976. Currently South
Africa sets the tax rates. Revenues from these taxes are pooled. Swaziland's



ANNEX 16
Page 2
share of the pool is determined roughly in the same proportion as
Swaziland's imports to the imports into the Customs Union area. The
share so determined is then increased by 42% to compensate Swaziland
for its lack of control over the tax rates and the development gap
with South Africa. The excise and sales tax component of the Customs
Union payments is negligible. In the past four years, Customs Union
payments to Swaziland have averaged about 22% of Swaziland imports,
but that percentage is decreasing.
September 20, 1976

Economic Analysis
Project Benefits
1.        Incremental crop production in Project RDA's was estimated follow-
ing models of parameters for inputs and outputs at different levels of devel-
opment, applied to estimated area under crops in each RDA (Annex 10). For
the economic analysis, a lag of one year was assumed to reflect delayed
phasing of investments; partial benefits were thus assumed for year 1.
2.        Incremental cattle sales were estimated on the basis of herd pro-
jections for the two intensive RDA's, and would result principally from in-
creased sales, some increases in herd productivity, and some improvement in
animal quality. Some incremental cattle sales should also occur in less
intensive RDA's, where emphasis at the initial stage would be on assistance
for marketing and preliminary animal production extension work.
3.        Some additional incremental production should result from small
irrigation schemes (principally rice and vegetables), fruit trees, fish (from
fish ponds), and milk (resulting from livestock extension work and improved
herd management).
4.        Benefits which cannot be quantified would result from soil conserva-
tion measures, improvement of social infrastructure, and support to central
management institutions for national rural development programs and future
project preparation.
Economic Rate of Return
5.        The economic analysis is based on the following assumptions:
(a) Project life is 20 years;
(b) All directly productive Project operating
and investment costs were included in the
rate of return calculation. The following
costs and benefits associated with them
(para 4) were excluded from the analysis:
(i) Human water supplies and clinics;



ANNEX 18
Page 2
(ii) A share of central management services
attributable to on-going rural develop-
ment programs in the eight RDA's not in-
cluded in this Project;
(iii) Part of the cost of training, studies,
and monitoring and evaluation which
relate to other on-going programs or
to preparation of future projects.
(c) Costs of production inputs and farm labor
were included in the analysis (para 6);
(d) Foreign expenditure costs and benefits were
valued at the prevailing exchange rate, which
was assumed to reflect accurately the value of
foreign exchange;
(e) Prices for incremental production were valued
at market prices as of 1975/76, which were
increased by 15% to reflect expected price
increases to February, 1977; it was assumed
that these prices were roughly equivalent to
farm-gate prices. Prices for major interna-
tionally traded commodities were adjusted to
reflect expected shifts in world commodity
prices; adjustments were calculated for maize,
cotton, tobacco, and beef;
(f) Labor costs for development works (civil works)
were valued at E 0.50, which was assumed to be
the approximate average level of uncontrolled
agricultural labor rates, rather than the
official minimum average rate (E 1.00); the
actual cost of agricultural labor was assumed
to reflect more closely than official wage
rates the overall economic cost of labor in
Swaziland;
(g) Taxes and duties were excluded from the
analysis; these were assumed to be the
actual revenue accruing to Swaziland from
Customs Union payments as a direct result
of Project-related imports, adjusted to
reflect both loss of revenue from substi-
tution of maize production for imports,
and that part of Customs Union revenues
not directly associated with taxes and
duties.



ANNEX 18
Page 3
(h) In order to reflect the importance of other
on-going and proposed rural development in-
vestments which would directly affect Project
RDA's and contribute to achievement of expected
benefits, a value was assigned to these non-
Project investments for the analysis; given
uncertainties about the level of these pro-
grams, two alternative figures were included,
and a rate of return was calculated for both
levels.
6.        The value of farm labor was calculated as follows:  it was
assumed that about 600,000 man-days of incremental labor would be required
by year 6, and that requirements would increase steadily up to that point
and to year 10. The value of agricultural labor reportedly ranges between
E 0.20 and E 0.80 a day, depending on season and geographic region. For
purposes of this analysis, it was assumed that incremental labor would be
valued at E 0.80 per day for 25% of the time, reflecting peak season re-
quirements, at E 0.20 25% of the time, to reflect off-season requirements,
and between those levels for the remaining 50% of the time. It appears
from available studies of Swazi Nation Land agriculture that existing labor
is significantly under-utilized, even at peak seasons of planting and har-
vesting.
7.        Based on these assumptions, the rate of return was estimated to be
between 20% and 25%; calculations are shown on Table 1. The sensitivity of
rates of return was tested under several unfavorable assumptions (including
delays in production increases, increases in costs, and decreases in the
value of benefits), and under all these assumptions the rate of return
would be satisfactory.
8.        The rates of return presented should be viewed as largely indi-
cative, offering an order of magnitude of expected benefits from the Proj-
ect. The uncertainties involved in the analysis are substantial for the
following reasons: (a) data on actual production in Project RDA's is
poor and field experience is limited, thus making projections of future
production and benefits uncertain; (b) many development schemes and pro-
grams outside the scope of the proposed Project will directly affect
Project RDA's and contribute to increasing production and incomes. Costs
of all programs are not available and, given their number and complexity,
it could be misleading to attribute benefits in Project RDA's to any single
program; (c) prediction of the development situation "with" and "without"
the Project is incalculable given the range of on-going and proposed pro-
grams that would affect Project RDA's; and (d) low initial capital costs
and high operating costs of Project investments, associated with somewhat
higher Project benefits tend to indicate a high rate of return, which may
overstate benefits from the Project. For these reasons, the mission
recommends that the quantitative rate of return be considered with other



ANNEX 18
Page 4
non-quantifiable benefits of the proposed Project, including principally
institutional development, income distribution effects, and the role of
the Project as part of a much larger, broad-based, and imaginative national
rural development program for Swaziland.
November 22, 1976
